The Conditional Proof that Tasawwur and Tasdiq Divide into Necessary and Acquired
Al-Dasuqi presents a conditional proof that tasawwur and tasdiq must be divided into necessary and acquired.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct al-Dasuqi's modus tollens argument for the division of conception and assent into intuitive and acquired knowledge.

Core Idea
The division of knowledge into necessary and acquired is proven by a conditional: if not divided, then all knowledge would be the same type—but it's not.

This passage from al-Dasuqi's commentary on the Tadhhib uses a conditional proof to establish a foundational epistemological distinction. You will see how a simple logical move (modus tollens) grounds the entire science of logic by showing that tasawwur and tasdiq each come in two kinds: intuitive and acquired.

Guided Path
1
Identify the antecedent and consequent
In a conditional proof, the antecedent is the 'if' clause. Here it is 'if they were not divided into necessary and acquired' (meaning if every instance were exclusively either only necessary or only acquired). The consequent is 'then all would be either intuitive or acquired' (meaning all are of one type, exclusively). Note that 'necessary' here means 'intuitive' (badīhī).
2
Recognize the logical form: modus tollens
The argument denies the consequent (shows it's false) and thereby denies the antecedent. The consequent is false because we know there exist both intuitive and acquired instances of tasawwur and tasdiq. So the antecedent (non-division) is false.
3
Apply the conclusion
If the antecedent is false, its negation is true: that is, they are divided into necessary and acquired. That is the required conclusion.
4
Trace al-Dasuqi's commentary
Notice how al-Dasuqi expands each phrase: he clarifies that 'necessary' means 'intuitive', and that 'all' refers to all individuals of conception and assent. He also explains the logical connection: falsity of the necessary condition entails falsity of the condition's antecedent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإِنَّما كانَ تقسيمُ التَّصورِ والتَّصديق إلى : الضَّروريّ والكسبي ضروريا ; لأنَّهما لو لم ينقسما إليهما ; لكان الجميعُ : إِمَّا بديهيا ، أو كسبيا والتّالي باطل بقسميه، فكذا المقدَّم. الدسوقي (قَوْلُهُ : ضَرُوْرِيَّا) أي: بديهاً . (قَوْلُهُ : لَوْ لَمْ ... إلخ) مُقدَّم ; لَكَانَ الجميعُ ... إلخ تالي، وقوله: (لَكَانَ الجميع؛ أي: جميع أفرادِ التَّصور، وجميع أفراد التصديق، وقوله: (إما بديهيا)؛ أي : فقط ، (وإمَّا كسبيا ) فقط . (قَوْلُهُ : وَالتَّالِي) أي : وهو كونُ الجميع، إما بديهي أو كسبي. (قَوْلُهُ : فَكَذَا الْمُقَدَّمُ) أي : فالمقدَّمُ وهو عدم انقسام كُلِّ مِنَ التَّصورِ والتَّصديقِ إلى الضَّروريّ والكسبي مثل التّالي في البطلان ; لأنَّ بطَلانَ اللازم يستلزم بطلانَ الملزوم؛ أي: وإذا بطل المقدَّمُ ثبت نقيضُه، وهو انقسامُ كُلِّ من التَّصورِ والتَّصديقِ إلى الضّروريّ والكسبي، وهو المطلوب.
And the division of conception and assent into the necessary and the acquired was necessary because if they were not divided into these two, then all would be either intuitive (badīhī) or acquired (kasbī), and the consequent is false in both its parts, so likewise the antecedent. Al-Dasūqī: His statement: 'necessary' means intuitive. His statement: 'if they were not... etc.' is the antecedent, 'then all would be... etc.' is the consequent, and his statement: 'then all' means all individuals of conception and all individuals of assent, and his statement: 'either intuitive' meaning only, 'or acquired' meaning only. His statement: 'and the consequent' means that which is that all are either intuitive or acquired. His statement: 'so likewise the antecedent' means the antecedent, which is the non-division of each of conception and assent into the necessary and the acquired, is like the consequent in falsehood, because the falsity of the necessary condition entails the falsity of that which necessitates it; meaning that when the antecedent is false, its contradictory is established, which is the division of each of conception and assent into the necessary and the acquired, and that is the required.
Source pages p. 142

Commentary
This passage presents a logical argument for why tasawwur and tasdiq must be divided into necessary (ḍarūrī) and acquired (kasbī). Al-Dasūqī explains the matn's condensed proof. The argument is a conditional proof: If they were not divided (i.e., if every instance of conception and assent were either necessary or acquired — but not both), then all would be either only intuitive or only acquired. This consequent is false because we know there are both intuitive and acquired instances in each category. Since the consequent is false, the antecedent (non-division) is also false. Therefore, the negation of the antecedent — that they are in fact divided — is true. The commentary clarifies the terms: 'necessary' here means intuitive (badīhī), and the division is that each type of knowledge can be either attained without effort (intuitive) or through reflection (acquired). This establishes the epistemological foundation for all subsequent discussions.

Key Terms
ضروري
كسبي
بديهي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In al-Dasūqī's argument for the division of tasawwur and tasdiq, what is the role of the statement 'the consequent is false in both its parts'?
A) It proves that the antecedent is true
B) It shows that all knowledge is acquired
C) It demonstrates that the division must be false
D) It provides the basis for rejecting the antecedent (i.e., proving the division)

Key Takeaway
Al-Dasūqī uses modus tollens to establish that tasawwur and tasdiq each have both intuitive and acquired types, which is the epistemological foundation of logic.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a similar conditional proof for the division of human actions into voluntary and involuntary. Use Islamic examples: e.g., acts of worship vs. reflex actions.
?
Identify the antecedent and consequent in the following statement: 'If the Quran were not from Allah, there would be many contradictions in it. But there are no contradictions. Therefore, the Quran is from Allah.' Which logical form is this? (modus tollens)

The Definition of a Law (Qanun) in Logic
Commentator defines a 'law' as a universal affirmative rule and excludes negatives.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the definition of a law (qanun) as a universal affirmative rule and distinguish it from universal negatives and conditionals based on the commentary.

Core Idea
A law in logic is a universal affirmative rule for deriving particular judgments.

In this passage, al-Dasuqi and al-Attar define a 'law' (qanun) as a universal affirmative proposition that serves as a rule for deriving particular judgments through conversion. They explicitly exclude universal negatives and conditionals from this definition, although a counter-argument by Abd al-Hakim is noted. Understanding this definition is crucial for grasping how logical rules are applied to specific cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core definition
Locate the phrase where the law is defined: 'أَنَّ الْمُوْجِبَةَ الْكُلِّيَّةَ تَنْعَكِسُ مُوْجِبَةٌ جُزْئِيَّةٌ هذا هو القانون'. This tells you that a law is a universal affirmative that converts to a particular affirmative.
2
Note the scope of the definition
The commentary states that universal negatives and conditionals are excluded. Check the text: 'فَخَرَجَتِ السَّالبةُ الكُلِّيَّةُ مِن تعريف القانونِ كَالشَّرِطِيَّةِ'. This means the law is specifically for affirmative universals.
3
Consider the objection
Abd al-Hakim argued that negatives are also laws because branches can be derived from them just as from affirmatives. The text says: 'استنباط الفروع كما يكونُ مِنَ الموجبات؛ يكونُ مِنَ السَّوالِبِ'. This challenges the mainstream view.
4
Determine the accepted verdict
Despite the objection, the author aligns with the majority that the parts of the art are universal affirmative propositions. So the definition stands as given.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مُقَدِّمَةُ الماتن كما إذا عُلِمَ أنَّ الموجِبَةَ الكلية تنعكس موجِبَةً جزئيةً؛ الدسوقي (قَوْلُهُ : كَمَا إِذَا عُلِمَ أي : لأنَّه إذا عُلِمَ ...إلخ، فالكافُ للتّعليلِ، وَ(مَا ) زائدة، وهو علَّةٌ لِقَولِهِ : (منطبقة) (قَوْلُهُ : أَنَّ الْمُوْجِبَةَ الْكُلِّيَّةَ تَنْعَكِسُ مُوْجِبَةٌ جُزْئِيَّةٌ) هذا هو القانون؛ أي: القاعدة الكُلّيّة. العطار موضوع طرفيها ، فَخَرَجَتِ السَّالبةُ الكُلِّيَّةُ مِن تعريف القانونِ كالشَّرِطَيَّةِ؛ فَوَافَقَ مَا تَقَرَّرَ عندَهُم أنَّ أجزاء الفنّ قضايا حمليّات موجبات كُلِّيَّات، وإنْ قال عبد الحكيم : إِنَّ السالبة مِنَ القوانين، وعَلَّلَ ذلكَ بأنَّ استنباط الفروع كما يكونُ مِنَ الموجبات؛ يكونُ مِنَ السَّوالِبِ، ثمَّ إِنْ فُسِّر الانطباق بالحمل ؛ فالمراد بالجزئيات : أفراد ذلكَ المفهوم الكُلِّيّ الَّذي هو موضوعُ القانون، مثلاً إذا قِيلَ : كُلُّ فاعل مرفوعٌ ؛ مَفهوم فاعل وهو مَن قامَ بِهِ الحدثُ أمرُ كُلّيّ، وله أفراد واقعةٌ في التّراكيب؛ كَقَامَ زِيدٌ وسَافَرَ عمرو ... إلخ، ولا خفاء في صحة حملِ ذلك المفهوم الكُلِّي على تلك الأفرادِ، فَإِنَّكَ تقولُ : زيدٌ مَنْ قامَ ؛ زيدٌ فاعل، وعمرُو مَن سافر؛ عمرو فاعل ... إلخ، وإِنْ فُسِّرَ بالاشتمال ؛ فالمراد بالجزئيات فروع تلك القاعدة تشبيهاً لها بالجزئيات في الاندراج ؛ على خلافِ ما هو شائع مِن إطلاق الجزئيَّاتِ على أفرادِ الكُلّيّ، والمراد باشتمال القاعدة على تلك الفروع ؛ وجودُهَا فيها بالقوَّةِ لا بالفعل، فإنَّ الحاصل بالفعل ليسَ إلا حكم واحدٌ، وهو الحاصِلُ في حمل محمول القضيَّةِ على موضوعِهَا، ثمَّ . الشَّارِحَ حذف قيدَ التَّعرُّفِ لِظهورِهِ ولِدَلالةِ قولِهِ : كما إذا ... إلخ، قال بعضُ الفُضَلاءِ وفي صيغةِ التَّفَقُلِ ؛ أي: قولُهُم يتعرَّفُ ; إشارة إلى أنَّ تلك المعرفة بالكلفة والمشقة، فخرجَ مِنَ التَّعريف القضيَّةُ الكُلِّيَّةُ الَّتي تكون فروعُهَا بديهيَّةً غيرَ مُحتاجةٍ إلى التَّخريج؛ كَقولنا : الشَّكلُ الأَوَّلُ مُنتج، فيكونُ ذِكْرُهَا في الفنّ بِطريق المبدئية لمسائِلَ أُخرَى. (قَوْلُهُ : كَمَا إِذَا عُلِمَ ...إلخ) لا ارتباط بما قَبلَهُ إِلَّا بِتقدير : ليتعرَّفَ أحكامها منها الذي هو بقيَّةُ تَعريف القانون، والكافُ لمجرَّدِ قرانِ الفعلين في الوجودِ كما في الرّضى، ونظيره قولُهم : فإنَّ الفكرَ كَمَا يجري في التصوراتِ؛ يجري في التصديقات، والفِعلانِ هُما علم المذكور مرَّتين، قالَ السَّيِّدُ : استخراج تلك الفروع مِنَ القاعدة يُسمَّى تفريعاً، وذلك بأنْ يُحمل موضوعُها ، أعني الفاعل؛ على زيدٍ مثلاً ; فيحصل قضيَّةٌ وتُجعَلُ صُغرى، وتلك القضيَّةُ الكُلِّيَّةُ كُبرى هكذا : زيدٌ فاعل، وكلُّ فاعل مرفوع، فينتج : زيد مرفوع، فقد خرج بهذا العمل هذا الفرعُ مِنَ القوَّةِ إلى الفعل، وقس على ذلكَ ا.هـ. (قَوْلُهُ: عُلِمَ أَنَّ كُلَّ إِنْسَانٍ ... إِلَخ) أي: علم ذلكَ بالقوَّةِ القريبة مِنَ الفعل، لا أنَّ هذا العِلْمَ حاصِل لهُ معَ العِلْمِ الأَوَّلِ، بَلِ المعنى تمكَّن من أن يعلم، وذلك كأن يقول : كُلُّ إنسانٍ حيوانٌ ; موجبةً كُلِّيَّةٌ ، وكُلُّ مُوجبةٍ كُلّيَّةٍ تَنعكسُ موجبةٌ جُزئيَّةٌ ، فكلُّ إنسان حيوان; تنعكس مُوجِبةً جزئيَّةٌ . (۱) (قَوْلُهُ: وتجعل المحمول ... إلخ) كذا بالنسخة التي بأيدينا، ولعل فيها سقطاً الجزئي) وبذلك يصح كلامه إذ المقدّمة من قبيل التصديق دون التصور . وهو (مع ذلك)
Introduction of the author: 'As when it is known that the universal affirmative converts to a particular affirmative.' al-Dasuqi: (His statement: 'As when it is known' means: because when it is known... etc., so the kaf is for causation, and (ma) is extra, and it is the cause for his statement: 'applicable'. (His statement: 'that the universal affirmative converts to a particular affirmative') this is the law; i.e., the universal rule. al-Attar: 'The subject of its two extremes, so the universal negative exited from the definition of law like the conditional; so it agrees with what is established with them that the parts of the art are categorical universal affirmative propositions, even if Abd al-Hakim said: the negative is among the laws, and he explained that by saying that deriving branches, just as it is from affirmatives, it is from negatives. Then, if 'applicability' is interpreted as predication, then what is meant by particulars are the individuals of that universal concept which is the subject of the law; for example when it is said: 'Every doer is raised'; the concept of doer, which is one who performs an action, is a universal thing, and it has individuals occurring in constructions, like 'Zaid stood' and 'Amr traveled'... etc., and there is no concealment in the correctness of predicating that universal concept to those individuals, for you say: 'Zaid is the one who stood; Zaid is a doer, and Amr is the one who traveled; Amr is a doer... etc.' And if it is interpreted as containment, then what is meant by particulars are the branches of that rule, by analogy to particulars in subsumption, contrary to what is common of applying the term 'particulars' to the individuals of the universal, and what is meant by the rule's containment of those branches is their existence in it in potentiality not actuality, for what is actual is only one judgment, namely that obtained in predicating the predicate of the proposition to its subject. Then the commentator omitted the condition of 'knowing' due to its obviousness and because his statement 'as when... etc.' indicates it. Some of the virtuous said: and in the form 'yata'arrafu' (he knows with difficulty); i.e., their statement 'yata'arrafu' indicates that that knowledge is with effort and difficulty, so there exited from the definition the universal proposition whose branches are self-evident, not needing derivation, like our statement: 'The first figure is productive', so its mention in the art is by way of being a starting point for other issues. (His statement: 'As when it is known... etc.') has no connection with what precedes except by the estimation: 'to know its judgments from it' which is the remainder of the definition of law, and the kaf is merely for the conjunction of two actions in existence as in al-Rida, and its analogue is their statement: 'For thought, just as it runs in concepts, it runs in assents', and the two actions are the knowledge mentioned twice. al-Sayyid said: 'Extracting those branches from the rule is called furu' (derivation), and that is by predicating its subject, namely the doer, onto Zaid for example, so a proposition is obtained and made a minor premise, and that universal proposition is a major premise thus: 'Zaid is a doer, and every doer is raised', so it yields: 'Zaid is raised', so by this operation this branch goes from potentiality to actuality, and compare that... etc.' (His statement: 'It is known that every human is an animal... etc.') means: that is known with potentiality close to actuality, not that this knowledge is obtained for him with the first knowledge, rather the meaning is he is able to know it, and that is as if he says: 'Every human is an animal' is a universal affirmative, and 'Every universal affirmative converts to a particular affirmative', so 'Every human is an animal' converts to a particular affirmative. (1) (His statement: 'And you make the predicate... etc.') This is as in the copy we have, and there is apparently a missing 'the particular' in it, and by that his speech becomes correct since the premise is of the type of assent not conception. And (with that) it is (still).
Source pages p. 165

Commentary
This passage is a commentary from al-Dasuqi and al-Attar on the definition of a 'law' (qanun) in logic. The author first states the conversion rule: 'As when it is known that the universal affirmative converts to a particular affirmative.' Al-Dasuqi explains that this is the definition of a law, i.e., a universal rule. Al-Attar then clarifies that the law applies only to categorical universal affirmative propositions; universal negatives and conditionals are excluded, although Abd al-Hakim argued that negatives are also laws because branches can be derived from them. The passage then discusses two interpretations of 'applicability' (intibaq): as predication (haml) or as containment (ishtimal). Under predication, the particulars are the individuals of the universal concept (e.g., every doer is raised; Zaid is a doer, so Zaid is raised). Under containment, the particulars are the branches of the rule existing in potentiality. The commentator then notes that the condition of 'knowing' (ta'arruf) was omitted because it is obvious. The passage emphasizes that deriving a specific judgment from a universal rule requires a syllogism in the first figure: taking a particular instance as minor premise and the universal rule as major premise to produce a conclusion. For example, from 'Every human is an animal' and the conversion rule 'Every universal affirmative converts to a particular affirmative', we can deduce 'Some animal is human'. The knowledge of the specific conversion is not immediate but potential, requiring the mental syllogism to actualize it.

Key Terms
قانون
انعكاس
انطباق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the commentary, which type of proposition is explicitly excluded from the definition of a law (qanun)?
A) Universal affirmative propositions
B) Universal negative propositions
C) Particular affirmative propositions
D) Singular propositions

Key Takeaway
A law in logic is a universal affirmative rule from which particular judgments are derived via conversion, and it excludes universal negatives and conditionals.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Using the definition from the commentary, determine whether the following proposition qualifies as a 'law': 'Every universal negative (E) converts to a particular negative (O)'. Explain your answer based on the text.
?

Applying the Law via Conversion and the First Figure Syllogism
Commentator demonstrates application of law via conversion in a first-figure syllogism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct a first-figure categorical syllogism that derives a particular judgment from a universal law using conversion.

Core Idea
Deriving a branch from a law requires a mental syllogism in the first figure.

The commentary explains that the law (e.g., 'Every universal affirmative converts to a particular affirmative') is applied by taking a concrete universal affirmative proposition (like 'Every human is an animal') and converting it to a particular affirmative ('Some animal is human'). This derivation is not immediate but involves a syllogism: the concrete proposition as minor premise, the law as major premise, leading to the converted conclusion. The passage also discusses two interpretations of 'applicability' (intibaq): predication and containment.

Guided Path
1
Understand the example given
The text says: 'كُلُّ إِنْسَانٍ حَيَوَانٌ' (Every human is an animal) is a universal affirmative. Then the law says: every universal affirmative converts to a particular affirmative. So we can derive: 'Some animal is human'.
2
Recognize the syllogistic structure
Al-Sayyid explains the derivation: we take a particular instance (Zaid is a doer) as minor premise with the universal law (Every doer is raised) as major premise to get a particular conclusion (Zaid is raised). This is how a branch is extracted from a law.
The syllogism is: Zaid is a doer (minor), every doer is raised (major), therefore Zaid is raised (conclusion).
3
Identify the figure and mood
This is a first-figure syllogism. The middle term 'doer' is subject in the major premise and predicate in the minor premise. The mood is Barbara (AAA-1) since all premises and conclusion are universal affirmative. This figure is always valid.
4
Distinguish potential vs actual knowledge
The text says the knowledge of the converted proposition is potential ('بالقوة القريبة من الفعل') before the syllogism; the syllogism actualizes it. This highlights that reasoning is required to apply the law.

مُقَدِّمَةُ الماتن كما إذا عُلِمَ أنَّ الموجِبَةَ الكلية تنعكس موجِبَةً جزئيةً؛ الدسوقي (قَوْلُهُ : كَمَا إِذَا عُلِمَ أي : لأنَّه إذا عُلِمَ ...إلخ، فالكافُ للتّعليلِ، وَ(مَا ) زائدة، وهو علَّةٌ لِقَولِهِ : (منطبقة) (قَوْلُهُ : أَنَّ الْمُوْجِبَةَ الْكُلِّيَّةَ تَنْعَكِسُ مُوْجِبَةٌ جُزْئِيَّةٌ) هذا هو القانون؛ أي: القاعدة الكُلّيّة. العطار موضوع طرفيها ، فَخَرَجَتِ السَّالبةُ الكُلِّيَّةُ مِن تعريف القانونِ كالشَّرِطَيَّةِ؛ فَوَافَقَ مَا تَقَرَّرَ عندَهُم أنَّ أجزاء الفنّ قضايا حمليّات موجبات كُلِّيَّات، وإنْ قال عبد الحكيم : إِنَّ السالبة مِنَ القوانين، وعَلَّلَ ذلكَ بأنَّ استنباط الفروع كما يكونُ مِنَ الموجبات؛ يكونُ مِنَ السَّوالِبِ، ثمَّ إِنْ فُسِّر الانطباق بالحمل ؛ فالمراد بالجزئيات : أفراد ذلكَ المفهوم الكُلِّيّ الَّذي هو موضوعُ القانون، مثلاً إذا قِيلَ : كُلُّ فاعل مرفوعٌ ؛ مَفهوم فاعل وهو مَن قامَ بِهِ الحدثُ أمرُ كُلّيّ، وله أفراد واقعةٌ في التّراكيب؛ كَقَامَ زِيدٌ وسَافَرَ عمرو ... إلخ، ولا خفاء في صحة حملِ ذلك المفهوم الكُلِّي على تلك الأفرادِ، فَإِنَّكَ تقولُ : زيدٌ مَنْ قامَ ؛ زيدٌ فاعل، وعمرُو مَن سافر؛ عمرو فاعل ... إلخ، وإِنْ فُسِّرَ بالاشتمال ؛ فالمراد بالجزئيات فروع تلك القاعدة تشبيهاً لها بالجزئيات في الاندراج ؛ على خلافِ ما هو شائع مِن إطلاق الجزئيَّاتِ على أفرادِ الكُلّيّ، والمراد باشتمال القاعدة على تلك الفروع ؛ وجودُهَا فيها بالقوَّةِ لا بالفعل، فإنَّ الحاصل بالفعل ليسَ إلا حكم واحدٌ، وهو الحاصِلُ في حمل محمول القضيَّةِ على موضوعِهَا، ثمَّ . الشَّارِحَ حذف قيدَ التَّعرُّفِ لِظهورِهِ ولِدَلالةِ قولِهِ : كما إذا ... إلخ، قال بعضُ الفُضَلاءِ وفي صيغةِ التَّفَقُلِ ؛ أي: قولُهُم يتعرَّفُ ; إشارة إلى أنَّ تلك المعرفة بالكلفة والمشقة، فخرجَ مِنَ التَّعريف القضيَّةُ الكُلِّيَّةُ الَّتي تكون فروعُهَا بديهيَّةً غيرَ مُحتاجةٍ إلى التَّخريج؛ كَقولنا : الشَّكلُ الأَوَّلُ مُنتج، فيكونُ ذِكْرُهَا في الفنّ بِطريق المبدئية لمسائِلَ أُخرَى. (قَوْلُهُ : كَمَا إِذَا عُلِمَ ...إلخ) لا ارتباط بما قَبلَهُ إِلَّا بِتقدير : ليتعرَّفَ أحكامها منها الذي هو بقيَّةُ تَعريف القانون، والكافُ لمجرَّدِ قرانِ الفعلين في الوجودِ كما في الرّضى، ونظيره قولُهم : فإنَّ الفكرَ كَمَا يجري في التصوراتِ؛ يجري في التصديقات، والفِعلانِ هُما علم المذكور مرَّتين، قالَ السَّيِّدُ : استخراج تلك الفروع مِنَ القاعدة يُسمَّى تفريعاً، وذلك بأنْ يُحمل موضوعُها ، أعني الفاعل؛ على زيدٍ مثلاً ; فيحصل قضيَّةٌ وتُجعَلُ صُغرى، وتلك القضيَّةُ الكُلِّيَّةُ كُبرى هكذا : زيدٌ فاعل، وكلُّ فاعل مرفوع، فينتج : زيد مرفوع، فقد خرج بهذا العمل هذا الفرعُ مِنَ القوَّةِ إلى الفعل، وقس على ذلكَ ا.هـ. (قَوْلُهُ: عُلِمَ أَنَّ كُلَّ إِنْسَانٍ ... إِلَخ) أي: علم ذلكَ بالقوَّةِ القريبة مِنَ الفعل، لا أنَّ هذا العِلْمَ حاصِل لهُ معَ العِلْمِ الأَوَّلِ، بَلِ المعنى تمكَّن من أن يعلم، وذلك كأن يقول : كُلُّ إنسانٍ حيوانٌ ; موجبةً كُلِّيَّةٌ ، وكُلُّ مُوجبةٍ كُلّيَّةٍ تَنعكسُ موجبةٌ جُزئيَّةٌ ، فكلُّ إنسان حيوان; تنعكس مُوجِبةً جزئيَّةٌ . (۱) (قَوْلُهُ: وتجعل المحمول ... إلخ) كذا بالنسخة التي بأيدينا، ولعل فيها سقطاً الجزئي) وبذلك يصح كلامه إذ المقدّمة من قبيل التصديق دون التصور . وهو (مع ذلك)
Introduction of the author: 'As when it is known that the universal affirmative converts to a particular affirmative.' al-Dasuqi: (His statement: 'As when it is known' means: because when it is known... etc., so the kaf is for causation, and (ma) is extra, and it is the cause for his statement: 'applicable'. (His statement: 'that the universal affirmative converts to a particular affirmative') this is the law; i.e., the universal rule. al-Attar: 'The subject of its two extremes, so the universal negative exited from the definition of law like the conditional; so it agrees with what is established with them that the parts of the art are categorical universal affirmative propositions, even if Abd al-Hakim said: the negative is among the laws, and he explained that by saying that deriving branches, just as it is from affirmatives, it is from negatives. Then, if 'applicability' is interpreted as predication, then what is meant by particulars are the individuals of that universal concept which is the subject of the law; for example when it is said: 'Every doer is raised'; the concept of doer, which is one who performs an action, is a universal thing, and it has individuals occurring in constructions, like 'Zaid stood' and 'Amr traveled'... etc., and there is no concealment in the correctness of predicating that universal concept to those individuals, for you say: 'Zaid is the one who stood; Zaid is a doer, and Amr is the one who traveled; Amr is a doer... etc.' And if it is interpreted as containment, then what is meant by particulars are the branches of that rule, by analogy to particulars in subsumption, contrary to what is common of applying the term 'particulars' to the individuals of the universal, and what is meant by the rule's containment of those branches is their existence in it in potentiality not actuality, for what is actual is only one judgment, namely that obtained in predicating the predicate of the proposition to its subject. Then the commentator omitted the condition of 'knowing' due to its obviousness and because his statement 'as when... etc.' indicates it. Some of the virtuous said: and in the form 'yata'arrafu' (he knows with difficulty); i.e., their statement 'yata'arrafu' indicates that that knowledge is with effort and difficulty, so there exited from the definition the universal proposition whose branches are self-evident, not needing derivation, like our statement: 'The first figure is productive', so its mention in the art is by way of being a starting point for other issues. (His statement: 'As when it is known... etc.') has no connection with what precedes except by the estimation: 'to know its judgments from it' which is the remainder of the definition of law, and the kaf is merely for the conjunction of two actions in existence as in al-Rida, and its analogue is their statement: 'For thought, just as it runs in concepts, it runs in assents', and the two actions are the knowledge mentioned twice. al-Sayyid said: 'Extracting those branches from the rule is called furu' (derivation), and that is by predicating its subject, namely the doer, onto Zaid for example, so a proposition is obtained and made a minor premise, and that universal proposition is a major premise thus: 'Zaid is a doer, and every doer is raised', so it yields: 'Zaid is raised', so by this operation this branch goes from potentiality to actuality, and compare that... etc.' (His statement: 'It is known that every human is an animal... etc.') means: that is known with potentiality close to actuality, not that this knowledge is obtained for him with the first knowledge, rather the meaning is he is able to know it, and that is as if he says: 'Every human is an animal' is a universal affirmative, and 'Every universal affirmative converts to a particular affirmative', so 'Every human is an animal' converts to a particular affirmative. (1) (His statement: 'And you make the predicate... etc.') This is as in the copy we have, and there is apparently a missing 'the particular' in it, and by that his speech becomes correct since the premise is of the type of assent not conception. And (with that) it is (still).
Source pages p. 165

Commentary
This passage is a commentary from al-Dasuqi and al-Attar on the definition of a 'law' (qanun) in logic. The author first states the conversion rule: 'As when it is known that the universal affirmative converts to a particular affirmative.' Al-Dasuqi explains that this is the definition of a law, i.e., a universal rule. Al-Attar then clarifies that the law applies only to categorical universal affirmative propositions; universal negatives and conditionals are excluded, although Abd al-Hakim argued that negatives are also laws because branches can be derived from them. The passage then discusses two interpretations of 'applicability' (intibaq): as predication (haml) or as containment (ishtimal). Under predication, the particulars are the individuals of the universal concept (e.g., every doer is raised; Zaid is a doer, so Zaid is raised). Under containment, the particulars are the branches of the rule existing in potentiality. The commentator then notes that the condition of 'knowing' (ta'arruf) was omitted because it is obvious. The passage emphasizes that deriving a specific judgment from a universal rule requires a syllogism in the first figure: taking a particular instance as minor premise and the universal rule as major premise to produce a conclusion. For example, from 'Every human is an animal' and the conversion rule 'Every universal affirmative converts to a particular affirmative', we can deduce 'Some animal is human'. The knowledge of the specific conversion is not immediate but potential, requiring the mental syllogism to actualize it.

Key Terms
انعكاس
شكل أول
انطباق

A) That the law of conversion is unnecessary
B) How a particular judgment is derived from a universal law
C) That Zaid is a prophet
D) That the first figure is invalid for universal premises

Key Takeaway
A law is applied by constructing a first-figure categorical syllogism where the universal rule is the major premise and a particular instance is the minor premise, yielding a specific conclusion.

Deriving Conversion Knowledge via Syllogism
Dusuqi explains how conversion of universal affirmative is known through a first-figure syllogism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct a first-figure categorical syllogism to derive the conversion of a universal affirmative proposition.

Core Idea
Conversion is known not directly from the rule but via a syllogism that applies the rule to a particular instance.

Dusuqi shows that knowing a logical conversion rule does not give immediate knowledge of its application to every instance. Instead, one must construct a syllogism using the rule as a major premise and the specific universal affirmative proposition as a minor premise. This lesson walks through that process with the example 'Every human is an animal' converting to 'Some animal is human'.

Guided Path
1
Identify the specific universal affirmative proposition
Take a concrete universal affirmative proposition, such as 'Every human is an animal'. This is the particular instance of the rule's subject.
2
State the conversion rule as a major premise
The rule is: 'Every universal affirmative converts to a particular affirmative.' Treat this as a universal major premise.
3
Use the specific proposition as the minor premise
The specific proposition 'Every human is an animal' is an instance of the rule's subject. It serves as the minor premise.
4
Derive the conclusion in the first figure
From the major and minor premises, the first-figure syllogism yields the converted proposition: 'Some animal is human'.
The middle term is the concept of being a universal affirmative.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الدسوقي (قَوْلُهُ : عُلِمَ أَنَّ كُلَّ إِنْسَانٍ حَيَوَانُ» ينعكس إلى «بعض الحيوان إنسان») أي : الذي هو جزئي من جزئيات موضوع القانون، وقوله: (ينعكس ... إلخ ) هكذا حكم ذلك الجزئي، وطريق العلم بذلك أَنَّكَ تأخذُ جزئيا من جزئيات موضوع القانون كالجزئي المذكور، وتحمل عليه موضوع القانون، وتجعلُ المحمول مُقدِّمةً صُغرى، وتجعلُ القانونَ مُقدِّمةٌ كُبرى، فيحصل قياس من الشَّكلِ الأَوَّلِ مُنتج لثبوتِهِ حكم موضوع القانون لذلك الجزئي، فيحصلُ العِلْمُ المذكورُ ، كأنْ يُقالَ : كلُّ إنسان حيوان، موجبة كلّيَّةٌ ، والموجبةُ الكُلّيّة تنعكس موجبة جزئيَّةٌ، وهي: بعضُ الحيوان إنسان. فقولُ الشَّارحِ : (عُلِمَ أَنَّ كُلَّ إنسان حيوان ... إلخ)؛ أي: بعد إقامة القياس المذكور؛ إذْ بمجرَّدِ العِلْم بالقاعدة المذكورة؛ لا يحصلُ العِلْم المذكور، وإنَّما يحصلُ التَّمكن منه، لكنْ لَمَّا كانَ هذا التَّمْكُنُ قويًّا؛ عبر عنه بالعِلْمِ؛ أي: وحيثُ كانَ العِلْم بالقاعدة يستلزمُ مَا ذكر ؛ كانَتِ القوانين منطبقةً على أحكام الجزئيات؛ إذْ لولا الانطباق المذكورُ ؛ ما حصل هذا العِلْمُ عندَ العِلْمِ بالقاعدة. العطار وَكَذا نظائره. الدسوقي (قَوْلُهُ : وَكَذَا نَظَائِرُهُ يحتملُ أنَّ المراد نظائر الجزئي المذكورِ مِن نحو : كُلُّ فرس حيوان، ويحتمل أنَّ المراد نظائرُ القاعدة المذكورة مِنْ أنَّ الموجبة الجزئية تنعكس كَنَفْسِهَا ، والسَّالبة الجزئية تنعكسُ كَنَفْسِهَا ، فإذا عُلِمَ أَنَّ السَّالبةَ الكُلِّيَّةَ تنعكس كَنَفْسِهَا ؛ عُلِمَ أن لا شيء مِنَ الإنسان بحجرٍ ؛ ينعكس إلى : لا شيء مِنَ الحجرِ بإنسان.
Dusuqi: (His statement: 'It is known that every human is an animal' converts to 'some animal is human') meaning: that which is a particular among the particulars of the subject of the rule, and his statement: (converts ... etc.) thus is the judgment of that particular. The way of knowing that is that you take a particular from the particulars of the subject of the rule, like the mentioned particular, and you attribute the subject of the rule to it, and you make the predicate a minor premise, and you make the rule a major premise, so there results a syllogism in the first figure productive of the establishment of the judgment of the subject of the rule for that particular, so the mentioned knowledge occurs. For example, it is said: Every human is an animal, a universal affirmative, and the universal affirmative converts to a particular affirmative, which is: Some animal is human. So the commentator's statement: ('It is known that every human is an animal ... etc.') means: after the establishment of the mentioned syllogism; for by mere knowledge of the mentioned rule, the mentioned knowledge does not occur, but rather the ability to attain it occurs. However, since this ability is strong, he expressed it as knowledge. That is: and since knowledge of the rule entails what was mentioned, the rules are applicable to the judgments of particulars; for if not for that applicability, this knowledge would not occur upon knowledge of the rule. al-'Attar: And likewise its analogues. Dusuqi: (His statement: 'And likewise its analogues' ) It is possible that what is meant is analogues of the mentioned particular, such as: Every horse is an animal, and it is possible that what is meant is analogues of the mentioned rule, such as: the particular affirmative converts to itself, and the particular negative converts to itself. So if it is known that the universal negative converts to itself, it is known that 'No human is a stone' converts to 'No stone is a human'.
Source pages p. 166

Commentary
This passage is an expansion on how conversion of propositions is known. Dusuqi begins by explaining the statement 'It is known that every human is an animal converts to some animal is human'. He clarifies that 'every human is an animal' is a particular instance of the subject of the logical rule of conversion. The way to know the conversion is to take a particular (like 'human'), attribute the subject of the rule (here 'human') to it, and then make the predicate (animal) the minor premise and the universal rule (the conversion law) the major premise. This yields a first-figure syllogism that proves the conversion for that particular. For example, 'Every human is an animal' is a universal affirmative; the rule says universal affirmatives convert to particular affirmatives, so we conclude 'Some animal is human'. Dusuqi then explains that the knowledge of conversion is not immediate from knowing the rule alone; it requires constructing such a syllogism. However, since the rule strongly entails the conclusion, the ability to know it is so strong that it is called knowledge. This shows that logical rules apply to particular judgments only through this mediation. al-'Attar then comments on the phrase 'And likewise its analogues', offering two interpretations: either analogues of the particular example (like 'every horse is an animal') or analogues of the rule itself (like conversion of particular affirmative and particular negative). The latter includes the conversion of universal negative: from 'No human is a stone' we get 'No stone is a human'.

Key Terms
انعكاس
قياس من الشكل الأول
موجبة كلية

C) By induction from multiple examples.

Key Takeaway
Knowledge of a particular conversion requires a syllogistic application of the general rule to the specific case.

Epistemological Nuance: Knowledge vs Ability in Rule Application
Dusuqi clarifies that knowing the rule gives only the ability to know particular conversions, not actual knowledge, but due to the strength of the ability, it is called knowledge metaphorically.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between having knowledge of a logical rule and having actual knowledge of its application, and explain why the ability is called knowledge metaphorically.

Core Idea
Knowing a rule gives ability, not actuality; strong ability is called knowledge.

After explaining the syllogistic method, Dusuqi addresses an epistemological point: mere knowledge of the conversion rule does not yield knowledge of a particular conversion. Rather, it gives the ability (quwwa) to know, which is so strong that it is expressed as knowledge. This lesson explores this subtle distinction and its implications for the applicability of rules to particulars.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of knowledge Dusuqi distinguishes
Dusuqi contrasts 'knowledge of the rule' (al-'ilm bi-l-qā'ida) and 'knowledge of the particular conversion' (al-'ilm al-madhkūr). He says the latter does not occur from the former alone.
2
Understand the role of the syllogism
The syllogism is the means that transforms the ability into actual knowledge. Without it, one only has the potential.
3
Analyze the metaphor of 'strong ability'
Dusuqi says that because the ability (al-tamakkun) from the rule is so strong, he calls it 'knowledge' figuratively. This shows the close connection between a rule and its instances.
This reflects the concept of al-quwwa al-qarība min al-fi‘l in Islamic logic.
4
Connect to the applicability of rules
Dusuqi concludes that if this strong entailment did not hold, rules would not be applicable to particulars. So the ability ensures that logical laws cover their instances.

الدسوقي (قَوْلُهُ : عُلِمَ أَنَّ كُلَّ إِنْسَانٍ حَيَوَانُ» ينعكس إلى «بعض الحيوان إنسان») أي : الذي هو جزئي من جزئيات موضوع القانون، وقوله: (ينعكس ... إلخ ) هكذا حكم ذلك الجزئي، وطريق العلم بذلك أَنَّكَ تأخذُ جزئيا من جزئيات موضوع القانون كالجزئي المذكور، وتحمل عليه موضوع القانون، وتجعلُ المحمول مُقدِّمةً صُغرى، وتجعلُ القانونَ مُقدِّمةٌ كُبرى، فيحصل قياس من الشَّكلِ الأَوَّلِ مُنتج لثبوتِهِ حكم موضوع القانون لذلك الجزئي، فيحصلُ العِلْمُ المذكورُ ، كأنْ يُقالَ : كلُّ إنسان حيوان، موجبة كلّيَّةٌ ، والموجبةُ الكُلّيّة تنعكس موجبة جزئيَّةٌ، وهي: بعضُ الحيوان إنسان. فقولُ الشَّارحِ : (عُلِمَ أَنَّ كُلَّ إنسان حيوان ... إلخ)؛ أي: بعد إقامة القياس المذكور؛ إذْ بمجرَّدِ العِلْم بالقاعدة المذكورة؛ لا يحصلُ العِلْم المذكور، وإنَّما يحصلُ التَّمكن منه، لكنْ لَمَّا كانَ هذا التَّمْكُنُ قويًّا؛ عبر عنه بالعِلْمِ؛ أي: وحيثُ كانَ العِلْم بالقاعدة يستلزمُ مَا ذكر ؛ كانَتِ القوانين منطبقةً على أحكام الجزئيات؛ إذْ لولا الانطباق المذكورُ ؛ ما حصل هذا العِلْمُ عندَ العِلْمِ بالقاعدة. العطار وَكَذا نظائره. الدسوقي (قَوْلُهُ : وَكَذَا نَظَائِرُهُ يحتملُ أنَّ المراد نظائر الجزئي المذكورِ مِن نحو : كُلُّ فرس حيوان، ويحتمل أنَّ المراد نظائرُ القاعدة المذكورة مِنْ أنَّ الموجبة الجزئية تنعكس كَنَفْسِهَا ، والسَّالبة الجزئية تنعكسُ كَنَفْسِهَا ، فإذا عُلِمَ أَنَّ السَّالبةَ الكُلِّيَّةَ تنعكس كَنَفْسِهَا ؛ عُلِمَ أن لا شيء مِنَ الإنسان بحجرٍ ؛ ينعكس إلى : لا شيء مِنَ الحجرِ بإنسان.
Dusuqi: (His statement: 'It is known that every human is an animal' converts to 'some animal is human') meaning: that which is a particular among the particulars of the subject of the rule, and his statement: (converts ... etc.) thus is the judgment of that particular. The way of knowing that is that you take a particular from the particulars of the subject of the rule, like the mentioned particular, and you attribute the subject of the rule to it, and you make the predicate a minor premise, and you make the rule a major premise, so there results a syllogism in the first figure productive of the establishment of the judgment of the subject of the rule for that particular, so the mentioned knowledge occurs. For example, it is said: Every human is an animal, a universal affirmative, and the universal affirmative converts to a particular affirmative, which is: Some animal is human. So the commentator's statement: ('It is known that every human is an animal ... etc.') means: after the establishment of the mentioned syllogism; for by mere knowledge of the mentioned rule, the mentioned knowledge does not occur, but rather the ability to attain it occurs. However, since this ability is strong, he expressed it as knowledge. That is: and since knowledge of the rule entails what was mentioned, the rules are applicable to the judgments of particulars; for if not for that applicability, this knowledge would not occur upon knowledge of the rule. al-'Attar: And likewise its analogues. Dusuqi: (His statement: 'And likewise its analogues' ) It is possible that what is meant is analogues of the mentioned particular, such as: Every horse is an animal, and it is possible that what is meant is analogues of the mentioned rule, such as: the particular affirmative converts to itself, and the particular negative converts to itself. So if it is known that the universal negative converts to itself, it is known that 'No human is a stone' converts to 'No stone is a human'.
Source pages p. 166

Commentary
This passage is an expansion on how conversion of propositions is known. Dusuqi begins by explaining the statement 'It is known that every human is an animal converts to some animal is human'. He clarifies that 'every human is an animal' is a particular instance of the subject of the logical rule of conversion. The way to know the conversion is to take a particular (like 'human'), attribute the subject of the rule (here 'human') to it, and then make the predicate (animal) the minor premise and the universal rule (the conversion law) the major premise. This yields a first-figure syllogism that proves the conversion for that particular. For example, 'Every human is an animal' is a universal affirmative; the rule says universal affirmatives convert to particular affirmatives, so we conclude 'Some animal is human'. Dusuqi then explains that the knowledge of conversion is not immediate from knowing the rule alone; it requires constructing such a syllogism. However, since the rule strongly entails the conclusion, the ability to know it is so strong that it is called knowledge. This shows that logical rules apply to particular judgments only through this mediation. al-'Attar then comments on the phrase 'And likewise its analogues', offering two interpretations: either analogues of the particular example (like 'every horse is an animal') or analogues of the rule itself (like conversion of particular affirmative and particular negative). The latter includes the conversion of universal negative: from 'No human is a stone' we get 'No stone is a human'.

Key Terms
القوة القريبة من الفعل
انعكاس

C) It gives no knowledge at all.

Key Takeaway
General logical rules provide the ability to derive particular cases, but actual knowledge requires a syllogistic step.

Contradictories of Coextensive Universals
Author defines the relationship between contradictories of coextensive universals

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether the contradictories of two coextensive universals are also coextensive and distinguish conceptual contradiction from propositional contradiction.

Core Idea
If two terms share exactly the same individuals, their negations also share exactly the same individuals.

This passage from the commentary on al-Tadhhib extends the discussion of the five universals to their contradictories. It shows that the relation of coextensiveness (tasāwī) between two universals implies the same relation between their negations. This principle is crucial for valid immediate inferences and for understanding how concepts relate in definitions and arguments in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main claim
Read the author's statement: 'And their two contradictories likewise.' This means that if two universals are coextensive (mutasāwiyān), then their contradictories (formed by negation) are also coextensive.
The commentator explains this is a necessary logical consequence, not just a similar relationship.
2
Analyze the example
The author gives the example of non-human (lā insān) and non-rational (lā nāṭiq). The proof works by contradiction: if some non-human were rational, then some rational being would be non-human, contradicting the original coextensiveness.
This shows the logical equivalence: (∀x)(Human(x) ↔ Rational(x)) implies (∀x)(¬Human(x) ↔ ¬Rational(x)).
3
Distinguish conceptual vs. propositional contradiction
Al-'Attār explains that in the conceptual domain, contradictories are concepts maximally distant without considering existence. In the propositional domain, contradiction requires one true and one false about the same subject. The passage deals with the conceptual kind.
This distinction is important because conceptual contradiction does not require existential commitment; both contradictories can be absent if the subject does not exist.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ: في مبادئ التصورات. (وَنَقِيْضَاهُمَا) أي: نَقِيضا المتساويين كاللا إنسان، واللاناطق. قال الدسوقي: (قَوْلُهُ: وَنَقِيْضَاهُمَا كَذَلِكَ) أي: ونقيضا المتساويين كالمتساويين في التّساوي، هذا مدلوله، ويلزم ذلك أنَّ النَّقيضَيْنِ مُتساويانِ، فَقولُ الشَّارِحِ؛ أي: مُتساويانِ؛ تفسير باللازم، تأمل). وقولُهُ: (ونقيضُهما كذلك؛ مثلاً: يجب أن يصدق: كلُّ لَا إنسان لا ناطق، وكُلُّ لا ناطق غيرُ إنسان، وإلا..؛ أي: وإلا يصدق هذا؛ لكانَ... إلخ؛ أي: لَصَدَقَ نَقيضُه وهو: بعضُ اللا إنسان ليسَ بِلا ناطق، فيكون بعضُ لَا إنسان ناطقاً؛ أي: وهو باطل؛ لانعكاسه إلى بعض الناطق لا إنسان، وهو محال. (قَوْلُهُ: كَاللا إنسان... إلخ) فيهِ مُسامحةٌ؛ حيثُ أُدخِلَ حرفُ التّعريف على حرفِ السَّلْبِ، وهو «لا» النافية مع كونِهِ خاصًا بالدخول على الأسماء، وهذا كثيراً ما يقعُ لأهل هذا الفنّ، ولعلهم ينظرون إلى أنَّ حرفَ السَّلْبِ صارَ كَجزء الكلمة التي دخل عليها حرف التّعريف، كما يأتي في المعدولة. قال العطار: (قَوْلُهُ: وَنَقِيْضَاهُمَا... إِلَخ) نَقيضُ كُلِّ شيءٍ رفعه، فإذا اعتبر مفهوم من غيرِ اعتبارِ صِدقِهِ على شيءٍ وضمَّ إليه كلمةَ النَّفي؛ حصلَ هناكَ مَفهوم آخَرُ في غاية البعدِ عنِ الأَوَّلِ، وَسُمِّيَا مُتَناقِضَيْن بمعنى أنَّهمُا مُتباعِدَانِ تَباعداً لا يتصوَّرُ ما هو أبلغ منه فيما بين المفهومات المعتبرة بِلَا مُلاحظة صدقهما على شيءٍ؛ لا بِمعنى أَنَّهُمَا لا يجتمعان في ذات ولا يرتفعان عنها؛ لِجَوازِ ارتفاعِهما عند عدم تِلْكَ الذَّاتِ، وهذا هو التَّناقض في بابِ التَّصوراتِ، فأمَّا إذا اعتبر صدقهُ على شيءٍ؛ فهوَ التَّناقضُ في القضايا المعرَّفُ بأَنَّهُ اختلافُ قضيَّتَيْنِ... إلخ، والمراد هُنَا الأَوَّلُ، والثَّاني الَّذي تعرَّضُوا لأحكامِهِ، فَلِذَلِكَ أخرجُوا الأوَّلَ عَن تعريفه بقيدِ قضيَّتَيْنِ.
A section on the principles of conceptualizations. (And their two contradictories) meaning: the two contradictories of the two coextensive terms, such as non-human and non-rational. Al-Dasuqi said: (His statement: and their two contradictories likewise) means: the two contradictories of the coextensive terms are like the coextensive terms in equality; this is its signification. From that it follows that the two contradictories are coextensive. So the commentator's statement [that they are coextensive] is an interpretation by implication. Ponder. And his statement: (And their contradictories likewise; for example: it must be true that every non-human is non-rational, and every non-rational is non-human. Otherwise... i.e., if this is not true, then... i.e., its contradictory would be true: some non-human is not non-rational, so some non-human is rational; i.e., this is false because it converts to some rational being is non-human, which is impossible. (His statement: such as non-human... etc.) There is leniency in it, since the definite article is entered on the letter of negation, 'la' the negating particle, despite it being specific to entering on nouns. This occurs frequently among the people of this art. Perhaps they consider that the letter of negation has become like a part of the word on which the definite article enters, as will come in the modificated (ma'dula) terms. Al-'Attar said: (His statement: and their two contradictories... etc.) The contradictory of each thing is its negation. So if a concept is considered without regard to its truth about anything, and the word of negation is attached to it, another concept results that is extremely distant from the first, and they are called contradictory in the sense that they are distant from each other in a distance than which no greater is conceivable among concepts considered without observing their truth about anything; not in the sense that they cannot coexist in a self nor be absent from it, because it is possible that both are absent when that self does not exist. This is contradiction in the domain of conceptualizations. But if truth about something is considered, that is contradiction in propositions, defined as two propositions differing... etc. The intended meaning here is the first. The second is that for which they discussed rulings. That is why they excluded the first from its definition by the qualification 'two propositions'.
Source pages p. 303

Commentary
This passage defines the relationship between the contradictories of two coextensive (mutasawiyan) universals. The author states that if two terms are coextensive—meaning every individual of one is an individual of the other and vice versa—then their contradictories are also coextensive. The example given is 'non-human' (لا إنسان) and 'non-rational' (لا ناطق). Al-Dasuqi explains that the commentator's statement that the contradictories are 'like the coextensive terms in equality' is actually an interpretation by implication; the correct meaning is that they are coextensive, not merely similar. The necessity is demonstrated: if every non-human were not non-rational, then some non-human would be rational, which implies some rational being is non-human—a contradiction. Al-Dasuqi also notes a linguistic leniency: the definite article is attached to the negating particle 'لا', which is normally reserved for nouns, but this usage is common in this discipline. Al-'Attar then clarifies two meanings of 'contradiction' (tanāquḍ). In the domain of pure concepts (tasawwurāt), contradictories are mutually exclusive concepts without regard to existence—e.g., 'human' and 'non-human' are as distant as possible. In the domain of propositions (taṣdīqāt), contradiction requires one proposition to be true and the other false about the same subject. The passage emphasizes that the contradiction here is the conceptual kind, which is why the definition of propositional contradiction includes the qualification 'two propositions'.

Key Takeaway
The contradictories of coextensive universals are also coextensive, a logical necessity grounded in the nature of conceptual contradiction without existential commitment.

Coextension of Contradictories: A Proof by Contradiction
Commentators prove that the contradictories of coextensive terms are also coextensive using a proof by contradiction and example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct a proof by contradiction to demonstrate that the contradictories of coextensive terms are themselves coextensive.

Core Idea
Coextension of terms implies coextension of their contradictories, proven via contradiction.

This passage presents a subtle logical result: if two terms apply to exactly the same individuals, then their negations also apply to the same individuals. The commentators prove this using a proof by contradiction, showing that assuming the opposite leads to an impossibility. This illustrates how logical principles can extend our knowledge about terms.

Guided Path
1
State the claim and example
The claim is that the contradictories of coextensive terms are coextensive. The example uses 'human' and 'rational' as coextensive terms, so their contradictories 'not-human' and 'not-rational' are claimed to be coextensive. Write down the universal affirmative 'Every not-human is not-rational' as the statement to be proved.
2
Assume the negation of the claim
Assume the universal affirmative is false. Its contradictory (the particular negative) is then true: 'Some not-human is not not-rational'. This is the starting point of the proof by contradiction.
3
Derive a consequence
From 'Some not-human is not not-rational', apply the principle that negation of a negation is affirmation: 'Some not-human is rational' follows. This is a particular affirmative.
This step uses obversion/double negation.
4
Convert the proposition
Convert the particular affirmative 'Some not-human is rational' to 'Some rational is not-human'. This conversion is valid for particular affirmatives.
Conversion of particular affirmatives is always valid.
5
Recognize the contradiction
The result 'Some rational is not-human' contradicts the premise that 'human' and 'rational' are coextensive (i.e., every rational is human). Hence the assumption is impossible, so the original universal affirmative must be true. Similarly, prove the converse direction holds.
This establishes the coextension of the contradictories.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ: في مبادئ التصورات. وَنَقِيضَاهُمَا كَذَلِكَ؛ أي: نَقِيضا المتساويين كالمتساويين في التّساوي. ويلزم ذلك أنَّ النَّقيضَيْنِ مُتساويانِ. مثَالُهُ: كَاللَّا إِنْسَانِ وَاللَّانَاطِقِ. فَيَصْدُقُ كُلُّ مِنْ نَقِيضِ الْمُتَسَاوِيَيْنِ عَلَى كُلِّ مَا يَصْدُقُ عَلَيْهِ نَقِيضُ الْآخَرِ. تَوْضِيحُهُ: أَنْ تَقُولَ: كُلُّ لَا إِنْسَانَ هُوَ لَا نَاطِقٌ، فَهَذِهِ مُوجَبَةٌ كُلِّيَّةٌ. فَلَوْ لَمْ يَصْدُقْ مُدَّعَانَا هَذَا لَصَدَقَ نَقِيضُهُ، وَنَقِيضُهُ سَالِبَةٌ جُزْئِيَّةٌ: بَعْضُ لَا إِنْسَانَ لَيْسَ بِلا نَاطِقٍ. وَهَذِهِ السَّالِبَةُ الْجُزْئِيَّةُ يَلْزَمُهَا مُوجَبَةٌ جُزْئِيَّةٌ: بَعْضُ لَا إِنْسَانَ نَاطِقٌ؛ لِأَنَّ نَفْيَ النَّفْيِ إِثْبَاتٌ. وَهَذَا مُحَالٌ لِانْعِكَاسِهِ إِلَى بَعْضِ النَّاطِقِ لَا إِنْسَانَ. وَكَذَا تَقُولُ فِي عَكْسِ الْمِثَالِ: كُلُّ لَا نَاطِقٍ لَا إِنْسَانَ. فَثَبَتَ أَنَّ نَقِيضَيِ الْمُتَسَاوِيَيْنِ مُتْسَاوِيَانِ.
Chapter: On the principles of concepts. And their two contradictories are likewise; i.e., the contradictories of coextensive terms are like coextensives in coextension. And from that it follows that the two contradictories are coextensive. Its example: like the not-human and the not-rational. So each of the contradictories of the coextensives holds of everything of which the contradictory of the other holds. Its clarification: you say: Every not-human is not-rational. This is a universal affirmative. If this claim were not true, its contradictory would be true, and its contradictory is a particular negative: Some not-human is not not-rational. And this particular negative entails a particular affirmative: Some not-human is rational; because the negation of a negation is an affirmation. And this is impossible because it converts to some rational is not-human. And likewise you say in the converse of the example: Every not-rational is not-human. So it is established that the contradictories of coextensives are coextensive.
Source pages p. 303

Commentary
This passage is a proof that the contradictories of coextensive terms (two terms that apply to exactly the same set of things) are themselves coextensive. The commentators begin by stating the claim: 'and their two contradictories are likewise', meaning the contradictories of coextensive terms are equal in extension. They then illustrate with the example of 'not-human' and 'not-rational'. The proof proceeds by contradiction: assume the claim does not hold. For instance, take the universal affirmative 'Every not-human is not-rational'. If this is false, its contradictory is true: 'Some not-human is not not-rational'. Since negation of a negation is affirmation, this implies 'Some not-human is rational'. But this converts to 'Some rational is not-human', which is false because 'human' and 'rational' are coextensive (or at least rational implies human). The same reasoning applies in reverse direction. Thus the original claim must be true, establishing that the contradictories are coextensive. The argument uses immediate inference rules: conversion, obversion, and the principle of contradiction.

Key Terms
نقيض (naqīḍ): The contradictory of a term: formed by prefixing the negation particle 'lā' to the term, producing a term that applies to everything the original term does not apply to.
متساويان (mutasāwiyān): Two terms that are coextensive, i.e., they apply to exactly the same set of individuals; here used as a relation between the original terms and also between their contradictories.
موجبة كلية (mūjaba kulliyya): A universal affirmative categorical proposition: 'Every S is P'.
سالبة جزئية (sāliba juz'iyya): A particular negative categorical proposition: 'Some S is not P'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If the terms 'kāfir' and 'disbeliever' are coextensive, what can we conclude about the terms 'not-kāfir' and 'not-disbeliever'?
A) They are also coextensive.
B) They are mutually exclusive.
C) They are subcontraries.
D) They are identical in meaning.

Key Takeaway
The key logical insight is that coextension of terms is preserved under negation, which can be proved using contradiction and immediate inferences.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Condition for Equal Concepts: Contradictories Must Match
Author explains the condition for two concepts being equal and the absurdity of one applying to a part of the other's contradictory.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to verify whether two concepts are equal by testing the co-extension of their contradictories, and reconstruct the conditional proof that establishes this condition.

Core Idea
Equal concepts require their contradictories to be equally extensive; otherwise equality breaks.

This passage from al-Dasuqi's commentary on the Tadhheeb al-Mantiq explains a crucial condition for two concepts being equal (mutasawiyān). You will learn how to test equality by examining the contradictories, and see a conditional argument that shows the absurdity of one equal applying to part of the other's contradictory. The logical tool you gain here is essential for evaluating definitions and immediate inferences in the Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Main Claim
Read the first part: 'Otherwise, the very same one of the two equals would apply to some part of the contradictory of the other, and that is impossible.' This sets up a conditional: if equality fails, an absurdity follows. The condition being argued for is that the contradictories of two equal concepts must also be co-extensive.
2
Understand the 'Otherwise' Scenario
The author explains what 'otherwise' means: if it is not the case that each contradictory of one equal applies to everything the other contradictory applies to (i.e., they are not fully co-extensive), then one of the equals would apply to part of the other's contradictory. This would make one equal more general, violating equality.
The scenario covers two cases: either the contradictories share no application, or they share only partially.
3
Follow al-Dasuqi's Concrete Example
Al-Dasuqi assigns: 'one of the two equals' = 'rational' (nāṭiq), 'the contradictory of the other' = 'not human' (lā insān). He shows that if the condition fails, then 'rational' would apply to something that is 'not human', which is impossible because 'rational' and 'human' are equal. This illustrates the logical problem.
The example assumes 'human' and 'rational' are equal, so their contradictories 'non-human' and 'non-rational' must also be co-extensive.
4
Reconstruct the Conditional Proof
The argument is a conditional modus tollens: If the contradictories are not fully co-extensive, then one equal is more general (which contradicts equality). Since equality is given, the consequent is false, so the antecedent must be false. Therefore, the contradictories are fully co-extensive. This proves the condition.
The form: (A → B), ¬B, therefore ¬A. Here A = 'contradictories not fully co-extensive', B = 'one equal more general'. The impossibility of B (muḥāl) is the denial.
5
Apply to Islamic Examples
Now test a pair from usul al-fiqh: consider 'wājib' (obligatory) and 'mā lā budda minhu' (that which cannot be avoided). Are they equal? Check whether their contradictories ('ghayr wājib' and 'mā yumkinu tarkuhu') are co-extensive. If yes, equality holds; if not, one is broader.
This condition helps in evaluating definitions and synonymy claims in Islamic disciplines.

(قَوْلُهُ : لأَنَّهُ صَدَقَ) أي: وُجِدَ .
Otherwise, the very same one of the two equals would apply to some part of the contradictory of the other, and that is impossible because it would be the truth of one of the two equals without the other. Al-Dasuqi: And his saying 'truth' etc. indicates the consequent. And his saying 'the very same one of the two equals' means, for example, 'rational'. And his saying 'to some part of the contradictory' means, for example, 'not human'. And his saying 'because it would be truth of one of the two equals' means 'rational', and his saying 'without the other' means 'human'. And his saying 'and that is impossible' indicates the second part of the conditional that invalidates the consequent, so the antecedent is invalidated, and its contradictory is established. His saying: 'Otherwise, the very same would apply' etc. means: if not every one of the contradictories of the two equals applies to everything to which the other contradictory applies, either by neither applying to anything to which the other contradictory applies at all, or each applying to some part of the other contradictory, then it would be necessary that the very same one of the two equals applies to some of what the other contradictory applies to; that is, it would be necessary that one of the two equals is more general than the other because it is unique in applying to some part of the contradictory of that other. His saying: 'because it would truth' means: it would exist.
Source pages p. 306

Commentary
This passage explains a condition regarding two concepts that are equal (mutasawiyan), i.e., co-extensive. The author states that if it were not for the condition (i.e., if they were not completely equal in application), then one of the two equals would apply to a part of the contradictory of the other. This is impossible because it would mean that one of the equals applies without the other, violating their equality. The commentator al-Dasuqi unpacks this: 'the truth' refers to the consequent of a conditional statement. He identifies 'one of the two equals' as 'rational', and 'a part of the contradictory' as 'not human', and 'without the other' as 'human'. He explains that the impossibility is indicated in the conditional that invalidates the consequent, thus the antecedent is false and its negation is true. Then the author elaborates: 'otherwise' means if it is not the case that every one of the contradictories of the two equals applies to everything to which the other contradictory applies—either because neither applies to anything of the other, or each applies to only a part—then necessarily one of the equals would apply to a part of what the other contradictory applies to, making one more general than the other. The phrase 'because it would truth' means 'it would exist'. The argument uses a conditional proof to show that if two concepts are truly equal, their contradictories must also be co-extensive; otherwise one of the equals would be broader, contradicting their equality.

Absolute Generality and Specificity and Their Negations
Defines absolute generality and specificity with example, explains negation inversion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the relationship of absolute generality/specificity between two terms and determine how their negations invert that relationship.

Core Idea
The negation of the more general becomes more specific, and vice versa.

In this lesson, we explore the concepts of absolute generality and specificity (al-a'amm wa al-akhass mutlaqan) as defined in classical mantiq. Using the classic example of animal and human, the author explains how one term can be absolutely more general than another, and then shows a fascinating property: when you negate both terms, their relationship reverses. This principle is crucial for understanding hierarchical relationships in definitions and for reasoning about categories in Islamic jurisprudence and theology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two terms in the example
Find which terms are being compared as absolutely general and specific. The passage gives 'animal' (hayawan) and 'human' (insan).
2
Check the direction of applicability
Verify that the general term applies to all individuals of the specific term, but not vice versa. Every human is an animal, but not every animal is human. This is what makes animal absolutely more general.
The phrase 'without the linguistic converse' (bidun al-'aks al-lughawi) indicates that the simple converse (every animal is human) is false.
3
Understand 'mutlaqan' (unrestricted)
Note that the generality holds in every aspect, not just some. The commentator al-Attar explains it means without restriction to one aspect rather than another.
This is contrasted with relative generality (a'amm min wajh), where generality holds in one aspect but not another.
4
Apply the negation inversion rule
Negate both terms and observe that the relationship flips: the negation of the more general (non-animal) becomes more specific than the negation of the more specific (non-human). Everything that is non-animal is also non-human, but not vice versa.
The passage explicitly says: 'their negations are opposite: the negation of the more general is more specific, and the negation of the more specific is more general.'
5
Test the 'without universal converse' condition
Confirm that while the negation of the general (non-animal) implies the negation of the specific (non-human), the reverse does not hold universally. For example, a horse is non-human but is not non-animal.
This one-way implication is central to the logical relationship.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَأَعَمُ وَأَخَصُ مُطْلَقَاً كالحيوان والإنسان، فإنَّ الحيوان يصدق على أفراد الإنسان، بدونِ العكس اللُّغويّ، فالصَّادقُ على كلّ الأفراد أعم مطلقاً، والآخَرُ أخص مطلقاً . وَنَقِيْضَاهُمَا بِالْعَكْسِ، فَنَقِيضُ الأَعَمِّ أَخَصُّ، وَنَقِيضُ الأَخَصّ أَعَمُ؛ لأنَّ كلَّ ما يصدقُ عليه نقيضُ الأعم، يصدقُ عليه نقيضُ الأخص، من غير عكس كلّي. قال الدسوقي: (قَوْلُهُ : فَأَعَمُ وَأَخَصٌ مُطْلَقَاً) أي : فأحدهما أعم عموماً مُطلقاً ، والآخَرُ أخ خصوصاً مُطلقاً . (قَوْلُهُ: بِدُوْنِ الْعَكْسِ اللُّغَوِيّ) هو مُطلق مخالفة، فيصدقُ بإبدال الأوَّلِ بالثاني والثَّاني بالأَوَّلِ؛ معَ بقاء الكم والكيف كَما هو. (قَوْلُهُ : بِالْعَكْسِ) أي : مُلتبسانِ بالعكس؛ أي: بعكس المعنيين. قال العطار: (فَأَعَمُ وَأَخَصُ مُطْلَقَاً) أي : مِن غير تقييد بوجه دون وجه.
So the more general and more specific in an absolute sense, like animal and human. For animal applies to all individuals of human, without the linguistic converse. So the one that applies to all individuals is absolutely more general, and the other is absolutely more specific. And their negations are opposite: the negation of the more general is more specific, and the negation of the more specific is more general, because everything that the negation of the more general applies to, the negation of the more specific applies to, without a universal converse. Al-Dasuqi said: His statement: 'more general and more specific in an absolute sense' means one of them is more general in an absolute generality, and the other is more specific in an absolute specificity. His statement: 'without the linguistic converse' means absolute opposition, so it is true by substituting the first with the second and the second with the first, while keeping quantity and quality as they are. His statement: 'by the converse' means they are intertwined with the converse, i.e., the converse of the two meanings. Al-Attar said: His statement: 'more general and more specific in an absolute sense' means without restriction to one aspect rather than another.
Source pages p. 308

Commentary
This passage defines the relationship of absolute generality and specificity (al-a'amm wa al-akhass mutlaqan) and explains how their negations invert that relationship. The author begins by giving the classic example: animal (al-hayawan) is absolutely more general than human (al-insan), because animal applies to all individuals of human, but not conversely (without the linguistic 'aks, i.e., 'every human is an animal' is true, but 'every animal is human' is false). The term 'mutlaqan' means unrestricted: the generality and specificity hold in every aspect, not just some. The commentator al-Dasuqi explains that 'mutlaqan' means one of them is general in an absolute sense and the other specific in an absolute sense. Al-Attar adds that it means without restriction to any particular aspect. The passage then turns to the negations: the negation of the absolutely general is more specific, and the negation of the absolutely specific is more general. This is because whatever the negation of the general applies to (e.g., 'non-animal' applies to stones, trees, etc.), the negation of the specific (e.g., 'non-human') also applies to those same things. However, the converse is not universally true: 'non-human' applies to horses and donkeys, but 'non-animal' does not apply to them (since they are animals). So the relationship is one-way: the negation of the general is more specific than the negation of the specific. Al-Dasuqi clarifies that 'by the converse' means the two negations are intertwined with the converse of the original meanings: the general becomes specific when negated, and the specific becomes general.

Key Takeaway
In absolute generality/specificity, negating both terms inverts the relationship: what was general becomes specific, and what was specific becomes general, but the converse is not universal.
