The Inversion of Generality Through Negation
Author states and proves that negation reverses the generality/specificity relationship with limited converse.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the relationship between the negations of general and specific terms and verify whether the converse is universal or particular using the principle of contraposition.

Core Idea
Negation flips the generality hierarchy: the negation of a general term is more specific, but the converse is only particular.

In this passage from Tadhheeb al-Mantiq, the author establishes a fundamental principle of term logic: how negation inverts the scope of universal and particular terms. Using the example of 'animal' and 'human', he shows that the negation of the broader term yields a narrower negation, while the negation of the narrower term yields a broader negation, but not universally. This principle is a cornerstone for handling definitions and divisions in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original general and specific terms
In the example, 'animal' is more general than 'human' because every human is an animal. Note this hierarchy.
2
State their negations
The negations are 'non-animal' and 'non-human'. Recognize that negation changes the extension of a term.
3
Determine the hierarchy of the negations
According to the text, 'non-animal' is more specific than 'non-human' because everything that is a non-animal (e.g., stone) is also a non-human. This follows from contraposition: if all humans are animals, then all non-animals are non-humans.
4
Test the converse
Does every non-human also be a non-animal? No, because e.g., a horse is non-human but is an animal. Therefore, the converse is only particular (some non-humans are non-animals).
5
Review the proof by contradiction
The text proves that the universal affirmative 'all non-animals are non-humans' must hold. If it did not, there would exist a non-animal that is a human, implying a human without being an animal, which is impossible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في مبادئ التصورات. ونقيضاهما أي نقيضا الأعم والأخص مطلقاً كاللاحيوان واللا إنسان بالعكس أي بعكس المعنيين فنقيض الأعم أخص ونقيض الأخص أعم لأن كل ما يصدق عليه نقيض الأعم يصدق عليه نقيض الأخص من غير عكس كلي. قوله: بالعكس أي ملتبسان بالعكس أي ملتبس بعكس المعنيين بمعنى الأصلين أي متلبسان بعكس صفة الأصلين من العموم والخصوص من التباس الموصوف بالصفة فتأمل. قوله: لأن كل ما يصدق عليه نقيض الأعم... إلخ أي أن كل ما يصدق عليه لا حيوان من الحجر والشجر وسائر النباتات والمعادن والعناصر يصدق عليه لا إنسان وليس كل ما يصدق عليه لا إنسان يصدق عليه لا حيوان وذلك لصدق لا إنسان بالفرس والبغل والحمار مع عدم صدق لا حيوان عليها. قوله: من غير عكس كلي وأما العكس الجزئي وهو بعض ما يصدق عليه نقيض الأخص يصدق عليه نقيض الأعم فصحيح بل هذا العكس المنطقي اللازم للقضية. قوله: أما الأول فلأنه لو لم يصدق كل ما يصدق عليه نقيض الأعم يصدق عليه نقيض الأخص... الخ. وتوضيحه أن تقول مثلا لو لم يصدق كل ما يصدق عليه لا حيوان يصدق عليه لا إنسان لصدق نقيضه ونقيضه سالبة جزئية وهو بعض ما صدق عليه لا حيوان ليس يصدق عليه لا إنسان وهذه السالبة الجزئية تستلزم موجبة جزئية وهي بعض ما يصدق عليه لا حيوان يصدق عليه إنسان لأن النقيضين لا يرتفعان وهذا اللازم باطل لما يلزم عليه من صدق الأخص وهو إنسان بدون الأعم وهو حيوان وإذا بطل هذا اللازم بطل ملزومه وهو السالبة الجزئية الناقضة للأصل فصدق الأصل وهو الموجبة الكلية القائلة كل ما يصدق عليه لا حيوان يصدق عليه لا إنسان وهو المطلوب.
Chapter on the principles of conceptualizations. And their two negations, i.e., the negations of the absolutely more general and more specific, like 'non-animal' and 'non-human', are (by the inverse), i.e., by the inverse of the two meanings. So the negation of the more general is more specific, and the negation of the more specific is more general, because everything of which the negation of the more general is true, the negation of the more specific is true of it, without a universal converse. He said: 'by the inverse' means they are accompanied by the inverse, i.e., accompanied by the inverse of the two meanings in the sense of the two origins, i.e., accompanied by the inverse of the attribute of the two origins of generality and particularity, in the manner of the description being attached to the described, so ponder. He said: 'because everything of which the negation of the more general is true... etc.' means that everything of which 'non-animal' is true, like stone, tree, all plants, minerals, and elements, 'non-human' is true of it, but not everything of which 'non-human' is true is 'non-animal' true of it, because 'non-human' is true of horse, mule, and donkey while 'non-animal' is not true of them. He said: 'without a universal converse' and as for the particular converse, which is 'some of that of which the negation of the more specific is true, the negation of the more general is true of it', it is correct; rather, this is the logical converse necessary for the proposition. He said: 'as for the first, because if it were not the case that everything of which the negation of the more general is true, the negation of the more specific is true of it... etc.' And its clarification is that you say, for example: if it were not the case that everything of which 'non-animal' is true, 'non-human' is true of it, then its negation would be true, and its negation is a particular negative, i.e., 'some of that of which 'non-animal' is true is not such that 'non-human' is true of it'. This particular negative entails a particular affirmative, i.e., 'some of that of which 'non-animal' is true is such that 'human' is true of it', because the two contradictories (negations) do not both disappear. This consequent is false, because it would entail the truth of the more specific, which is 'human', without the more general, which is 'animal'. And when this consequent is false, its antecedent is false, i.e., the particular negative that contradicts the original statement. Thus the original statement is true, i.e., the universal affirmative saying 'everything of which 'non-animal' is true, 'non-human' is true of it', and that is what is required.
Source pages p. 309

Commentary
This passage defines and proves a key relationship between the negations of general and specific terms in Islamic logic. The text opens with a chapter heading, "Fi Mabadi' al-Tasawwurat" (On the Principles of Conceptualizations), indicating that this discussion belongs to the foundational elements of concept formation. The main claim is stated: the negation of the more general (al-a'am) and the negation of the more specific (al-akhas) are inversely related. That is, the negation of a general term is more specific, and the negation of a specific term is more general. For example, 'non-animal' is a negation of the general term 'animal', and it is more specific than 'non-human' (the negation of 'human'), because 'non-animal' excludes a narrower class. The proof given is that everything of which the negation of the general is true (e.g., anything that is not an animal) is also something of which the negation of the specific is true (e.g., not human). However, the converse does not hold universally: not everything that is not human is not an animal (e.g., a horse is not human but is an animal). Therefore, the relationship is one-directional in terms of universal scope, but a particular converse holds: some things that are not human are also not animal (like stones). The commentary by al-Dasuqi expands on the meaning of 'by the inverse' (bil-'aks), explaining that the two negations are 'accompanied by the inverse' of the two original terms' attributes of generality and particularity. He then illustrates the main point with examples from creation: stones, trees, plants, minerals, and elements fall under 'non-animal' and also under 'non-human', while horses, mules, and donkeys fall under 'non-human' but not under 'non-animal'. The distinction between universal and particular converse is clarified: the universal converse is invalid, but the particular converse (the logical converse of the universal affirmative) is valid. The text then provides a detailed proof by contradiction for the first part (that everything falling under the negation of the general falls under the negation of the specific). Assume the contrary: suppose some x is not a general (e.g., not an animal) but is not also not a specific (i.e., it is a human). This would imply that a specific term (human) is true without the general term (animal), which is impossible by definition (human is a type of animal). Therefore, the original universal affirmative must be true. This proof is central to understanding how negations invert generality hierarchies.

Key Terms
نقيض (naqid): The negation or contradictory of a term; e.g., the negation of 'animal' (حيوان) is 'non-animal' (لا حيوان). [Note: No significant shift; stable across the tradition.]
أعم (a'am): The more general term, broader in extension, such as 'animal' compared to 'human'. [Note: No significant shift.]
أخص (akhas): The more specific term, narrower in extension, such as 'human' compared to 'animal'. [Note: No significant shift.]
عكس ('aks): The converse; here it refers to the relationship between the negations being the inverse of the original terms' generality/specificity. [Note: In logic, 'aks often refers to conversion of propositions; here it is used more broadly to mean 'inverse'.]
كلّي (kulli): Universal; in this context, a universal converse would mean 'every x that is negation of specific is also negation of general', which is false. [Note: No significant shift.]
جزئي (juz'i): Particular; the particular converse holds: 'some x that is negation of specific is also negation of general'. [Note: No significant shift.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following correctly illustrates the relationship described in the passage?
A) Everything that is not an animal is not a human, and everything that is not a human is not an animal.
B) Everything that is not an animal is not a human, but some things that are not humans are animals.
C) Some things that are not animals are not humans, and everything that is not a human is not an animal.
D) The negation of 'animal' is more general than the negation of 'human'.

Key Takeaway
Negation inverts the hierarchy of generality: the negation of a general term is more specific, but the reflection back is only partial; this is proven by contraposition and illustrated with the example of 'animal' and 'human'.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Using the terms 'kalam' (speech) and 'fi'l' (action) in usul al-fiqh, where every speech-act is an action, determine the relationship between their negations. Is the universal converse valid? Provide an example.
?
In a kalam argument about the attributes of Allah, consider the terms 'possible' (mumkin) and 'contingent' (hadith). Assuming every contingent is possible, what is the relationship between their negations? Verify the universal and particular converse with examples from the created world.

Why Non-Animal Implies Non-Human: A Proof by Contradiction
Author proves that if the negation of the broader applies, the negation of the narrower necessarily applies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the proof by contradiction that if the negation of a broader term applies, then the negation of the narrower term necessarily applies, using the example of 'non-animal' and 'non-human'.

Core Idea
Negation of the broader is more specific than negation of the narrower.

This passage demonstrates a fundamental logical principle: the negation of a broader category (e.g., non-animal) implies the negation of a narrower category (e.g., non-human). The author proves this by contradiction, showing that denying this leads to an absurdity. This principle is essential for understanding how negations relate to hierarchies of concepts, a tool used extensively in Islamic theology and jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim to prove
The claim is the universal affirmative: 'Every non-animal is a non-human'. Understand this means anything that is not an animal is also not a human.
This is the principle 'negation of broader implies negation of narrower' applying to the pair animal/human.
2
Assume the opposite
Assume the claim is false, so its contradictory is true: 'Some non-animal is not a non-human'. Translate this as: there exists something that is non-animal but not non-human, i.e., it is human.
The contradictory of a universal affirmative is a particular negative.
3
Derive the absurdity
From 'not non-human' we get 'human'. So we have 'Some non-animal is a human'. This is impossible because a human is necessarily an animal (the broader). Thus we have a human existing without being an animal, a contradiction.
This step uses the rule that two contradictories cannot both be false, and the principle of the hierarchy: narrower implies broader.
4
Conclude the original is true
Since the assumption of falsehood leads to an absurdity, the original universal affirmative must be true. Therefore, the principle holds: if the negation of the broader applies, the negation of the narrower applies.
This is a classic proof by contradiction (burhan al-khalf).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أَمَّا الأَوَّلُ فَلِأَنَّهُ لَوْ لَمْ يَصْدُقْ كُلُّ مَا يَصْدُقُ عَلَيْهِ نَقِيضُ الْأَعَمِّ، يَصْدُقُ عَلَيْهِ نَقِيضُ الْأَخَصِّ لَصَدَقَ نَقِيضُهُ، وَهُوَ سَالِبَةٌ جُزْئِيَّةٌ: بَعْضُ مَا يَصْدُقُ عَلَيْهِ نَقِيضُ الْأَعَمِّ لَيْسَ يَصْدُقُ عَلَيْهِ نَقِيضُ الْأَخَصِّ، وَهَذِهِ السَّالِبَةُ الْجُزْئِيَّةُ تَسْتَلْزِمُ مُوجِبَةً جُزْئِيَّةً: بَعْضُ مَا يَصْدُقُ عَلَيْهِ نَقِيضُ الْأَعَمِّ يَصْدُقُ عَلَيْهِ عَيْنُ الْأَخَصِّ؛ لِأَنَّ النَّقِيضَيْنِ لَا يَرْتَفِعَانِ. وَهَذَا لَازِمٌ بَاطِلٌ؛ لِمَا يَلْزَمُ عَلَيْهِ مِنْ صِدْقِ الْأَخَصِّ دُونَ الْأَعَمِّ. فَإِذَا بَطَلَ هَذَا اللَّازِمُ بَطَلَ مَلْزُومُهُ، فَصَدَقَ الْأَصْلُ وَهُوَ الْمُوجَبَةُ الْكُلِّيَّةُ. وَتَوْضِيحُهُ بِالْمَادَّةِ: أَنْ تَقُولَ: كُلُّ لَا حَيَوَانَ لَا إِنْسَانَ. إِذْ لَوْ لَمْ يَصْدُقْ لَصَدَقَ نَقِيضُهُ: بَعْضُ لَا حَيَوَانَ لَيْسَ لَا إِنْسَانَ، وَيَلْزَمُهُ بَعْضُ لَا حَيَوَانَ إِنْسَانٌ، وَهُوَ مُحَالٌ؛ لِمَا فِيهِ مِنْ وُجُودِ الْأَخَصِّ وَهُوَ الْإِنْسَانُ بِدُونِ الْأَعَمِّ وَهُوَ الْحَيَوَانُ.
As for the first, it is because if it were not true that: 'Everything that the negation of the broader applies to, the negation of the narrower applies to', then its contradictory would be true, which is a particular negative: 'Some of what the negation of the broader applies to, the negation of the narrower does not apply to'. And this particular negative entails a particular affirmative: 'Some of what the negation of the broader applies to, the narrower itself applies to', because two contradictories cannot both be false. And this consequent is false, because it would entail the narrower being true without the broader. So when this consequent is false, its antecedent is false, so the original (the universal affirmative) is true. Its illustration with a concrete example: you say 'Every non-animal is a non-human'. For if it were not true, its contradictory would be true: 'Some non-animal is not a non-human', which entails 'Some non-animal is a human', which is impossible because it involves the narrower (human) existing without the broader (animal).
Source pages p. 310

Commentary
This passage proves the logical claim that the negation of a broader term (e.g., 'non-animal') is more specific than the negation of a narrower term (e.g., 'non-human'). The author uses a proof by contradiction. He assumes the universal affirmative 'Every non-animal is a non-human' is false. Its contradictory is the particular negative 'Some non-animal is not a non-human'. Because contradictories cannot both be false, this particular negative entails the particular affirmative 'Some non-animal is a human' (since negating the negation gives affirmation). This consequent is absurd because it asserts the existence of a human (the narrower) without being an animal (the broader), which is impossible since human implies animal. Therefore, the assumption of falsehood is false, so the original universal affirmative is true. The proof relies on the logical equivalence between a particular negative and a particular affirmative when the subject exists, and the impossibility of the narrower class existing without the broader class.

نقيض الأخص

B) Because two contradictories cannot both be true.
C) Because two contradictories cannot both be false, so negating the negation gives affirmation.
D) Because of the principle of non-contradiction.

Key Takeaway
The principle that the negation of a broader term implies the negation of a narrower term is proven by contradiction: denying it leads to the impossible coexistence of the narrower without the broader.

Immediate Inference: Proving the Non-Universality of the Converse via Contraposition
Author proves that the converse of the universal affirmative (negation of broader to narrower) does not hold universally.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct a proof that the converse of a universal affirmative is not universally true, using contrapositive conversion and the falsity of a consequence.

Core Idea
The contrapositive of a universal affirmative is valid, but its direct converse is not universal.

This passage presents a subtle but important proof in immediate inference. The author shows that even though the statement 'every negation of the broader is a negation of the narrower' is true, its converse 'every negation of the narrower is a negation of the broader' is false. The proof employs contrapositive conversion (عكس النقيض) and a reductio ad absurdum argument. We will walk through each step, paying attention to how the commentators clarify the quantifiers.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim and its contradictory
The claim to be proved is a particular negative: 'Not every negation of the narrower is a negation of the broader.' Its contradictory is a universal affirmative: 'Every negation of the narrower is a negation of the broader.' The proof assumes this universal affirmative is true and then derives a contradiction.
2
Apply contrapositive conversion
From the assumed universal affirmative, apply the valid conversion of contrapositive: 'Every Q is P' becomes 'Every not-P is not-Q'. Here, from 'Every non-human is non-animal' we derive 'Every animal is human'. This step is crucial because it transforms the proposition into a more testable form.
Contrapositive conversion (عكس النقيض) is a valid immediate inference; it preserves truth.
3
Evaluate the derived proposition
The derived proposition 'Every animal is human' is false, as there are many animals that are not human. This falsehood is obvious and accepted by all.
The example uses common knowledge to establish falsity without further argument.
4
Conclude the falsity of the assumption
Since the contrapositive is false, its antecedent (the assumed universal affirmative) must also be false. This follows from the principle that if a necessary consequence (lāzim) is false, the condition (malzūm) is false. Therefore, the original claim (the particular negative) is true.
This logical move relies on the relationship between a statement and its contrapositive: they are logically equivalent.
5
Note the stylistic refinement by al-ʿAtṭār
Al-ʿAtṭār points out that to avoid ambiguity, the negation should precede 'every' in Arabic, making the quantifier explicitly a particular negative. This ensures the reader correctly identifies the proposition type.
This is a linguistic precision important for avoiding misinterpretation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأمَّا الثَّاني ; فلأنَّه لو لم يَصدُقُ : ليس كل ما يصدق عليه نقيض الأخصّ، ليس يصدق عليه نقيضُ الأعم» ; لَصَدَقَ : «كل ما يصدق عليه نقيضُ الأخطّ ، يصدق عليه نقيضُ الأعم».
الدسوقي: (قَوْلُهُ: وَأَمَّا الثَّانِي) أي : وهو قوله : من غير عكس كلّي، وتوضيحه: أن تقول : لو لم يصدق قولنا : ليس كل ما يصدق عليه لا إنسان؛ يصدق عليه لا حيوان؛ لَصَدَقَ نقيضُهُ وهو موجبةٌ كُلِّيَّة ; لأنَّ مُدَّعانا سالبة جزئية، ونقيضها موجبة كُلِّيَّةٌ ، وهو : كلُّ مَا يصدقُ عليهِ لَا إنسان; يصدقُ عليهِ لا حيوان، ويعكس بعكس النَّقيض الموافق إلى : كلُّ مَا يصدق عليه حيوان; يصدق عليه إنسان، وهو باطل; لأنَّه صدق الأخصّ وهو إنسان على جميع أفراد الأعم وهو حيوان . وإذا بطل عكس نقيض المدَّعى ; كان نقيضُه باطلاً; لأنَّ العكس لازم لِلنَّقيض، وكذبُ اللازم يستلزم كذب الملزوم ، وإذا بطل نقيضُ المدعى; كان المدعى صادقاً، وهو المطلوب.
العطار: (قَوْلُهُ : فَلِأَنَّهُ لَوْ لَمْ يَصْدِقْ : كُلُّ مَا يَصْدُقُ عَلَيْهِ نَقِيضُ الْأَخَصِّ; لَيْسَ يَصْدُقُ ... إلخ) الأَوْلَى تقديم «ليس» على كلّ ; لأنَّ مُدَّعانا سالبة جزئية، والسَّلْبُ إذا تقدَّمَ على كلِّ» ; يكونُ السُّورُ سلباً جزئيا . اهـ شيخنا .
As for the second: because if the statement 'not everything that the negation of the narrower applies to, the negation of the broader applies to it' were not true, then the statement 'everything that the negation of the narrower applies to, the negation of the broader applies to it' would be true. Al-Dasuqi: (His statement: 'As for the second') i.e., his statement 'without a universal converse', and its clarification: say: if our statement 'not every non-human is non-animal' were not true, then its contradictory would be true, which is a universal affirmative, because our claim is a particular negative, and its contradictory is a universal affirmative, namely 'everything that non-human applies to, non-animal applies to it'. Then it is converted by the valid contrapositive to 'everything that animal applies to, human applies to it', which is false because it would make the narrower (human) true of all individuals of the broader (animal). And if the contrapositive of the contradictory of the claim is false, then the contradictory itself is false, because the contrapositive is a necessary consequence of the contradictory, and the falsity of the necessary consequence entails the falsity of its condition. When the contradictory of the claim is false, the claim itself is true, which is what was desired. Al-Atar: (His statement: 'Because if the statement: everything that the negation of the narrower applies to, the negation of the broader does not apply to... etc.') It would be better to place 'not' before 'every', because our claim is a particular negative, and when the negation precedes 'every', the quantifier becomes a particular negative. End of our teacher's words.
Source pages p. 312

Commentary
This passage presents a proof by contradiction for the second claim: that the converse of the previously established universal affirmative is not universal. In other words, while 'every negation of the broader is a negation of the narrower' is true, its converse 'every negation of the narrower is a negation of the broader' is false. The truth is actually a particular negative: 'not every negation of the narrower is a negation of the broader.' The proof assumes the opposite, i.e., that the universal affirmative converse is true. From this assumption, the commentator al-Dasuqi explains that if the claim (the particular negative) were false, its contradictory (the universal affirmative) would be true. Then, by the valid conversion of contrapositive (عكس النقيض الموافق), the universal affirmative 'every non-human is non-animal' converts to 'every animal is human', which is clearly false because the species 'human' does not cover all animals. Since the contrapositive is false, its antecedent (the universal affirmative) must also be false, thus the original claim (the particular negative) is true. Al-Atar adds a stylistic note: it is better to place the negation before 'every' to clearly indicate a particular negative quantifier. The passage demonstrates the importance of contrapositive conversion in logical proofs and the careful handling of quantifiers in Arabic logical terminology.

Key Terms

سالبة جزئية
موجبة كلية

B) Because it would make the species 'human' apply to all animals, which is false (e.g., horses are animals but not human).
C) Because the contrapositive conversion is invalid.
D) Because the universal affirmative is always false when the subject is empty.

Key Takeaway
The contrapositive of a universal affirmative is a valid immediate inference, but the direct converse may be false; logical proof can reveal the non-universality of a converse by deriving an absurd consequence.

Proof of the Particular Negative via Contrapositive
Al-Dasuqi proves a logical rule using reductio and contrapositive conversion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct a reductio proof that establishes a particular negative statement using contrapositive conversion.

Core Idea
The truth of a particular negative is proven by showing its universal affirmative contradictory leads to absurdity via contrapositive.

Al-Dasuqi presents a rigorous logical proof demonstrating that a specific particular negative proposition follows from the impossibility of its universal affirmative counterpart. This lesson will walk you through his reductio argument and the use of contrapositive ('aks al-naqid) as an immediate inference. Understanding this technique equips you to validate similar claims in Islamic logical texts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim and its contradictory
the claim (mudda'a) is a particular negative: 'not every thing that is not human is not animal'. Its contradictory is a universal affirmative: 'every thing that is not human is not animal'.
In Arabic logic, the contradictory of a particular negative (saliba juz'iyya) is a universal affirmative (mujiba kulliyya).
2
Assume the contradictory is true
For reductio, assume the universal affirmative 'every thing that is not human is not animal' is true.
3
Apply contrapositive to the assumed proposition
Convert 'every not human is not animal' by swapping subject and predicate and negating both: yields 'every animal is human'.
This conversion (aks al-naqid al-muwafiq) preserves truth if the original is true.
4
Show absurdity and conclude
The contrapositive 'every animal is human' is false because human is more specific than animal; not all animals are human. Therefore the assumption (the universal affirmative) is false, so its contradictory (the original particular negative) is true.
This is a standard reductio: falsehood of the contrapositive implies falsehood of the original universal, hence truth of its contradictory.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأمَّا الثَّاني ؛ فلأنَّه لو لم يَصدُقُ : ليس كل ما يصدق عليه نقيض الأخصّ، ليس يصدق عليه نقيضُ الأعم» ؛ لَصَدَقَ : «كل ما يصدق عليه نقيضُ الأخطّ ، يصدق عليه نقيضُ الأعم». الدسوقي (قَوْلُهُ: وَأَمَّا الثَّانِي) أي : وهو قوله من غير عكس كلّي، وتوضيحه: أن تقول : لو لم يصدق قولنا : ليس كل ما يصدق عليه لا إنسان؛ يصدق عليه لا حيوان؛ لَصَدَقَ نقيضُهُ وهو موجبةٌ كُلِّيَّة ; لأنَّ مُدَّعانا سالبة جزئية، ونقيضها موجبة كُلِّيَّةٌ ، وهو : كلُّ ما يصدقُ عليهِ لَا إنسان؛ يصدقُ عليهِ لا حيوان، ويعكس بعكس النَّقيض الموافق إلى : كلُّ مَا يصدق عليه حيوان؛ يصدق عليه إنسان، وهو باطل؛ لأنَّه صدق الأخصّ وهو إنسان على جميع أفراد الأعم وهو حيوان . وإذا بطل عكس نقيض المدَّعى ؛ كان نقيضُه باطلاً؛ لأنَّ العكس لازم لِلنَّقيض، وكذبُ اللازم يستلزم كذب الملزوم ، وإذا بطل نقيضُ المدعى؛ كان المدعى صادقاً، وهو المطلوب.
And as for the second: because if it were not true that 'not every thing that the negation of the more specific applies to, the negation of the more general applies to', then it would be true that 'every thing that the negation of the more specific applies to, the negation of the more general applies to'. Al-Dasuqi: His saying: 'and as for the second' means his statement: 'without a universal conversion', and its clarification: you say: if our statement 'not every thing that 'not human' applies to, 'not animal' applies to' were not true, then its contradictory would be true, which is a universal affirmative; because our claim is a particular negative, and its contradictory is a universal affirmative, which is: 'every thing that 'not human' applies to, 'not animal' applies to', and it is converted by the appropriate contrapositive to: 'every thing that 'animal' applies to, 'human' applies to', and this is false, because it requires the more specific (human) to apply to all individuals of the more general (animal). And if the contrapositive of the claim's contradictory is false, then its contradictory is false, because the conversion is entailed by the contradictory, and the falsehood of the entailer necessitates the falsehood of the entailed. And if the contradictory of the claim is false, then the claim is true, and that is what was sought.
Source pages p. 312

Commentary
This passage is a logical proof by reductio ad absurdum. Al-Dasuqi begins by stating the second condition (likely from a previous text) and then provides a detailed explanation. The claim to be proved is a particular negative: 'not every thing that is a negation of the more specific is a negation of the more general' (i.e., ليس كل ما يصدق عليه لا إنسان يصدق عليه لا حيوان). The proof assumes the opposite: that the negation of this claim (i.e., the universal affirmative 'every thing that is not human is not animal') is true. From this universal affirmative, he applies the contrapositive ('aks al-naqid) in its 'appropriate' form (al-muwāfiq): swapping subject and predicate with their negations, yielding 'every animal is human'. But this is clearly false, since 'human' is more specific than 'animal' and does not apply to all animals. Therefore, the assumed universal affirmative must be false, so its contradictory (the original particular negative) is true. The reasoning relies on the principle that the contrapositive is logically equivalent to the original proposition (as a necessary consequence). In the context of Islamic logic, this proof demonstrates a standard technique for establishing the truth of a particular negative statement by showing that its universal affirmative counterpart leads to an unacceptable result.

نقيض الأعم
عكس النقيض
سالبة جزئية

C) Every human is animal.

A particular negative can be proved by showing that its universal affirmative contradictory leads to a false contrapositive, thus forcing the original to be true.

Why Negations of Partially Opposed Universals May Differ
Author answers why negations of partially opposed universals may not share the same relationship.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze the logical relationship between the negations of two universals that have umum min wajh and determine whether the relationship is the same or changes to total opposition.

Core Idea
The negations of two partially opposed universals may have total opposition.

In this passage, the author addresses a question about the relationship between the negations of two universals that have 'commonality from one aspect' (umum min wajh). The objector expects the negations to have the same relationship, but the answer reveals an asymmetry: the negations can be totally opposed. This illustrates a key principle in immediate inferences involving contradictory negations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
The objector notes that for equal universals, the author stated the negations share the same relationship, so why not for partially opposed universals? The objection expects consistency.
2
Find the author's answer
The author replies that it is not consistent because sometimes the negations have total opposition, as in the example of animal and non-human.
3
Work through the example
Animal and non-human have umum min wajh: they meet in horse, animal is exclusive to man, non-human to stone. Their negations (non-animal and human) have total opposition: nothing is both non-animal and human.
4
Generalize the principle from al-Dasuqi
Al-Dasuqi clarifies: for any two universals with umum min wajh, the relationship between their negations may be umum min wajh or total opposition; only partial opposition is consistent (since total opposition implies it).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل: النسبة بين هذين الكليين المباينة الجزئية، فلم لم يقل: ونقيضاهما كذلك كما قال في المتساويين؟ قلت: لأن العموم من وجه يتحقق بين الحيوان واللا إنسان مع التباين الكلي بين نقيضيهما، فإن اللاحيوان لا يصدق على الإنسان وبالعكس. قال الدسوقي: حاصله أن كل كليين بينهما عموم وخصوص وجهي لا يطرد أن يكون بين نقيضيهما كذلك، بل تارة يكون بين نقيضيهما العموم والخصوص الوجهي، وتارة يكون بين نقيضيهما التباين الكلي، والمطرد إنما هو التباين الجزئي. ويجتمعان في الفرس، وينفرد الأول في الإنسان والثاني في الحجر.
So it is said: The relationship between these two universals is partial opposition, so why did he not say: 'and their two negations are likewise' as he said in the case of equal universals? I said: Because commonality from one aspect holds between animal and non-human with total opposition between their two negations, for non-animal does not apply to human and vice versa. Al-Dasuqi said: The summary is that every two universals between which there is commonality and particularity from one aspect, it does not necessarily hold that between their two negations it is likewise; rather, sometimes between their two negations there is commonality and particularity from one aspect, and sometimes between their two negations there is total opposition, and what is consistent is only partial opposition. And they unite in the horse, and the first is exclusive to man and the second to stone.
Source pages p. 316

Commentary
This passage is dialectical: it presents an objection and its answer regarding the logical relationship between the negations of two universals that have 'partial opposition' (al-mubayana al-juz'iyya) or 'commonality from one aspect' (umum min wajh). The questioner asks why the author (or commentator) did not state that the negations of such universals also have the same relationship, as was done for equal universals (mutasawiyan). The answer explains that this is not consistent: for example, 'animal' (hayawan) and 'non-human' (la insan) have a relationship of umum min wajh (they agree in some instances, like horse, and differ in others: 'animal' includes humans, 'non-human' includes stones). However, their negations—'non-animal' (la hayawan) and 'human' (insan)—have total opposition (tabayun kulli), because nothing can be both non-animal and human. Al-Dasuqi's commentary clarifies the general rule: when two universals have umum min wajh, their negations may sometimes also have umum min wajh, but sometimes they have total opposition. The only relationship that is guaranteed between the negations is partial opposition (since total opposition implies partial opposition). The example of horse, man, and stone illustrates the scope: animal and non-human meet in horse, animal is exclusive to man, non-human to stone. This guards against the misunderstanding that logical relationships between contraries or negations mirror those between the original terms.

Key Terms
المباينة الجزئية
التباين الكلي
عموم من وجه
نقيض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If the two universals 'animal' (hayawan) and 'non-human' (la insan) have a relationship of umum min wajh, what is the relationship between their negations 'non-animal' (la hayawan) and 'human' (insan)?
A) Total opposition (tabayun kulli)
B) Umum min wajh
C) Equality (tasawi)
D) Partial opposition (mubayana juz'i)

Key Takeaway
The relationship between the negations of two universals with umum min wajh is not fixed; it can be total opposition, so one cannot assume the original relationship is preserved.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Given the two universals 'al-insan' (human) and 'al-hayawan' (animal), which have a relationship of umum wa khusus mutlaq (absolute commonality and particularity), determine the relationship between their negations 'la insan' and 'la hayawan', and explain why it is not necessarily total opposition.
?
Construct a similar example from fiqh: assume the terms 'khamr' (wine) and 'nabidh' (date drink) have umum min wajh (some date drinks are intoxicating, some are not; wine is always intoxicating). Analyze the relationship between their negations 'la khamr' and 'la nabidh' and state whether it is total opposition or something else.

Why Negations of Partially Overlapping Universals Are Not Always Alike
Commentator expands on why only partial difference is constant for negations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the relationship between negations of universals with 'umum wa khusus min wajh' is not always the same, and identify the constant relationship as partial difference.

Core Idea
The negations of partially overlapping universals may differ totally, but always have at least partial difference.

In the study of immediate inferences in logic, we examine how negating terms affects their relationships. The commentator here addresses a subtle point: why the author only said the negations of partially overlapping universals have partial difference, not the same overlap. We'll uncover the reasoning behind this precision.

Guided Path
1
Recall the relationship types
Review the three main relationships between universals: co-extensive (متساويان), umum wa khusus min wajh (عموم وخصوص وجهي), and total difference (تباين كلي).
Example: animal and rational are co-extensive? Actually animal and human have umum wa khusus min wajh? Wait, human is a species of animal, so animal and human have umum wa khusus mutlaq? The common terms in Mantiq: animal and human have general-specific absolute. For min wajh, use examples like animal and white. But the passage uses animal and not-human, which is unusual. Stick to the passage.
2
Analyze the question
The commentator asks: why did the author not say the negations have the same relationship (umum wa khusus min wajh) as the originals? Identify the implied claim that one might expect consistency.
For co-extensive universals, the author did say the negations are likewise (also co-extensive).
3
Understand the answer
The commentator explains that it is not constant: sometimes the negations have umum wa khusus min wajh, sometimes total difference. The constant is only partial difference (تباين جزئي), because total difference implies partial difference (since total affirmation implies partial).
Example: animal and not-human have umum wa khusus min wajh, but their negations (not-animal and human) are totally different.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بين اللاحيوان واللا أبيض، عموم من وجه كما يُعرَفُ بأدنى تأمل، فلم لم يقل : ونقيضاهما كذلك» كما قال فى المتساويين؟ قُلْتُ : لأنَّ العموم من وجه يتحقَّقُ بين الحيوان واللا إنسان مع التباين الكلّيّ بين نقيضيهما، فإنَّ اللاحيوان لا يصدق على الإنسان وبالعكس. (قَوْلُهُ: قُلْتُ ... إلخ) حاصله : أنَّ كلَّ كُلِّيَّين بينهما عموم وخصوص وجهي لا يَطَّردُ أن يكون بين نقيضَيْهما كذلك، بل تارةً يكون بين نقيضَيْهما العمومُ والخصوص الوجهي، وتارةً يكون بين نقيضيهِمَا التَّباينُ الكُلِّي، والمطَّردُ إنَّما هو التباين الجزئيُّ ؛ لأنَّهُ إِمَّا مَوجودٌ صراحةً ، أو في ضمن التباين الكُلّيّ ؛ لأنَّ الإيجاب الكُلِّيَّ في ضمنِهِ الإيجابُ الجزئي، فكل إنسانٍ حيوان في ضمنِهِ : بعضُ الإنسانِ حيوانُ، فَلَمَّا كانَ المطَّردُ إنَّما هو التَّباين الجزئي ؛ عبرَ بِهِ المصنف. (قَوْلُهُ : يَتَحَقَّقُ بَيْنَ الْحَيَوَانِ وَالَّلا إِنْسَانِ) وذلكَ لِتصادقهما في الفرس، وانفرادِ الحيوان في الإنسان، وانفرادِ اللا إنسان في الحجر (قَوْلُهُ : بَيْنَ نَقِيضَيْهِمَا :أي: وهما : لا حيوان وإنسان، وقوله: (وبالعكس)؛ أي والإنسانُ لا يصدق عليه شيءٌ مِمَّا صدق عليه لا حيوان . (قَوْلُهُ : بَيْنَ اللَّاحَيَوَانِ وَاللَّا أَبْيَضَ ... إِلَخ يجتمعان في الحجر الأسود مثلاً، وينفرد اللاحيوانُ في الحجرِ الأبيض، واللاأبيضُ في الإنسان الأسود، وقد تسامحُوا في إدخال أل» على حرفِ النَّفي؛ لتنزيلهم إيَّاهُ منزلة الجزء مِمَّا بعده، ونظيره قولُ الشَّاعِرِ : فَلَا وَاللهِ لا يُلقَى لِمَابِيْ وَلَا لِلِمَابهم أبداً دواء (قَوْلُهُ: يَتَحَقَّقُ بَيْنَ الحَيَوَانِ وَاللَّا إِنْسَانِ) أي: بين الأعمّ ونَقيض الأخص، فيجتمعان في الفرس، وينفرد الأوَّلُ في الإنسانِ والثَّاني في الحجرِ . فلو قالَ: «ونقيضَاهما كذلك لانْتَقَضَ بذلك؛ بل النّسبة بينهما التَّباين الجزئي . فإِنَّهما إن تفارَقًا في جميعِ الصُّوَرِ كاللاحيوان والإنسان؛ فالتَّباين إِنْ الكلِّيُّ ثابت، وهو مستلزِمُ للتَّباين الجزئي، وإلا ؛ فالعموم من وجه. فالتباين الجزئي ثابت بين نقيضيهما أيضاً على التَّقديرين، (كالمتَبَابِنَيْنِ) فإنَّ بين نقيضَيْهما أيضاً (قَوْلُهُ : لَانْتَقَضَ بِذَلِكَ أي : بهذا المثال، وهو الحيوانُ واللا إنسانُ . (قَوْلُهُ : بَل النِّسْبَةُ بَيْنَهُمَا ) أي : بينَ الكُلِّيَّين (۱) اللَّذَيْنِ بينهما عموم وخصوص وجهي . (قَوْلُهُ : فَإِنَّهُمَا ) أي : النَّقيضَيْنِ ؛ علَّة لِقولِهِ : (بل النسبة ... إلخ)، وقوله: (وهو)؛ أي : التَّباينُ الكُلَّيُّ مُستلزم للجزئي. (وقوله : وإلا ) أي : وإلا يتفارقا في جميعِ الصُّوَرِ، بَلْ في بعضِهَا، فَالعمومُ . . . إلخ . وقوله : (على التَّقديريْنِ)؛ أي: تقديرِ تفارقِ النَّقيضين في جميعِ الصُّوَرِ وتفارقهما في بعضها . (قَوْلُهُ : كَالْمُتَبَايَنَيْن) أي : كنقيضي المتباينين أو المراد: كالمتباينين من حيثُ النقيض ؛ أي: فبين نقيضَيْهما تباين جزئي، وأمَّا هُما؛ فبينهما تباين كلي.
Between 'not-animal' and 'not-white' there is general and specific from one aspect, as known with minimal reflection. So why did he not say: 'And their two negations are likewise' as he said regarding co-extensives? I say: Because general and specific from one aspect occurs between animal and not-human while there is total difference between their two negations, for not-animal does not apply to human and vice versa. (His saying: 'I say... etc.) Its summary: That any two universals between which there is general and specific from one aspect, it is not constant that between their two negations it is likewise. Rather, sometimes between their two negations there is general and specific from one aspect, and sometimes between their two negations there is total difference. And what is constant is only partial difference, because it is either present explicitly or implicitly within total difference, because total affirmation implies partial affirmation: 'Every human is an animal' implies 'Some human is an animal.' So since what is constant is only partial difference, the author expressed it. (His saying: 'It occurs between animal and not-human') That is because they agree in horse, and animal is exclusive to human, and not-human is exclusive to stone. (His saying: 'Between their two negations') i.e., they are: not-animal and human. And his saying: (and vice versa) i.e., human does not apply to anything that not-animal applies to. (His saying: 'Between not-animal and not-white... etc.') They coincide in e.g., black stone, and not-animal is exclusive to white stone, and not-white is exclusive to black human. They have been lenient in inserting 'al' on the particle of negation, treating it as a part of what follows. An analogous instance is the poet's saying: 'For by Allah, no remedy is ever found for my affliction nor for theirs.' (His saying: 'It occurs between animal and not-human') i.e., between the more general and the negation of the more specific, so they coincide in horse, and the first is exclusive to human and the second to stone. So if he had said: 'and their two negations are likewise', it would have been contradicted by that; rather the relationship between them is partial difference. For if they differ in all instances, like not-animal and human, then total difference is established, and it entails partial difference; otherwise, general and specific from one aspect. So partial difference is established between their two negations also on both suppositions. (Like the two completely different) for between their two negations also (His saying: 'would be contradicted by that') i.e., by this example, which is animal and not-human. (His saying: 'Rather the relationship between them') i.e., between the two universals that have general and specific from one aspect. (His saying: 'For they') i.e., the two negations; a cause for his saying: (Rather the relationship... etc.), and his saying: (and it) i.e., total difference entails partial. (And his saying: 'otherwise') i.e., if they do not differ in all instances but in some, then general and specific... etc. And his saying: (on both suppositions) i.e., the supposition of the two negations differing in all instances and their differing in some. (His saying: 'Like the two completely different') i.e., like the two negations of two completely different universals, or the intended meaning: like two completely different from the perspective of negation; i.e., between their two negations there is partial difference, but as for them (the original universals), between them there is total difference.
Source pages p. 317

Commentary
This passage is an expansion of a matn point about the relationship between the negations (نقيضان) of two universals (كليان) that are related by 'umum wa khusus min wajh' (general and specific from one aspect). The commentator begins by presenting a question: why did the author (المصنف) not state that the two negations of such universals also have the same relationship (i.e., umum wa khusus min wajh), just as he stated for co-extensive universals (متساويان)? The answer is that it is not consistent: sometimes the negations have the same relationship, but sometimes they have total difference (تباين كلي). The constant is only partial difference (تباين جزئي), because it is either explicit or implied within total difference. The commentator then gives examples: 'animal' and 'not-human' (الحيوان واللا إنسان) coincide in horse (both apply), but animal excludes not-human in human, and not-human excludes animal in stone. Their negations (لا حيوان وإنسان) are totally different (no overlap). This shows that if the author had claimed the relationship is the same for negations, this example would contradict it. However, partial difference remains: in the example of total difference between negations, partial difference is implied (since total includes partial). In the other scenario where negations have umum wa khusus min wajh, partial difference is explicit. Thus the author only mentioned partial difference as the constant relationship. The commentary concludes by comparing this to two completely different universals (متباينان): their negations have partial difference (since they agree in some instances?), but the originals have total difference. Note: The text has some ellipses and repetitive commentary, but the main argument is clear.

Key Terms
عموم وخصوص وجهي
نقيض
تباين كلي
تباين جزئي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Consider the two universals 'animal' (حَيَوان) and 'not-human' (لا إنسان). They have umum wa khusus min wajh. What is the relationship between their negations, 'not-animal' and 'human'?
A) Co-extensive (متساويان)
B) Total difference (تباين كلي)
C) Umum wa khusus min wajh (عموم وخصوص وجهي)
D) Same as original relationship

Key Takeaway
For two universals with umum wa khusus min wajh, their negations always have at least partial difference, but may be totally different; only partial difference is constant.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Given two universals: 'black' (aswad) and 'goat' (ghanam). They have umum wa khusus min wajh: they agree in black goats, black is exclusive in non-goat black things, goat is exclusive in non-black goats. Determine the relationship between 'not-black' and 'not-goat'. Is it also umum wa khusus min wajh? Why or why not? (Hint: consider the constant principle from this lesson.)

Applying the Principle: Determining Negation Relationships
Commentator demonstrates with examples how to apply the rule.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct examples of universals with umum wa khusus min wajh and determine whether their negations have umum wa khusus min wajh or total difference, using the constant principle of partial difference.

Core Idea
Practising the rule: negations of partially overlapping universals always have at least partial difference.

Having understood why the author only mentioned partial difference, we now practise determining the relationship between negations for various pairs of universals. This skill is essential for checking consistency in logical definitions and syllogistic reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original relationship
Confirm that two universals have umum wa khusus min wajh by finding a common instance and exclusive instances for each.
Example: animal and white – they coincide in white animals, animal is exclusive in non-white animals, white is exclusive in non-animal white things.
2
Form the negations
Apply negation to each universal to get the contradictory opposites (e.g., not-animal and not-white).
Be careful with Arabic: لا حيوان, لا أبيض.
3
Test the relationship between negations
Check if the negations have any common instances. If none, they are totally different; if there is overlap and each has exclusive instances, then they have umum wa khusus min wajh.
Example: not-animal and not-white coincide in black stone? Actually the passage says they coincide in black stone? Wait: 'يَجْتَمِعَانِ فِي الْحَجَرِ الأسْوَدِ' they agree in black stone. So they have umum wa khusus min wajh: they also have exclusives.
4
Apply the constant principle
State that regardless of the specific relationship, there is at least partial difference (تباين جزئي) between the negations, because total difference entails it.
This is the key insight of the commentary.

Source Text

(قَوْلُهُ: قُلْتُ ... إلخ) حاصله : أنَّ كلَّ كُلِّيَّين بينهما عموم وخصوص وجهي لا يَطَّردُ أن يكون بين نقيضَيْهما كذلك، بل تارةً يكون بين نقيضَيْهما العمومُ والخصوص الوجهي، وتارةً يكون بين نقيضيهِمَا التَّباينُ الكُلِّي، والمطَّردُ إنَّما هو التباين الجزئيُّ ؛ لأنَّهُ إِمَّا مَوجودٌ صراحةً ، أو في ضمن التباين الكُلّيّ ؛ لأنَّ الإيجاب الكُلِّيَّ في ضمنِهِ الإيجابُ الجزئي، فكل إنسانٍ حيوان في ضمنِهِ : بعضُ الإنسانِ حيوانُ، فَلَمَّا كانَ المطَّردُ إنَّما هو التَّباين الجزئي ؛ عبرَ بِهِ المصنف. (قَوْلُهُ : يَتَحَقَّقُ بَيْنَ الْحَيَوَانِ وَالَّلا إِنْسَانِ) وذلكَ لِتصادقهما في الفرس، وانفرادِ الحيوان في الإنسان، وانفرادِ اللا إنسان في الحجر (قَوْلُهُ : بَيْنَ نَقِيضَيْهِمَا :أي: وهما : لا حيوان وإنسان، وقوله: (وبالعكس)؛ أي والإنسانُ لا يصدق عليه شيءٌ مِمَّا صدق عليه لا حيوان . (قَوْلُهُ : بَيْنَ اللَّاحَيَوَانِ وَاللَّا أَبْيَضَ ... إِلَخ يجتمعان في الحجر الأسود مثلاً، وينفرد اللاحيوانُ في الحجرِ الأبيض، واللاأبيضُ في الإنسان الأسود، وقد تسامحُوا في إدخال أل» على حرفِ النَّفي؛ لتنزيلهم إيَّاهُ منزلة الجزء مِمَّا بعده، ونظيره قولُ الشَّاعِرِ : فَلَا وَاللهِ لا يُلقَى لِمَابِيْ وَلَا لِلِمَابهم أبداً دواء (قَوْلُهُ: يَتَحَقَّقُ بَيْنَ الحَيَوَانِ وَاللَّا إِنْسَانِ) أي: بين الأعمّ ونَقيض الأخص، فيجتمعان في الفرس، وينفرد الأوَّلُ في الإنسانِ والثَّاني في الحجرِ . فلو قالَ: «ونقيضَاهما كذلك لانْتَقَضَ بذلك؛ بل النّسبة بينهما التَّباين الجزئي . فإِنَّهما إن تفارَقًا في جميعِ الصُّوَرِ كاللاحيوان والإنسان؛ فالتَّباين إِنْ الكلِّيُّ ثابت، وهو مستلزِمُ للتَّباين الجزئي، وإلا ؛ فالعموم من وجه. فالتباين الجزئي ثابت بين نقيضيهما أيضاً على التَّقديرين، (كالمتَبَابِنَيْنِ) فإنَّ بين نقيضَيْهما أيضاً (قَوْلُهُ : لَانْتَقَضَ بِذَلِكَ أي : بهذا المثال، وهو الحيوانُ واللا إنسانُ . (قَوْلُهُ : بَل النِّسْبَةُ بَيْنَهُمَا ) أي : بينَ الكُلِّيَّين (۱) اللَّذَيْنِ بينهما عموم وخصوص وجهي . (قَوْلُهُ : فَإِنَّهُمَا ) أي : النَّقيضَيْنِ ؛ علَّة لِقولِهِ : (بل النسبة ... إلخ)، وقوله: (وهو)؛ أي : التَّباينُ الكُلَّيُّ مُستلزم للجزئي. (وقوله : وإلا ) أي : وإلا يتفارقا في جميعِ الصُّوَرِ، بَلْ في بعضِهَا، فَالعمومُ . . . إلخ . وقوله : (على التَّقديريْنِ)؛ أي: تقديرِ تفارقِ النَّقيضين في جميعِ الصُّوَرِ وتفارقهما في بعضها . (قَوْلُهُ : كَالْمُتَبَايَنَيْن) أي : كنقيضي المتباينين أو المراد: كالمتباينين من حيثُ النقيض ؛ أي: فبين نقيضَيْهما تباين جزئي، وأمَّا هُما؛ فبينهما تباين كلي.
Between 'not-animal' and 'not-white' there is general and specific from one aspect, as known with minimal reflection. So why did he not say: 'And their two negations are likewise' as he said regarding co-extensives? I say: Because general and specific from one aspect occurs between animal and not-human while there is total difference between their two negations, for not-animal does not apply to human and vice versa. (His saying: 'I say... etc.) Its summary: That any two universals between which there is general and specific from one aspect, it is not constant that between their two negations it is likewise. Rather, sometimes between their two negations there is general and specific from one aspect, and sometimes between their two negations there is total difference. And what is constant is only partial difference, because it is either present explicitly or implicitly within total difference, because total affirmation implies partial affirmation: 'Every human is an animal' implies 'Some human is an animal.' So since what is constant is only partial difference, the author expressed it. (His saying: 'It occurs between animal and not-human') That is because they agree in horse, and animal is exclusive to human, and not-human is exclusive to stone. (His saying: 'Between their two negations') i.e., they are: not-animal and human. And his saying: (and vice versa) i.e., human does not apply to anything that not-animal applies to. (His saying: 'Between not-animal and not-white... etc.') They coincide in e.g., black stone, and not-animal is exclusive to white stone, and not-white is exclusive to black human. They have been lenient in inserting 'al' on the particle of negation, treating it as a part of what follows. An analogous instance is the poet's saying: 'For by Allah, no remedy is ever found for my affliction nor for theirs.' (His saying: 'It occurs between animal and not-human') i.e., between the more general and the negation of the more specific, so they coincide in horse, and the first is exclusive to human and the second to stone. So if he had said: 'and their two negations are likewise', it would have been contradicted by that; rather the relationship between them is partial difference. For if they differ in all instances, like not-animal and human, then total difference is established, and it entails partial difference; otherwise, general and specific from one aspect. So partial difference is established between their two negations also on both suppositions. (Like the two completely different) for between their two negations also (His saying: 'would be contradicted by that') i.e., by this example, which is animal and not-human. (His saying: 'Rather the relationship between them') i.e., between the two universals that have general and specific from one aspect. (His saying: 'For they') i.e., the two negations; a cause for his saying: (Rather the relationship... etc.), and his saying: (and it) i.e., total difference entails partial. (And his saying: 'otherwise') i.e., if they do not differ in all instances but in some, then general and specific... etc. And his saying: (on both suppositions) i.e., the supposition of the two negations differing in all instances and their differing in some. (His saying: 'Like the two completely different') i.e., like the two negations of two completely different universals, or the intended meaning: like two completely different from the perspective of negation; i.e., between their two negations there is partial difference, but as for them (the original universals), between them there is total difference.
Source pages p. 317

Commentary
This passage is an expansion of a matn point about the relationship between the negations (نقيضان) of two universals (كليان) that are related by 'umum wa khusus min wajh' (general and specific from one aspect). The commentator begins by presenting a question: why did the author (المصنف) not state that the two negations of such universals also have the same relationship (i.e., umum wa khusus min wajh), just as he stated for co-extensive universals (متساويان)? The answer is that it is not consistent: sometimes the negations have the same relationship, but sometimes they have total difference (تباين كلي). The constant is only partial difference (تباين جزئي), because it is either explicit or implied within total difference. The commentator then gives examples: 'animal' and 'not-human' (الحيوان واللا إنسان) coincide in horse (both apply), but animal excludes not-human in human, and not-human excludes animal in stone. Their negations (لا حيوان وإنسان) are totally different (no overlap). This shows that if the author had claimed the relationship is the same for negations, this example would contradict it. However, partial difference remains: in the example of total difference between negations, partial difference is implied (since total includes partial). In the other scenario where negations have umum wa khusus min wajh, partial difference is explicit. Thus the author only mentioned partial difference as the constant relationship. The commentary concludes by comparing this to two completely different universals (متباينان): their negations have partial difference (since they agree in some instances?), but the originals have total difference. Note: The text has some ellipses and repetitive commentary, but the main argument is clear.

Key Terms
عموم وخصوص وجهي
نقيض
تباين كلي
تباين جزئي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Between not-animal and not-white, the commentator says they have umum wa khusus min wajh. Which of the following describes that relationship?
A) Co-extensive
B) Total difference
C) Umum wa khusus min wajh
D) Partial difference (including both)

Key Takeaway
The relationship between negations of partially overlapping universals can be either total difference or umum wa khusus min wajh, but always at least partial difference.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Choose two universals from fiqh that have partial overlap, e.g., 'obligatory' (واجب) and 'financial' (مالي). Determine the relationship between their negations: 'not-obligatory' and 'not-financial'. Is it the same as the original? Show your work using the constant principle.

The Basis of Predication: Union in Existence According to 'Abd al-Ḥakīm
al-Dusuqi quotes Abd al-Hakim's analysis on the existential union underlying predication.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain 'Abd al-Ḥakīm's refined notion of union in existence as the basis of predication and identify why the universal cannot be directly predicated of the particular without reinterpretation.

Core Idea
Predication requires existential union, but that union is one-sided: the particular exists primarily, the universal only by abstraction.

In logic, the basis (manāṭ) of predication (ḥaml) is a crucial issue. The common view holds that predication is possible because subject and predicate share a single existence. But 'Abd al-Ḥakīm al-Siyālkūtī, quoted by al-Dusuqi, offers a refinement: this union cannot be literal, because one accident cannot inhere in two subjects. Instead, the union is ontological priority—the particular has primary existence, and the universal is abstracted from it. This subtle distinction resolves apparent paradoxes and prevents invalid converse predications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the naive view of union
The naive view holds that predication requires a single existence shared equally by subject and predicate. For example, 'Zayd is human' would mean humanity and Zayd share one existence. Recognize why this is problematic: an accident cannot subsist in two subjects.
2
Understand 'Abd al-Ḥakīm's refinement
He defines union in existence not as co-equal sharing but as a relationship of priority: existence belongs to the particular primarily (aṣāla) and to the universal secondarily (bi al-taba') by abstraction. The universal is derived from the particular.
3
Apply to example 'Zayd is human'
Here 'human' is predicated of Zayd because Zayd exists primarily and the universal 'human' is abstracted from him. The union is from Zayd to humanity, not reciprocal.
4
Check the converse 'Human is Zayd'
According to the refined view, this is invalid unless reinterpreted inversely (e.g., by converting the proposition) or metaphorically. Because the union is one-sided, you cannot simply reverse the predicate.
5
Examine the exception for natural universal
'Abd al-Ḥakīm notes that for those who hold the natural universal exists externally (like the ancients), the union is two-sided, so predication of the particular on the universal becomes valid. Understand this as a distinct metaphysical position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وللفاضل عبد الحكيم في هذا المحلّ تحقيق نفيس؛ رأينا ذِكْرَهُ أَوْلَى مِنْ تَرَكِهِ؛ قالَ رَحِمَهُ اللهُ تعالى: مناط الحمل الاتحاد في الوجود، وليس معناه أنَّ وُجوداً واحداً قائمٌ بِهِمَا؛ لامتناع قيامِ العَرَضِ لواحد بمحلين، بل معناه أنَّ الوجود لأحدهما أصالة، ولِلآخَرِ بِالتَّبع، بأنْ يكونَ مُنتزعاً منه، ولا شَكٍّ أنَّ الجزئي هو الموجود أصالة، والأمورُ الكُلِّيَّةُ، سواءٌ كانَت ذاتيَّةً أو عَرَضيَّةٌ؛ مُنتزعةٌ منه على مَا هو تحقيق المتأخّرينَ؛ فالحُكُمُ بِاتِّحادِ الأمورِ الكُلِّيَّةِ مع الجزئي صحيحٌ دونَ العكس، فإن وقع محمولاً كَمَا في بعض الإنسانِ زيد؛ فهو محمول على العكس أو: على التَّأويل، فاندفع ما قِيلَ إِنَّهُ يجوز أنْ يُقالَ: زيد إنسانُ، فَلْيَجُز: الإنسانُ زيد؛ لأنَّ الاتِّحادَ مِنَ الجانبين، فظهر أنَّه لا يمكن حمله على الكُلّيّ، وأما على الجزئي؛ فَلِأَنَّهُ إمَّا نفسُهُ بحيثُ لا تَغاير بينَهُما أصلاً بوَجهِ مِنَ الوجوه حتَّى بالملاحظة والالتفاتِ على ما قال بعضُ المحققينَ أَنَّهُ إذا لُوحِظَ شَخصٌ مَرَّتَيْنِ، وقِيلَ: زيدٌ زيد؛ كانَ مُغَايراً بحسب الملاحظة والاعتبار قطعاً، ويكفي هذا القدرُ مِنَ التَّغاير في الحمل فَلَا يمكنُ تَصوُّرُ الحمل بينَهُما، فضلاً عَن إمكانِهِ، وإِمَّا جُزئيٌّ آخَرُ مُغايرٌ لَهُ ولو بالملاحظة والالتفاتِ، فالحملُ وإِنْ كانَ يتحقَّقُ ظَاهِرَاً؛ لكنَّه في الحقيقةِ حُكُمُ بتصادقِ الاعتبارين على ذاتٍ واحدةٍ، فإِنَّ مَعنى المثالِ المذكور أنَّ زيداً المدركَ أوّلاً هو زيد المدرك ثانياً؛ فالمقصود منه تصادقُ الاعتبارين عليهِ، وَكَذا في قولك: هذا الصَّاحِكُ، وهذا الكاتِبُ، المقصود اجتماعُ الوَصفَيْن فيه، ففي الحقيقة: الجزئي مقول عليه للاعتبارين، نَعم، على القول بوجودِ الكُلِّيَّ الطَّبيعي في الخارج كما هو رأي الأقدمين، والوجود الواحدُ إنَّما قام بالأمور المتعدّدة مِن حيثُ الوحدة؛ لَا مِنْ حيثُ التَّعدُّد، يصحُ حمله على الكُلّيّ؛ لإستوائهِما في الوجودِ والاتحادِ مِن جَانِبَيْنِ، ولعلَّ هذا مبني على ما نقل على الفارابي والشَّيْخُ مِن صِحَّةِ حَمْل الجزئي؛ قالَ: هذا ما عندي في هذا البحثِ الغَامِضِ، والله المُلْهِمُ لِلصَّوابِ ا.هـ.
And for the virtuous Abd al-Hakim in this place is a precious investigation; we considered its mention more appropriate than omitting it. He said, may God have mercy on him: The basis of predication is union in existence, and its meaning is not that a single existence subsists in both; because it is impossible for one accident to subsist in two subjects. Rather, its meaning is that existence belongs to one of them primarily, and to the other secondarily, by being abstracted from it. There is no doubt that the particular is what exists primarily, and the universal matters—whether essential or accidental—are abstracted from it according to the investigation of the later scholars. So the judgment of union of universal matters with the particular is valid, not the reverse. If it occurs as a predicate, as in 'some human is Zayd', then it is predicated inversely or through interpretation. Thus what was said, that it is permissible to say 'Zayd is a human', so let 'Human is Zayd' be permissible because the union is from both sides, is refuted. So it became clear that it is not possible to predicate it on the universal. As for predication on the particular, then either it is itself such that there is no difference between them at all in any respect, even by observation and attention, according to what some verifiers said—that if a person is observed twice and it is said 'Zayd is Zayd', it is different according to observation and consideration definitely, and this amount of difference suffices for predication, so predication between them cannot be conceived, let alone be possible—or it is another particular different from it, even by observation and attention. Then predication, even if it occurs apparently, is in reality a judgment of the co-extensiveness of the two considerations on a single essence. For the meaning of the mentioned example is that Zayd perceived first is Zayd perceived second; so the intent is the co-extensiveness of the two considerations upon him. Similarly, in your saying 'This is the laughing one, and this is the writer', the intent is the combination of the two descriptions in him. So in reality, the particular is predicated of the two considerations. Yes, on the view of the existence of the natural universal in the external world, as is the opinion of the ancients, and the single existence subsists in multiple things from the perspective of unity, not from the perspective of multiplicity, then predication on the universal is permissible because of the equality of both in existence and union from both sides. And perhaps this is based on what was transmitted from al-Farabi and the Shaykh (Ibn Sina) regarding the validity of predicating the particular. He said: This is what I have in this obscure research, and God inspires to what is correct.
Source pages p. 334

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement (hāshiya) by al-Dusuqi, quoting the investigation of 'Abd al-Ḥakīm (al-Siyālkūtī) on the basis of predication (al-ḥaml) between a universal and a particular. The passage clarifies that the union (ittiḥād) required for predication is not a literal sharing of one existence in two subjects—since one accident cannot subsist in two. Rather, it means that existence belongs to the particular primarily (aṣāla) and to the universal secondarily (bi al-taba') by being abstracted from it. Thus the universal is predicated on the particular validly (e.g., 'Zayd is human'), but the converse ('Human is Zayd') is invalid unless reinterpreted inversely or by way of metaphor. The author then addresses the case of two particulars: if they are identical in every respect, even in observation, predication is impossible because any difference suffices for predication; if they are different, the predication actually refers to two considerations of one essence (e.g., 'This laughing one is this writer' means the two attributes coexist in one person). The exception is the ancient view of the natural universal existing externally, where both sides are equal, allowing predication of the particular on the universal. All examples must be from Islamic sciences; here the author uses 'Zayd' as a concrete person, which is common in logic texts.

Key Terms
مناط الحمل
الجزئي
الكلي
الاتحاد في الوجود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to 'Abd al-Ḥakīm, why cannot one say 'Human is Zayd' in the same sense as 'Zayd is human'?
A) Because the predicate must be more general.
B) Because the union in existence is from the particular to the universal, not vice versa.
C) Because 'human' is a genus.
D) Because predication requires complete identity.

Key Takeaway
Predication between a universal and a particular rests on a one-sided existential union: the particular is primary, the universal is abstracted, so the converse is not straightforwardly valid.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take the statement 'This intoxicating drink is khamr'. Explain how the predication holds according to the refined notion: the particular drink exists primarily, and the universal 'khamr' is abstracted from it. Then explain why 'Khamr is this intoxicating drink' would not be valid without reinterpretation (e.g., by conversion or metaphorical interpretation).
?

Conversion of Universal Affirmatives and the Uniformity of Logical Rules
Author discusses the logical converse of the rule and critiques the use of universal affirmative conversion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the standard conversion rule for universal affirmative propositions and distinguish between material-specific and rule-based conversions.

Core Idea
Universal affirmatives convert as particulars, not universals—coextensive terms are the exception, not the rule.

This passage tackles a common misconception about converting universal affirmative propositions. The author shows that while 'every human is rational' can be reversed to 'every rational is human,' this is due to material equality, not a general logical rule. Understanding this distinction is crucial for applying conversion correctly in Islamic logical arguments, especially when dealing with definitions and differentiae.

Guided Path
1
Identify the logical principle under discussion
Find the claim that 'not every differentia of the lower constitutes the higher' – this is the converse of an earlier rule. The author uses the conversion of the universal affirmative to prove this.
The principle involves immediate inference (conversion).
2
Recall the standard conversion rule for universal affirmatives
According to logicians, the universal affirmative 'Every S is P' normally converts to the particular affirmative 'Some P is S'. This is the uniform rule, regardless of material content.
The rule must hold in all materials (ittarad).
3
Examine the counterexample of coextensive terms
In the example 'Every human is rational', the predicate 'rational' is coextensive with 'human'. Here, the conversion 'Every rational is human' is true. But this is a special case, not the general rule.
The universal conversion is valid only when the predicate equals the subject in extension.
4
Apply the rule to the differentia case
The differentia 'rational' constitutes the lower (human) but not the higher (animal). The universal affirmative 'Every constituent of the lower is a constituent of the higher' would convert to 'Some constituent of the higher is a constituent of the lower', which is true (e.g., sentient). But the universal converse fails because 'rational' is not a constituent of animal.
Thus the converse of the differentia rule holds only as a particular, not as a universal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فليس كل فصل يقوم السافل فهو يقوم العالي؛ إذ الموجبة الكلية لا تنعكس كلية. قال الدسوقي: وأما بالمعنى المنطقي؛ فينعكس كما أشار إليه الشارح بقوله: (إذ الموجبة الكلية لا تنعكس)؛ أي: عند أهل هذا الفن كلية؛ أي: لا تنعكس عندهم كلية عكساً صحيحاً. قال العطار: قوله: إذ الموجبة الكلية لا تنعكس كلية استدلال على أنه ليس كل فصل... إلخ، وفيه نظر؛ لأنه إنما يتم الاستدلال به إذا كان المراد بلا تنعكس كلية؛ لا يصح أن تنعكس كلية، وليس كذلك، بل المراد لا يلزم أن تنعكس كلية، وإلا فقد تنعكس كلية، وحينئذ فيتوجه أن يقال: يمكن أن تكون هنا منعكسة كلية، وكذا يقال في قوله الآتي: وقد عرفت أنها لا تنعكس كلية، قاله المحشي، وهو مردود بأن المناطقة قالوا: إن الكلية تنعكس جزئية في جميع المواد، وعكسها كلية في بعضها، وهو ما إذا كان المحمول مساوياً للموضوع، نحو: كل إنسان ناطق؛ فإنه ينعكس إلى: كل ناطق إنسان، لكنه غير معتبر؛ بل تنعكس جزئية لأن انعكاسها كلية لخصوص هذه المادة، فيتخلف فيما إذا كان المحمول أعم، ومبنى قواعدهم على الاطراد؛ فتأمّل. (ثم تعليق الشَّرنوبي: قوله: وليس كذلك ... إلخ) فيه أنه يصح هنا انعكاسها كلية لانتقاضها بناطق، فإنه يقوم السافل وهو الإنسان ولا يقوم العالي وهو الحيوان، وصحة انعكاس الكلية إلى كلية في نحو كل إنسان ناطق فلخصوص المادة وهو تساوي المحمول بالموضوع فليس منطقياً، إذ قواعد الفنّ يجب اطّرادها والمطرد هو عكسها جزئية، فليس في كلام الشارح نظر، بل فيما نظر به واعلم أنني بعد أن كتبت هذه الملاحظة وجدت العطار يؤيدني، ولله الحمد بعد أن نقل هذه العبارة عن الشيخ يس. ا.هـ. الشَّرنوبي.
So not every differentia that constitutes the lower constitutes the higher, for the universal affirmative does not convert universally. Al-Dasuqi said: As for the logical sense, it does convert as the commentator indicated by his statement: 'since the universal affirmative does not convert universally' – meaning according to the practitioners of this art, it does not convert universally in a valid conversion. Al-Attar said: His statement 'since the universal affirmative does not convert universally' is an inference for the claim that not every differentia... etc. But there is a problem in it, because the inference is only valid if by 'does not convert universally' it is meant that it is not valid to convert it universally. But it is not so; rather it means that it is not necessary that it converts universally. Otherwise, it may convert universally. And then it can be said that it is possible that here it does convert universally. And similarly for his later statement: 'you have known that it does not convert universally' – said by the commentator, but it is refuted because the logicians said that the universal converts particularly in all materials, and its conversion as universal occurs in some materials, namely when the predicate is coextensive with the subject, like 'every man is rational'; for it converts to 'every rational is a man'. But this is not considered (as the rule), rather it converts particularly because its universal conversion is specific to this material, and it fails when the predicate is more general. And the basis of their rules is uniformity. So ponder. Then al-Sharnubi's comment: 'His statement: it is not so...' There is in it that here its conversion as universal is valid because of the counterexample of 'rational', for it constitutes the lower (man) and does not constitute the higher (animal). And the validity of the conversion of the universal to universal in the case of 'every man is rational' is due to the specificity of the material, namely the equality of predicate and subject, so it is not logical. For the rules of the art must be uniform, and the uniform rule is its conversion as particular. So there is no problem in the commentator's statement, rather in what he considered as a problem. And know that after I wrote this note, I found al-Attar supporting me, and praise be to God, after he transmitted this statement from Shaykh Yas. End quote. Al-Sharnubi.
Source pages p. 388

Commentary
This passage critically examines the logical converse of the rule that every differentia of the higher constitutes the lower. The author (or commentator) argues that it is not true that every differentia of the lower constitutes the higher, because the universal affirmative proposition (e.g., 'every constituent of the lower is a constituent of the higher') does not convert universally. Al-Dasuqi explains that in the logical sense, the proposition does convert, but only as a particular affirmative – a point already indicated by the commentator's phrase. Al-Attar raises a subtle objection: the commentator's inference relies on the understanding that the universal affirmative cannot convert universally at all, but in fact it can convert universally in some materials (specifically when subject and predicate are coextensive, as in 'every man is rational' -> 'every rational is a man'). Therefore, it is possible that in the present case the universal does convert universally. However, al-Attar then refutes this objection by citing the standard logicians' position: the universal affirmative always converts as a particular affirmative in all subject matters; its conversion as universal is not the general rule but a special case due to material equivalence. Since the rules of logic must be uniform, the general rule (conversion as particular) prevails. Thus, the original inference stands: not every differentia of the lower constitutes the higher. Al-Sharnubi adds a note supporting al-Attar's refutation, emphasizing that the universal conversion of 'every man is rational' is not a logical rule but a material accident, and that the correct counterpart to the differentia rule is the particular affirmative: some constituents of the lower (like 'growing' and 'sensitive') do constitute the higher, but not all (e.g., 'rational' does not constitute 'animal').

Key Terms
انعكاس
الموجبة الكلية
اطراد

A) Because the conversion is invalid in all materials.
B) Because it violates the principle of uniformity (ittirād) since it only holds when subject and predicate are coextensive.
C) Because the logicians did not study such conversions.
D) Because 'rational' is not a true differentia.

Key Takeaway
Universal affirmative propositions convert as particulars in all materials; universal-to-universal conversion is not a logical rule but a material accident.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Distinguishing Perpetual and Necessary Propositions
Author defines perpetual and absolute propositions and distinguishes perpetuity from necessity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between perpetual (دائمة) and necessary (ضرورية) propositions and explain why necessity implies perpetuity but not vice versa using examples from Islamic theology.

Core Idea
Necessity entails perpetuity, but a proposition can be perpetually true without being essentially necessary.

In this lesson, we explore the foundational concepts of perpetual and necessary propositions as defined in classical logic. The author clarifies that while every necessary proposition is perpetual, not every perpetual proposition is necessary, a crucial distinction for understanding modal logic in Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Define perpetual proposition
Identify that 'perpetual' means the predicate holds for all times the subject exists, as in 'Every human is an animal perpetually'.
Note the unrestricted nature of the perpetuity in absolute propositions.
2
Define necessary proposition
Necessity means the predicate cannot separate from the subject, e.g., 'Every human is an animal' is necessary because animality is essential.
3
Recognize the relationship
The text states: 'الضرورة تستلزم الدوام، ولا عكس' (necessity implies perpetuity, but not vice versa). Memorize this principle.
4
Apply to examples
Consider 'كل إنسان حيوان' — it is both necessary and perpetual. 'زيد يركب الخيل دائماً' is perpetual but not necessary because riding is accidental.
Al-Dasuqi illustrates this with 'كل إنسان حادث دائماً' as a perpetual but not necessary example.
5
Explain the asymmetry
Why? Necessity means the predicate is inseparable, so it must hold at all times. But perpetuity only means it does hold at all times; it could be possible for it to cease, hence not necessary.
Refer to al-Razi's comment on the possible not necessarily occurring.
