الاتحاد المعتبر في التناقض (وَالاتِّحَادِ) بالجرّ ؛ عطف على قوله : «الاختلاف»؛ أي: كَمَا لَا بدَّ في تحققِ التَّناقض من الاختلافِ في الأمور الثلاثة المذكورة، وهي: الكَيف ، والكم ، والجِهَةُ ؛ كذلك لا بدَّ في التناقض من الاتحادِ (فِيمَا عَدَاهَا؛ أي: فِيمَا عَدا الكيف والكمّ والجِهَة . فلا بد في التناقض من اختلاف واتّحادٍ ؛ أما الاختلاف، ففي الأمور الثَّلاثة المذكورة، وأمّا الاتحاد، ففيما عداها . واختلف في ذلكَ، فَقِيلَ : يجب الاتحاد في ثمانية أشياء : الموضوع، والمحمول، والزَّمانُ، والمكان، والإضافة، والشَّرطُ، والقوَّة ، والفعل، والجزء، والكلُّ. الدسوقي (قَوْلُهُ : وَالْقُوَّةُ وَالْفِعْلُ) هما واحد كما أنَّ الجزء والكل واحد، والواو فيهما بمعنى أو ) ، أو هما متبادلان؛ أي: القوَّةُ أو الفعلُ، والجزء أو الكل، فأحدهما واحدٌ من الثَّمانية . العطار (قَوْلُهُ : المَوْضُوع) لم يقل : المحكوم عليه ؛ لأنَّه يشملُ الشَّرطيَّاتِ، ولم يذكرها ولأنَّ اعتبار الوحداتِ الثَّمانية لا يظهرُ في الشَّرطيَّاتِ كَمَا نَبَّه عليه العصام. (قَوْلُهُ : وَالزَّمَانُ) اعترض بأنَّهُ يتحقَّقُ التَّناقضُ في مثل قولنا : زيد أب لعمرو أمس، وليسَ بِأَب له اليوم؛ مع عدم وحدةِ الزَّمانِ، وأجيب: بأنَّا لا نُسلّم تحقق التَّناقض فيهِ؛ لأنَّ صدقَ إحداهما وكذبَ الأخرى ليس لذات الاختلاف؛ بل لخصوص المادَّةِ، وذلكَ لأنَّ الأبوَّةَ صِفَةٌ لَو تحقَّقَتْ أمس؛ تَحقَّقَتِ اليوم، وأمَّا مَا يُقالُ: إِنَّ وحدةَ الزَّمانِ تَستلزم وحدةَ المكان ضرورة امتناع أن يكونَ الشَّيءُ في زمانٍ واحدٍ في مكانَيْنِ ؛ فَغَلَط ؛ لأنَّ هَهُنا شَيْئَيْنِ : أَحدُهُما النِّسَبةُ الإيجابيَّةُ، والآخَرُ السَّلبيَّةُ، فَيجوزُ أنْ يكونَا جميعاً فى زمانٍ واحدٍ، ويكونَ كُلٌّ مِنْهُمَا في مكانٍ آخَرَ كَقولِنَا : زيد جالس الآن في المسجدِ، زيد ليس بجالس الآن في السُّوقِ، وَمُحصِّلُهُ : أنَّ المكانَ ظَرفٌ للمحمول، والزَّمانَ ظرفٌ لِلنّسبةِ . (قَوْلُهُ : وَالإِضَافَةُ) هِيَ النّسبةُ المتكرّرَةُ ؛ كَالأبوَّةِ والبنوَّةِ . (قَوْلُهُ: وَالشَّرْطُ) أي : إذا اعتُبِرَ في إحداهما قيدٌ؛ لَا بُدَّ أَنْ يُعتبر ذلك القيد في الأُخرى، فَلَا تَناقُضَ عند الاختلافِ فيهِ، بأن يُعتبر في إحداهما دون الأخرى، أو يُعتَبرَ في كُلِّ منهما شرطٌ مُخالف لشرط الأُخرى. (قَوْلُهُ: وَالْقُوَّةُ وَالْفِعْلُ) قالَ عبد الحكيم: المراد بالقوَّةِ؛ عدم الحصول في زمانِ الحال مع إمكانِهِ لهُ، وبالفعل : الحصول في الحالِ، وَهُمَا غيرُ الإمكانِ
The union considered in contradiction: (and the union) is in the genitive case, coordinated with 'the difference'; i.e., just as it is necessary for the verification of contradiction to have difference in the three mentioned matters—namely, quality, quantity, and direction—so too is it necessary for contradiction to have union in what is other than them; i.e., in what is aside from quality, quantity, and direction. Thus in contradiction there must be difference and union. As for difference, it is in the three mentioned matters; as for union, it is in what is other than them. There is disagreement on that, and it was said: Union must be in eight things: the subject, the predicate, time, place, relation, condition, potentiality and actuality, and part and whole. al-Dasūqī: (His statement: 'and potentiality and actuality') they are one, just as part and whole are one, and the wāw in them is in the sense of 'or', or they are interchangeable; i.e., potentiality or actuality, and part or whole, so each one of them is one of the eight. al-‘Aṭṭār: (His statement: 'the subject') he did not say 'the subject of judgment' because it includes conditional propositions, and he did not mention them, and because consideration of the eight unities is not apparent in conditional propositions as al-‘Iṣām pointed out. (His statement: 'time') It was objected that contradiction is verified in statements like 'Zayd is the father of ‘Amr yesterday and is not his father today' despite absence of unity of time. The answer: we do not concede that contradiction is verified in it, because the truth of one and the falsity of the other is not due to the difference itself but to the specific matter; that is because paternity, if it was realized yesterday, it is realized today. As for what is said: 'unity of time necessitates unity of place necessarily because it is impossible for a thing to be in two places at one time' — it is mistaken, because here there are two things: one is the affirmative relation and the other is the negative relation, so it is permissible for both to be at one time, while each of them is in a different place, as in our statement: 'Zayd is sitting now in the mosque, Zayd is not sitting now in the market.' Its conclusion is that place is a container for the predicate, and time is a container for the relation. (His statement: 'relation') it is the repeated relation like paternity and sonship. (His statement: 'condition') i.e., if a condition is considered in one of them, that same condition must be considered in the other; thus there is no contradiction upon difference in it, meaning that it is considered in one without the other, or in each a condition contrary to the other's condition. (His statement: 'potentiality and actuality') ‘Abd al-Ḥakīm said: By 'potentiality' is meant non-existence at the present time while being possible for it, and by 'actuality' is meant existence at present, and they are other than possibility.
Source pages p. 644

Commentary
This passage is a commentary that defines the conditions for contradiction (tanāquḍ) in logic. It begins by stating that contradiction requires both difference and union: difference in three aspects—quality (ījāb/salab), quantity (kullī/juz’ī), and direction (al-jiha)—and union in everything else. The author then notes disagreement on what union entails, and reports the view that union must hold in eight things: subject, predicate, time, place, relation, condition, potentiality/actuality, and part/whole. al-Dasūqī clarifies that 'potentiality and actuality' count as one item (expressed by 'or'), and similarly 'part and whole' as one, so the total is eight. al-‘Aṭṭār then explains each item: 'subject' is used instead of 'subject of judgment' to include conditional propositions, though the eight unities are not apparent in conditionals. Regarding 'time', an objection is raised: statements like 'Zayd is the father of ‘Amr yesterday, and is not his father today' seem to be a contradiction despite different times. The answer is that this is not true contradiction because the truth/falsity is due to the specific matter (paternity persists) not to the logical form. Another mistaken claim is that unity of time necessitates unity of place; this is false because the two propositions (affirmative and negative) can coexist at one time in different places, e.g., 'Zayd is sitting now in the mosque' and 'Zayd is not sitting now in the market' are not contradictory because place is a container of the predicate, not the relation. 'Relation' means a repeated relationship like paternity. 'Condition' means that if a condition is attached to one proposition, the same condition must apply to the other for contradiction to occur. 'Potentiality and actuality' are defined by ‘Abd al-Ḥakīm: potentiality is the non-existence of something at present while it is possible, actuality is its existence at present; these are distinct from mere possibility. This commentary guards against over-simplifying contradiction as mere difference in quality, emphasizing the subtlety of the required unions.

A) Because the time differs
B) Because paternity is a persistent property, making the falsity due to matter not form
C) Because the subject differs
D) Because the quantity differs

Key Takeaway
When assessing contradiction, one must distinguish between logical form and material content; persistent attributes can make time difference irrelevant to the contradiction check.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider: 'The Quran is created' and 'The Quran is not created in time.' Are these contradictory? Apply the eight unions, especially time and condition, and explain.
?

The Eight Conditions for True Contradiction
Author lists the eight units of unity required for true contradiction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which of the eight units of contradiction is missing in a pair of propositions and determine whether they truly contradict.

Core Idea
Contradiction requires unity in eight aspects—subject, predicate, time, place, relation, condition, potentiality, and part/whole.

In logic, two propositions contradict when one must be true and the other false. But if they differ in any of eight key aspects, they can both be true and thus are not contradictory. This lesson walks you through these eight 'units' (waḥdāt) that must be identical for contradiction to hold.

Guided Path
1
Recall the definition of contradiction
Two propositions contradict if they cannot both be true and cannot both be false at the same time. This requires unity in all relevant aspects.
2
Understand the principle behind the eight units
If any of the eight aspects—subject, predicate, time, place, relation, condition, potentiality/actuality, part/whole—differs between two propositions, they may both be true and thus are not contradictory.
3
Work through each of the eight examples
For each of the eight, see how the difference allows both propositions to be true. For example, 'Zayd is standing' and 'Amr is not standing' differ in subject, so both can be true (Zayd standing, Amr not). The same principle applies for each unit.
Make sure to internalize why each difference prevents contradiction.
4
Test your understanding with the provided examples
Practice by identifying which unit is violated in given pairs. Use the examples in the passage as a starting point.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فلا يناقض : ت المنطقية. ١. «زيد قائم»، «عمرو ليس بقائم»؛ لاختلاف الموضوع. ٢. ولا «زيد قائم»، «زيد ليس بقاعد»؛ لاختلاف المحمول. ٣. ولا «زيد قائم»؛ أي: ليلاً، «زيد ليس بقائم»؛ أي: نهاراً؛ لاختلاف الزمان. ٤. ولا «زيد قائم»؛ أي: في المسجد، «زيد ليس بقائم»؛ أي: في السوق؛ لاختلاف المكان. ٥. ولا «زيد أب»؛ أي: لبكر، «زيد ليس بأب»؛ أي: لعمرو؛ لاختلاف الإضافة. ٦. ولا «الجسم مفرق للبصر»؛ أي: بشرط كونه أبيض، «الجسم ليس بمفرق للبصر»؛ أي: بشرط كونه أسود؛ لاختلاف الشرط. ولا الخمر في الدن مسكر»؛ أي: بالقوة، «الخمر في الدن ليس مسكر»؛ أي: بالفعل؛ لاختلاف القوة والفعل. ولا «الزنجي أسود»؛ أي: بعضه، «الزنجي ليس بأسود»؛ أي: كله؛ لاختلاف الجزء والكل. فهذه الوحدات الثمانية التي ذكرها القدماء في تحقق التناقض.

(قوله: فهذه الوحدات الثمانية) قال العصام: إنما ذكروها مع أن تعريف التناقض يتكفل بتمييزه عما عداه؛ لأنه كثيراً ما يعرض الغلط للمتعلم من مشاهدة الاختلاف بين القضيتين؛ فيظنه موجباً للتناقض لعدم تنبهه لإضمار ما أخرج الاختلاف من الاقتضاء المذكور في التعريف، إما بإخراجه عن أصل التناقض، أو الاختلاف لذاته، فذكروا عدة من الأمور العارضة للاختلاف؛ تمكيناً للمتعلم في مقام التنبيه وتمييزاً له في التفحص عن تحقق الاختلاف المذكور، ولم يستوفوا بيان ما يعرض من تكثير الوحدات التي يشترطونها؛ لأنها مما لا يعد ولا يحصى، فأحالوها على فطنة المتعلم بعد تقويتها بهذا المقدار من التنبيه، وبهذا؛ اندفع ما ذكره العلامة التفتازاني من أن الاختلاف يكون بغير الأمور المذكورة، وظهر أن الرد إلى الوحدتين إخلال بما هو الغرض من تفصيل الوحدات الثمانية، والرد إلى وحدة النسبة مبالغة في الإخلال، هكذا حقق المقام؛ فإنه من مواهب الحكيم العلام.
So it not contradict: the logical [examples]. 1. 'Zayd is standing', 'Amr is not standing'—due to difference of subject. 2. And not 'Zayd is standing', 'Zayd is not sitting'—due to difference of predicate. 3. And not 'Zayd is standing' meaning at night, 'Zayd is not standing' meaning during the day—due to difference of time. 4. And not 'Zayd is standing' meaning in the mosque, 'Zayd is not standing' meaning in the market—due to difference of place. 5. And not 'Zayd is a father' meaning of Bakr, 'Zayd is not a father' meaning of Amr—due to difference of relation. 6. And not 'The body is vision-dispersing' meaning on condition of being white, 'The body is not vision-dispersing' meaning on condition of being black—due to difference of condition. And not 'The wine in the jar is intoxicating' meaning in potentiality, 'The wine in the jar is not intoxicating' meaning in actuality—due to difference of potentiality and actuality. And not 'The Zanjī is black' meaning some of him, 'The Zanjī is not black' meaning all of him—due to difference of part and whole. These are the eight units that the early scholars mentioned in the realization of contradiction.

(His saying: These are the eight units) Al-ʿIṣām said: They only mentioned them even though the definition of contradiction suffices to distinguish it from other things; because often the learner is mistaken by observing difference between two propositions and thinks it necessitates contradiction due to not being alert to what the difference excludes from the requirement mentioned in the definition, either by excluding it from the essence of contradiction, or difference in itself. So they mentioned a number of incidental matters of difference; to enable the learner in the station of alerting and distinguishing for him in scrutinizing the realization of the mentioned difference. And they did not exhaust the explanation of what occurs from multiplying the units they stipulate; because it is not countable nor limited, so they referred it to the learner's acumen after strengthening it by this amount of alerting. And by this is repelled what the scholar al-Taftāzānī mentioned that difference occurs by other than the mentioned matters. And it became clear that reducing to two units is a deficiency in what is the purpose of detailing the eight units, and reducing to the unity of the relation is an exaggeration in deficiency. Thus the matter has been verified; indeed it is from the gifts of the wise scholar.
Source pages p. 646

Commentary
This passage defines the conditions for true logical contradiction (tanāquḍ) by listing eight 'units' (waḥdāt) that must be identical between two propositions for them to truly contradict. The author begins by stating that contradiction does not occur when any of these units differ, providing examples for each. First, difference in subject: 'Zayd is standing' and 'Amr is not standing' are not contradictory because the subject changes. Second, difference in predicate: 'Zayd is standing' and 'Zayd is not sitting' are not contradictory because the predicate changes. Third, difference in time: 'Zayd is standing at night' and 'Zayd is not standing during the day'—differing time prevents contradiction. Fourth, difference in place: same action in mosque vs. market. Fifth, difference in relation: being a father of one person vs. not being father of another. Sixth, difference in condition: the body being vision-dispersing under condition of whiteness vs. not under blackness. Seventh, difference between potentiality and actuality: wine in the jar potentially intoxicating vs. not actually intoxicating. Eighth, difference between part and whole: the Zanjī being black in part vs. not black in his entirety. The passage then states these are the eight units mentioned by the early logicians. The commentary from al-ʿIṣām explains why the scholars listed these specific units even though the definition of contradiction itself implies these conditions: learners often mistakenly think any difference between propositions shows contradiction, so these examples train the mind to identify the precise unity required. The commentary also refutes al-Taftāzānī's objection that difference can occur in ways besides these eight, arguing that these eight cover the principal categories and further details are left to the learner's acumen. The reduction to only two units (as some proposed) is rejected as insufficient for the purpose of clear teaching.

Key Terms
تناقض
وحدات
الجزء والكل
القوة والفعل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following pairs of propositions would be a genuine logical contradiction? (Assume no other differences.)
A) 'Zayd is standing in the mosque' and 'Zayd is not standing in the market'
B) 'Zayd is standing at night' and 'Zayd is not standing during the day'
C) 'Zayd is standing' and 'Zayd is not standing' (same time, same place, etc.)
D) 'Zayd is a father of Bakr' and 'Zayd is not a father of Amr'

Key Takeaway
For two propositions to contradict, they must be identical in all eight units; difference in any one unit removes the contradiction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the pair: 'The Quran is created' (at time A) and 'The Quran is not created' (at time B). Identify which of the eight units differs and explain why they do not truly contradict.

The Pedagogical Purpose of the Eight Units and the Debate with al-Taftāzānī
Commentator defends the eight units and refutes al-Taftāzānī's objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain al-ʿIṣām's defense of the eight units against al-Taftāzānī's objection that other differences can prevent contradiction.

Core Idea
The eight units are not exhaustive but sufficient for training; reducing them loses pedagogical clarity.

The commentary by al-ʿIṣām addresses why the early logicians listed exactly these eight units even though other differences could exist. He also responds to al-Taftāzānī's criticism that the list is incomplete. This lesson unpacks that discussion.

Guided Path
1
Understand the purpose of the eight units
The eight units are not meant to be exhaustive but to train the learner's mind to look for specific aspects of unity. They cover the principal categories of difference.
Al-ʿIṣām calls this 'tanbīh' (alerting) and 'tamyīz' (distinguishing).
2
Identify al-Taftāzānī's objection
Al-Taftāzānī argued that difference can occur in ways beyond these eight, implying the list is too limited.
This objection is mentioned in the commentary.
3
Analyze al-ʿIṣām's response
Al-ʿIṣām replies that the early scholars left further details to the learner's acumen; the eight are sufficient for initial training. Reducing to only two units (subject and predicate) would defeat the pedagogical purpose.
4
Evaluate the debate
Consider which position is stronger. Does reducing to two units sacrifice clarity? Or is Taftāzānī's concern about completeness valid?

فلا يناقض : ت المنطقية. ١. «زيد قائم»، «عمرو ليس بقائم»؛ لاختلاف الموضوع. ٢. ولا «زيد قائم»، «زيد ليس بقاعد»؛ لاختلاف المحمول. ٣. ولا «زيد قائم»؛ أي: ليلاً، «زيد ليس بقائم»؛ أي: نهاراً؛ لاختلاف الزمان. ٤. ولا «زيد قائم»؛ أي: في المسجد، «زيد ليس بقائم»؛ أي: في السوق؛ لاختلاف المكان. ٥. ولا «زيد أب»؛ أي: لبكر، «زيد ليس بأب»؛ أي: لعمرو؛ لاختلاف الإضافة. ٦. ولا «الجسم مفرق للبصر»؛ أي: بشرط كونه أبيض، «الجسم ليس بمفرق للبصر»؛ أي: بشرط كونه أسود؛ لاختلاف الشرط. ولا الخمر في الدن مسكر»؛ أي: بالقوة، «الخمر في الدن ليس مسكر»؛ أي: بالفعل؛ لاختلاف القوة والفعل. ولا «الزنجي أسود»؛ أي: بعضه، «الزنجي ليس بأسود»؛ أي: كله؛ لاختلاف الجزء والكل. فهذه الوحدات الثمانية التي ذكرها القدماء في تحقق التناقض.

(قوله: فهذه الوحدات الثمانية) قال العصام: إنما ذكروها مع أن تعريف التناقض يتكفل بتمييزه عما عداه؛ لأنه كثيراً ما يعرض الغلط للمتعلم من مشاهدة الاختلاف بين القضيتين؛ فيظنه موجباً للتناقض لعدم تنبهه لإضمار ما أخرج الاختلاف من الاقتضاء المذكور في التعريف، إما بإخراجه عن أصل التناقض، أو الاختلاف لذاته، فذكروا عدة من الأمور العارضة للاختلاف؛ تمكيناً للمتعلم في مقام التنبيه وتمييزاً له في التفحص عن تحقق الاختلاف المذكور، ولم يستوفوا بيان ما يعرض من تكثير الوحدات التي يشترطونها؛ لأنها مما لا يعد ولا يحصى، فأحالوها على فطنة المتعلم بعد تقويتها بهذا المقدار من التنبيه، وبهذا؛ اندفع ما ذكره العلامة التفتازاني من أن الاختلاف يكون بغير الأمور المذكورة، وظهر أن الرد إلى الوحدتين إخلال بما هو الغرض من تفصيل الوحدات الثمانية، والرد إلى وحدة النسبة مبالغة في الإخلال، هكذا حقق المقام؛ فإنه من مواهب الحكيم العلام.
So it not contradict: the logical [examples]. 1. 'Zayd is standing', 'Amr is not standing'—due to difference of subject. 2. And not 'Zayd is standing', 'Zayd is not sitting'—due to difference of predicate. 3. And not 'Zayd is standing' meaning at night, 'Zayd is not standing' meaning during the day—due to difference of time. 4. And not 'Zayd is standing' meaning in the mosque, 'Zayd is not standing' meaning in the market—due to difference of place. 5. And not 'Zayd is a father' meaning of Bakr, 'Zayd is not a father' meaning of Amr—due to difference of relation. 6. And not 'The body is vision-dispersing' meaning on condition of being white, 'The body is not vision-dispersing' meaning on condition of being black—due to difference of condition. And not 'The wine in the jar is intoxicating' meaning in potentiality, 'The wine in the jar is not intoxicating' meaning in actuality—due to difference of potentiality and actuality. And not 'The Zanjī is black' meaning some of him, 'The Zanjī is not black' meaning all of him—due to difference of part and whole. These are the eight units that the early scholars mentioned in the realization of contradiction.

(His saying: These are the eight units) Al-ʿIṣām said: They only mentioned them even though the definition of contradiction suffices to distinguish it from other things; because often the learner is mistaken by observing difference between two propositions and thinks it necessitates contradiction due to not being alert to what the difference excludes from the requirement mentioned in the definition, either by excluding it from the essence of contradiction, or difference in itself. So they mentioned a number of incidental matters of difference; to enable the learner in the station of alerting and distinguishing for him in scrutinizing the realization of the mentioned difference. And they did not exhaust the explanation of what occurs from multiplying the units they stipulate; because it is not countable nor limited, so they referred it to the learner's acumen after strengthening it by this amount of alerting. And by this is repelled what the scholar al-Taftāzānī mentioned that difference occurs by other than the mentioned matters. And it became clear that reducing to two units is a deficiency in what is the purpose of detailing the eight units, and reducing to the unity of the relation is an exaggeration in deficiency. Thus the matter has been verified; indeed it is from the gifts of the wise scholar.
Source pages p. 646

Commentary
This passage defines the conditions for true logical contradiction (tanāquḍ) by listing eight 'units' (waḥdāt) that must be identical between two propositions for them to truly contradict. The author begins by stating that contradiction does not occur when any of these units differ, providing examples for each. First, difference in subject: 'Zayd is standing' and 'Amr is not standing' are not contradictory because the subject changes. Second, difference in predicate: 'Zayd is standing' and 'Zayd is not sitting' are not contradictory because the predicate changes. Third, difference in time: 'Zayd is standing at night' and 'Zayd is not standing during the day'—differing time prevents contradiction. Fourth, difference in place: same action in mosque vs. market. Fifth, difference in relation: being a father of one person vs. not being father of another. Sixth, difference in condition: the body being vision-dispersing under condition of whiteness vs. not under blackness. Seventh, difference between potentiality and actuality: wine in the jar potentially intoxicating vs. not actually intoxicating. Eighth, difference between part and whole: the Zanjī being black in part vs. not black in his entirety. The passage then states these are the eight units mentioned by the early logicians. The commentary from al-ʿIṣām explains why the scholars listed these specific units even though the definition of contradiction itself implies these conditions: learners often mistakenly think any difference between propositions shows contradiction, so these examples train the mind to identify the precise unity required. The commentary also refutes al-Taftāzānī's objection that difference can occur in ways besides these eight, arguing that these eight cover the principal categories and further details are left to the learner's acumen. The reduction to only two units (as some proposed) is rejected as insufficient for the purpose of clear teaching.

The eight units are a pedagogical tool, not an exhaustive list; they train the learner to spot the most common differences that prevent contradiction.

The Later Scholars' Simplification of Contradiction Conditions
Author presents the later scholars' simplification of contradiction conditions to subject and predicate unity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which of the eight units for contradiction are subsumed under subject and predicate, and evaluate whether a given pair of propositions is contradictory using the two-unit rule.

Core Idea
Later logicians reduced eight contradiction conditions to two: subject and predicate unity.

The classical logicians required eight units (subject, predicate, time, place, condition, part/whole, etc.) for two propositions to be contradictory. The later scholars (al-muta'akhkhirūn) simplified this by showing that the extra six are not independent but are always attached to either the subject or the predicate. This lesson will help you apply this simplification and avoid common misunderstandings.

Guided Path
1
Recall the eight units of contradiction
Earlier logicians required identity in subject, predicate, time, place, condition, part/whole, etc., for two propositions to be contradictory. List them to understand what is being simplified.
The exact number may vary, but eight is standard in the tradition.
2
Identify the two essential units
The later view holds that only unity of subject and unity of predicate are necessary. The remaining units are not independent; they are subsumed under subject or predicate.
This is a major simplification that makes contradiction checking more flexible.
3
Analyze how other units attach
Using the examples: time can be part of the predicate (e.g., 'Zayd is standing at night') or part of the subject (e.g., 'The one standing at night is Zayd'). Similarly for condition, part/whole, etc. Determine which component the additional element belongs to.
Al-Dasūqī notes that the eight units entirely revert to either subject or predicate.
4
Test with new examples
Given a pair of propositions, check if subject and predicate are identical. If yes, they are contradictory even if other conditions differ, as long as those conditions are part of subject or predicate consistently.
If the additional condition is attached differently (e.g., one has 'at night' in the predicate, the other in the subject), the propositions may not be directly contradictory without further analysis.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَأَمَّا عِنْدَ الْمُتَأَخِّرِينَ فَيَكْفِي وَحْدَتَانِ: وَحْدَةُ الْمَوْضُوعِ، وَوَحْدَةُ الْمَحْمُوْلِ، وَالْوَحْدَاتُ الْبَاقِيَةُ مُنْدَرِجَةً فِيهِمَا. قَالَ الدَّسُوقِيُّ: الْأَوْلَى عَدَمُ التَّقْيِيْدِ؛ إِذْ هَذِهِ الْوَحْدَاتُ الثَّمَانِيَةُ قَدْ تَرْجِعُ إِلَى الْمَحْمُوْلِ بِتَمَامِهَا، وَقَدْ تَرْجِعُ إِلَى الْمَوْضُوعِ، يَرْجِعُ مَا ذُكِرَ أَنَّهُ مُنْدَرِجُ فِي وَحْدَةِ الْمَوْضُوعِ لِوَحْدَةِ الْمَحْمُوْلِ، وَمَا ذُكِرَ أَنَّهُ مُنْدَرِجُ فِي وَحْدَةِ الْمَحْمُوْلِ لِوَحْدَةِ الْمَوْضُوعِ، مَثَلًا: زَيْدٌ قَائِمٌ لَيْلًا، زَيْدٌ لَيْسَ بِقَائِمٍ لَيْلًا، وَحْدَةُ الزَّمَانِ فِيْهِ تَرْجِعُ لِوَحْدَةِ الْمَحْمُوْلِ، وَنَحْوُ الْقَائِمِ لَيْلًا زَيْدٌ، لَيْسَ الْقَائِمُ لَيْلًا زَيْدًا، وَحْدَةُ الزَّمَانِ فِيْهِ تَرْجِعُ إِلَى وَحْدَةِ الْمَوْضُوعِ، وَكَذَا وَحْدَةُ الشَّرْطِ، وَوَحْدَةُ الْجُزْءِ أَوِ الْكُلِّ.
As for the later scholars, two units suffice: unity of subject and unity of predicate, and the remaining units are subsumed under them. Al-Dasūqī said: The first [point] is not to restrict; for these eight units may entirely revert to the predicate, and may revert to the subject. What is mentioned as being subsumed under unity of subject reverts to unity of predicate, and what is mentioned as subsumed under unity of predicate reverts to unity of subject. For example: 'Zayd is standing at night' and 'Zayd is not standing at night' – here unity of time reverts to the predicate. And similarly, 'The one standing at night is Zayd' and 'The one standing at night is not Zayd' – unity of time reverts to the subject. And similarly unity of condition, and unity of part or whole.
Source pages p. 648

Commentary
This passage presents the position of later logicians (al-muta'akhkhirūn) who simplify the eight units required for contradiction to only two: unity of subject and unity of predicate. The commentator al-Dasūqī elaborates that the other units (time, place, condition, part/whole, etc.) are not independent but are subsumed under subject or predicate depending on how the proposition is phrased. For example, time can be part of the predicate (e.g., 'Zayd is standing at night') or part of the subject (e.g., 'The one standing at night is Zayd'). This refinement shows a shift from the earlier exhaustive list to a more principle-based approach. The examples illustrate this flexibility.

وحدة المحمول

B) 'The one standing at night is Zayd' and 'The one standing at night is not Zayd' (time in subject).
C) Both A and B can be contradictory if the time reference is identical.

Key Takeaway
The later view reduces the conditions for contradiction to subject and predicate unity; all other units are subsumed under these.

Resolving Complex Conditions for Contradiction
Author gives examples of reducing compound conditions to simple subject-predicate form

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reduce any compound condition in a proposition to achieve unity of subject and predicate, ensuring proper contradiction pairs.

Core Idea
Hidden conditions break contradiction; make every qualifier explicit.

In logic, two propositions can only be true contradictories if they share the exact same subject and predicate in every respect. Often, conditions like place, relation, or potentiality are implied but not stated. This lesson teaches you to identify and extract those hidden conditions, turning ambiguous pairs into proper contradictions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the hidden condition
In each pair, look for words like 'meaning in X' or 'meaning potentially' that qualify the predicate. These are the conditions that must be made explicit.
2
Incorporate the condition into the predicate
Rephrase the proposition so that the condition becomes an integral part of the predicate, not a separate implication. For example, 'Zaid is sitting in the mosque' instead of 'Zaid is sitting, meaning in the mosque.'
3
Verify that both contradictory propositions include the same condition
Ensure that the positive and negative statements both contain the exact same qualified predicate. Only then can they serve as true contradictories.
4
Apply to any domain
This method works for any hidden condition: place, time, relation, potentiality, actuality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويرجعُ قولنا : زيد جالس، وتريد في المسجد، زيد ليس بجالس، وتريد في المسجد، إلى قولنا : زيد جالس في المسجدِ، زيد ليس بجالس في المسجد. ويرجعُ قولنا : زيد أب، وتريد لعمرو، زيد ليس بأَب، وتريد لعمرو، إلى قولنا : زيد أب لعمرو، زيد ليس بأَب لعمرو . ويرجع قولنا : الخمرُ في الدَّنّ مُسكِرٌ، وتريد بالقوَّةِ، الخمر في الدن ليس يمسكر، وتريد بالقوَّة إلى قولنا : الخمرُ في الدَّنّ مُسكر بالقوَّةِ، الخمر في ليسَ بِمُسكر بالقوة. ويرجعُ قولنا : الخمرُ في الدَّنَّ مُسكر، تريد بالفعل، الخمر في بمسكر، وتريد بالفعل، إلى قولنا : الخمرُ في الدَّنَّ مُسكر بالفعل، الخمرُ في ليسَ بِمُسكر بالفعل .
And our statement: 'Zaid is sitting,' meaning in the mosque, and 'Zaid is not sitting,' meaning in the mosque, is reduced to our statement: 'Zaid is sitting in the mosque,' 'Zaid is not sitting in the mosque.' And our statement: 'Zaid is a father,' meaning of 'Amr, and 'Zaid is not a father,' meaning of 'Amr, is reduced to our statement: 'Zaid is a father of 'Amr,' 'Zaid is not a father of 'Amr.' And our statement: 'The wine in the jar is intoxicating,' meaning potentially, and 'The wine in the jar is not intoxicating,' meaning potentially, is reduced to our statement: 'The wine in the jar is potentially intoxicating,' 'The wine in the jar is not potentially intoxicating.' And our statement: 'The wine in the jar is intoxicating,' meaning actually, and 'The wine in the jar is not intoxicating,' meaning actually, is reduced to our statement: 'The wine in the jar is actually intoxicating,' 'The wine in the jar is not actually intoxicating.'
Source pages p. 650

Commentary
This passage illustrates how to resolve complex propositions into simple ones to ensure the unity of subject and predicate, a necessary condition for contradiction. The author gives four examples: (1) 'Zaid is sitting' and 'Zaid is not sitting,' both qualified by the place 'in the mosque,' are reduced to a single proposition with a unified predicate: 'Zaid is sitting in the mosque' and its negation. (2) 'Zaid is a father' and 'Zaid is not a father,' qualified by 'of Amr,' are reduced to 'Zaid is a father of Amr' and its negation. (3) 'The wine in the jar is intoxicating' qualified by 'potentially' is similarly reduced. (4) The same with 'actually.' The key point is that when a condition (like place, relation, potentiality, actuality) implicitly qualifies either the subject or the predicate, we must make it explicit so that the two contradictory propositions have exactly the same subject and predicate in all respects. This guards against apparent contradictions where the conditions differ.

The Inversion Argument Against Fixed Condition Assignment
Critique of attributing conditions to subject vs predicate

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify how the inversion of a proposition (al-'aks) reveals the arbitrariness of attributing specific unity conditions to the subject versus the predicate.

Core Idea
If you flip a proposition, the conditions flip too — so the assignment of conditions is not absolute.

In Islamic logic, the conditions for contradiction include unity of subject, predicate, time, place, etc. Some logicians assigned certain conditions (like condition, part, whole) to the subject, and others (time, place, relation) to the predicate. But as the author of Sharh al-Risala points out, this division collapses when you invert the proposition.

Guided Path
1
Extract the criticized division
Identify which conditions are attributed to the subject (shart, kull, juz') and which to the predicate (zaman, makan, idafa, quwwa, fi'l) according to the view being critiqued.
This division is found in earlier logical works.
2
Apply inversion to a sample proposition
Take a proposition like 'Zayd is sitting in the mosque' (with place condition in the predicate). Convert it to 'A sitter in the mosque is Zayd'. Notice where the place condition now appears.
Inversion (al-'aks) is a valid immediate inference.
3
Observe the reversal
In the inverted proposition, the place condition is now part of the subject. Thus the original assignment (place to predicate) is not preserved. The same would happen for other conditions.
This shows the division is not absolute.
4
Formulate the author's conclusion
The author concludes that it is better to attribute all conditions to both subject and predicate without specification, because the division fails under inversion.
Al-Sayyid defends the original division as more intuitive, but the logical objection stands.

قال المصنِّفُ في شرح الرِّسالةِ : وَهَهُنَا نظر، وهو أن جعل وحدةَ الشَّرط والجزء والكُلِّ راجعة إلى وحدة الموضوع، والبواقي إلى وحدة المحمول؛ مِمَّا لا يصح على إطلاقِهِ؛ لأنَّهُ إذا عكشتَ القضايا المذكورة انعكس الأمرُ، وصارت وحدةُ الشَّرط والجزء والكُلِّ راجعة إلى المحمول، والبواقي إلى الموضوع، فالأولى القولُ برجوع جميع الوحدات إلى وحدة الموضوع والمحمول من غير تخصيص. وأجاب السيد : بأنَّ المخصص كأنَّهُ راعَى مَا هو الظَّاهِرُ مِن أنَّ رجوع وحدةِ الشَّرطِ وَوحدةِ الكُلِّ والجزء إلى وحدة الموضوع، ورجوع البواقي إلى وحدة المحمول أظهرُ؛ لأنَّ اعتبار الشَّرطِ والكُلِّ والجزء في الموضوع، واعتبار الزَّمانِ والمكان والإضافةِ والقوَّةِ والفعل في المحمول أنسبُ. وفي شرح المطالع : لا يُقالُ : الزَّمانُ خارج عن طَرفَي القضيَّة؛ لأنَّ نسبة المحمول إلى الموضوع لا بُدَّ لها مِن زمانٍ، فَلَو كَانَ الزَّمانُ داخلاً في المحمول؛ لَكَانَ نسبةُ ذلك المحمول إلى الموضوع واقعةً في زمان، فيكونُ للزَّمانِ زمانُ آخَر، ولأنَّ تعلَّقَ الزَّمانِ بالقضيَّةِ بحسب ظرفيَّةِ النِّسبةِ والشَّيء لا يصيرُ ظرفاً لآخر إلا بعدَ تَحقُّقِهِ، فيكونُ تعلُّق الزَّمانِ مُتأخّراً عن النِّسبةِ المتأخّرةِ عَن طَرفَي القضيَّة، فلو كان داخلاً فى أحدهما؛ لَكانَ مُتأخّراً عن نفسه بمراتب، وإِنَّهُ محال لأَنَّا نقولُ : تعلُّق المكان أيضاً بحسب الظرفية، إذ لا بُدَّ لِلنِّسبة مِن مكانٍ، كَمَا لا بُدَّ لها من زمان، فلا وجه لاندراج وحدة المكان تحتَ وحدة الموضوع وإخراج وحدة الزَّمانِ عنها. ومُحصِّلُ الجوابِ معارضةٌ لِلسؤالِ السَّائلِ أَنَّهُ على تقديرِ لزومِ الزَّمانِ لِلزَّمانِ يلزمُ أيضاً أن يكون للمكان مكان آخَرُ بالدَّليل المذكور، ثمَّ هذا مبني على القول بأنَّ الزَّمانَ مَوجودٌ، وأنَّهُ مِقدارُ الحركةِ كَما هو رأي الحكماء، أما على مَذهبِ المتكلّمينَ مِن أَنَّهُ أمر موهوم اعتباريُّ، فَلَا مانع أَنْ يَكُونَ لِلزَّمانِ زمانٌ؛ إِذْ لَا حَجْرَ في الاعتباريَّاتِ والوهميَّاتِ.
The author said in Sharh al-Risala: And here there is a consideration: that making the unity of condition, part, and whole return to the unity of the subject, and the rest to the unity of the predicate, is not absolutely valid; because if you invert the mentioned propositions, the matter is reversed, and the unity of condition, part, and whole returns to the predicate, and the rest to the subject. So the better view is to say that all unities return to the unity of subject and predicate without specification. And al-Sayyid responded: The specifier seems to have observed what is apparent: that returning the unity of condition, whole, and part to the unity of the subject, and the rest to the unity of the predicate, is more evident; because considering condition, whole, and part in the subject, and considering time, place, relation, potentiality, and actuality in the predicate, is more appropriate. And in Sharh al-Mutala': It cannot be said that time is outside the two parts of the proposition; because the relation of predicate to subject must have a time. If time were included in the predicate, then the predicate would be related to the subject at a time, resulting in a time for time. Also, the attachment of time to the proposition is in terms of the relativity of the relation, and a thing does not become a container for another except after its realization, so the attachment of time is subsequent to the relation which is subsequent to the two parts. If it were included in one of them, it would be subsequent to itself by degrees, which is impossible. We say: the attachment of place is also in terms of relativity, for the relation must have a place, just as it must have a time, so there is no reason to include unity of place under the subject and exclude unity of time from it. The summary of the answer is a counterargument to the questioner's objection: on the assumption that time requires time, likewise place would require another place by the same proof. Moreover, this is based on the view that time is existent and is the measure of motion, as the philosophers hold. As for the theologians' view that it is an imaginary conceptual matter, there is no obstacle to time having a time, since there is no restriction in conceptual and imaginary matters.
Source pages p. 650

Commentary
This passage critically examines the view that certain conditions (like condition, part, whole) are attributed to the unity of the subject, while others (time, place, relation, potentiality, actuality) are attributed to the unity of the predicate. The author of Sharh al-Risala notes that this division is not absolute: if you invert the propositions (e.g., convert 'Zaid is sitting in the mosque' to 'A sitter in the mosque is Zaid'), the condition of place shifts from predicate to subject, showing the arbitrariness. So he suggests that all conditions should be considered together without specific assignment. Al-Sayyid defends the original division as more intuitive. Then a deeper critique from Sharh al-Mutala' argues that time cannot be part of the predicate because then the predicate's relation to the subject would occur in time, leading to an infinite regress of times. The same argument applies to place if it is considered part of the subject. The response counters by pointing out that if time cannot be part of the predicate, then place also cannot be part of the subject, so both should be external. The discussion then notes that this objection rests on the philosophers' view that time is real; on the theologians' view that time is conceptual, the regress is harmless. This shows the depth of debate within Islamic logic about the precise conditions for contradiction.

كل وجزء
ظرفية
إضافة

A) An infinite regress argument
B) A counterexample with a different school of thought
C) The inversion of propositions
D) An appeal to theological views on time

Key Takeaway
The inversion of propositions demonstrates that the assignment of unity conditions to subject versus predicate is not fixed, undermining the rigid division.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take a proposition like 'Zayd is a father of Amr before 2020' (condition of relation and time). Invert it to 'A father of Amr before 2020 is Zayd'. Identify how the conditions shift from predicate to subject. Does this support the author's critique?

Construct your own proposition with a condition of whole (kull) in the subject, e.g., 'Every human is rational'. Invert it to 'Some rational is human'. Notice what happens to the condition of whole. Does the inversion preserve the attribution?

The Infinite Regress Argument: Time and Place in Propositions
Infinite regress argument about time and place in propositions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the argument that including time in the predicate leads to an infinite regress, and evaluate how this depends on metaphysical assumptions about the reality of time.

Core Idea
If time were in the predicate, it would need another time — a regress that the theologians avoid by treating time as conceptual.

A deeper argument from Sharh al-Mutala' shows that including time as a condition in the predicate leads to a vicious infinite regress. But the same reasoning applies to place if included in the subject. The response of the commentator highlights that this problem only arises if time is considered a real entity; on the theologians' view of time as conceptual, the regress is harmless.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premise about time and relation
The relation of predicate to subject must occur in some time. So every proposition has a time condition attached to the relation.
This is a standard view in logic: time is required for the truth of a proposition.
2
Assume time is included in the predicate
If the time condition is part of the predicate, then the predicate itself contains a reference to time. For example, 'Zayd is sitting at noon' has 'at noon' as part of the predicate.
This is the view that attributes time to the predicate.
3
Trace the infinite regress
The predicate 'sitting at noon' relates to the subject Zayd. But that relation itself must occur at some time. If 'at noon' is already in the predicate, then the relation needs another time, leading to an infinite series of times.
The argument is: if time is in the predicate, time requires another time, ad infinitum.
4
Draw the conclusion and extend to place
Since infinite regress is impossible (tasalsul muhal), time cannot be in the predicate. The same reasoning applies if place is in the subject: the relation requires a place, leading to a similar regress.
The response in Sharh al-Mutala' notes the parallel for place, showing both must be external.
5
Consider the metaphysical escape
The regress assumes time is real (as philosophers hold). But the theologians view time as an imaginary conceptual measure (amr wahmi i'tibari). If time is conceptual, then conceptual time can have conceptual time without a real regress.
Thus the validity of the regress argument depends on the nature of time.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The Eight Unities Required for Logical Contradiction
Author explains the eight conditions and the consequence of any difference.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the eight unities that must be identical for two propositions to be contradictory and identify when a difference in one condition negates contradiction.

Core Idea
Contradiction requires unity in subject, predicate, time, place, condition, relation, part, and power.

In Islamic logic, two propositions are truly contradictory only if they are exactly the same in all aspects except truth value. This lesson explores the eight conditions (al-waḥdāt al-thamāniya) that must be unified. Understanding these conditions prevents false claims of contradiction in theological and juristic debates.

Guided Path
1
Recall the definition of contradiction
Two propositions that cannot both be true and cannot both be false simultaneously. This relation requires that they be identical in all relevant respects except truth value.
If any condition differs, the propositions refer to different judgments, so they could both be true or both false.
2
Understand why unity is required
If any of the eight unities is missing, the relation (nisba) changes, and the propositions no longer negate each other. The logicians restrict the conditions to eight; otherwise any difference could lift contradiction.
The passage states: 'lack of unity of any of the unities entails difference of relation.'
3
Memorize the eight unities
The classical list includes: subject (mawḍū‘), predicate (maḥmūl), time (zamān), place (makān), condition (sharṭ), relation (iḍāfa), part (juz’), and power (quwwa). Some versions vary slightly.
These ensure that the two statements are about exactly the same fact.
4
Apply to an example
Take 'Zaid is writing' and 'Zaid is not writing'. If 'writing' refers to the same pen, same time, same place, etc., they are contradictory. But if the instrument differs (e.g., Wasiti pen vs Turkish pen), they can both be true.
This shows how a single difference avoids contradiction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثَّمانية، وعدم وحدة شيء من الوحدات يستلزم اختلاف النّسبةِ . وإلا فلا حصر فيما ذكروه؛ لارتفاع التناقض باختلافِ : فَصْلٌ: فِي التَّنَاقُضِ : ١. الآلة، نحو: «زيد كاتب»؛ أي: بالقلم الواسطي، «زيد ليس بكاتب»؛ أي : بالقلم التركي. ٢. والعلَّةِ نحو النَّجَّار عامل ؛ أي : للسلطان النَّجّار ليس بعامل»؛ أي: لغيره. ٣. والمفعول به نحو زيدٌ ضاربٌ ؛ أي: عَمْرواً، زيدٌ ليس بضارب»؛ أي: بكراً . ٤ . والمميَّز نحو : عندي عشرون؛ أي: درهماً»، «ليس عندي عشرون؛ أي: ديناراً»، إلى غيرِ ذلك .
The eight conditions, and the lack of unity of any of the unities entails difference of relation. Otherwise there is no restriction in what they mentioned; because contradiction is lifted by difference of: Chapter on Contradiction: 1. Instrument, such as: 'Zaid is a writer' meaning with the Wasiti pen, 'Zaid is not a writer' meaning with the Turkish pen. 2. Cause, such as: 'The carpenter is a worker' meaning for the Sultan, 'The carpenter is not a worker' meaning for other than him. 3. Object, such as: 'Zaid is a beater' meaning Amr, 'Zaid is not a beater' meaning Bakr. 4. Distinguished, such as: 'I have twenty' meaning dirhams, 'I do not have twenty' meaning dinars, and so forth.
Source pages p. 652

Commentary
This passage is from a commentary (sharh) explaining the conditions for two propositions to be contradictory (tanāquḍ). The author first mentions that among the eight unities (waḥdāt) required for contradiction—unity of subject, predicate, time, place, etc.—if any one of them is missing, the relation (nisba) changes, and the statements are no longer contradictory. Without these unities, contradiction could be avoided by any number of differences, making the logicians' restriction of eight conditions insufficient. Then, in a new chapter, the author enumerates specific examples of differences that prevent contradiction: 1. Difference in instrument (āla), e.g., writing with a Wasiti pen versus a Turkish pen. 2. Difference in cause (ʿilla), e.g., working for the Sultan versus working for someone else. 3. Difference in object (mafʿūl bihi), e.g., beating Amr versus beating Bakr. 4. Difference in the thing distinguished (mumayyaz), e.g., having twenty dirhams versus twenty dinars. The list continues with other aspects like time, place, etc. The key point is that for two statements to be truly contradictory, they must be identical in all these respects.

Key Terms
تناقض
آلة
علة
مفعول به
مميَّز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Difference in subject
B) Difference in predicate
C) Difference in instrument used for writing
D) Difference in truth value

Key Takeaway
For true logical contradiction, two propositions must agree in all eight conditions; a single difference removes the contradiction.

?

Examples of Non-Contradiction Through Difference in Instrument, Cause, Object, and Distinguished
Author gives examples of differences that avoid contradiction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and explain how differences in instrument (āla), cause (ʿilla), object (mafʿūl bihi), and distinguished (mumayyaz) prevent two statements from being logically contradictory.

Core Idea
Changing the instrument, cause, object, or specification can turn contradictory-looking statements into non-contradictory ones.

The passage gives concrete examples showing that even if two statements appear to assert and deny the same thing, they can both be true if they refer to different instruments, causes, objects, or specifications. This lesson analyzes each example to understand how they avoid contradiction.

Guided Path
1
Read the first example: 'Zaid is a writer' and 'Zaid is not a writer'
Note the difference in instrument: Wasiti pen versus Turkish pen. The two statements are about different actions because the instrument differs.
Thus both can be true: Zaid writes with one pen but not the other.
2
Analyze the second example: carpenter working for Sultan vs other
The cause (ʿilla) of the work differs: one for the Sultan, one for someone else. The statements refer to different employments.
Both statements can be true simultaneously.
3
Apply to object (mafʿūl bihi): beating Amr vs Bakr
The object of the action differs. 'Zaid is a beater' of Amr and 'Zaid is not a beater' of Bakr are not contradictory because the claim is about different persons.
This is a key distinction: the predicate 'beater' is ambiguous without specifying the object.
4
Understand the distinguished (mumayyaz) example: twenty dirhams vs dinars
The word 'twenty' is ambiguous until clarified by the mumayyaz (dirhams or dinars). The statements 'I have twenty dirhams' and 'I do not have twenty dinars' can both be true.
This shows that specification of the unit can resolve apparent contradiction.
5
Generalize the principle
Any of the eight unities, if different, removes contradiction. The four examples illustrate instrument, cause, object, and distinguished; similar examples exist for time, place, etc.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثَّمانية، وعدم وحدة شيء من الوحدات يستلزم اختلاف النّسبةِ . وإلا فلا حصر فيما ذكروه؛ لارتفاع التناقض باختلافِ : فَصْلٌ: فِي التَّنَاقُضِ : ١. الآلة، نحو: «زيد كاتب»؛ أي: بالقلم الواسطي، «زيد ليس بكاتب»؛ أي : بالقلم التركي. ٢. والعلَّةِ نحو النَّجَّار عامل ؛ أي : للسلطان النَّجّار ليس بعامل»؛ أي: لغيره. ٣. والمفعول به نحو زيدٌ ضاربٌ ؛ أي: عَمْرواً، زيدٌ ليس بضارب»؛ أي: بكراً . ٤ . والمميَّز نحو : عندي عشرون؛ أي: درهماً»، «ليس عندي عشرون؛ أي: ديناراً»، إلى غيرِ ذلك .
The eight conditions, and the lack of unity of any of the unities entails difference of relation. Otherwise there is no restriction in what they mentioned; because contradiction is lifted by difference of: Chapter on Contradiction: 1. Instrument, such as: 'Zaid is a writer' meaning with the Wasiti pen, 'Zaid is not a writer' meaning with the Turkish pen. 2. Cause, such as: 'The carpenter is a worker' meaning for the Sultan, 'The carpenter is not a worker' meaning for other than him. 3. Object, such as: 'Zaid is a beater' meaning Amr, 'Zaid is not a beater' meaning Bakr. 4. Distinguished, such as: 'I have twenty' meaning dirhams, 'I do not have twenty' meaning dinars, and so forth.
Source pages p. 652

Commentary
This passage is from a commentary (sharh) explaining the conditions for two propositions to be contradictory (tanāquḍ). The author first mentions that among the eight unities (waḥdāt) required for contradiction—unity of subject, predicate, time, place, etc.—if any one of them is missing, the relation (nisba) changes, and the statements are no longer contradictory. Without these unities, contradiction could be avoided by any number of differences, making the logicians' restriction of eight conditions insufficient. Then, in a new chapter, the author enumerates specific examples of differences that prevent contradiction: 1. Difference in instrument (āla), e.g., writing with a Wasiti pen versus a Turkish pen. 2. Difference in cause (ʿilla), e.g., working for the Sultan versus working for someone else. 3. Difference in object (mafʿūl bihi), e.g., beating Amr versus beating Bakr. 4. Difference in the thing distinguished (mumayyaz), e.g., having twenty dirhams versus twenty dinars. The list continues with other aspects like time, place, etc. The key point is that for two statements to be truly contradictory, they must be identical in all these respects.

آلة
علة
مفعول به
مميَّز

A difference in only one aspect of the judgment makes the statements logically consistent.

Identifying Factors of Contradiction in Non-Modal Propositions
Author enumerates cases of contradiction in non-modal propositions by varying cause, object, and specificator.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the factor (cause, object, or specificator) that distinguishes contradictory non-modal propositions.

Core Idea
Contradiction arises not only from truth values but also from differing factors like cause, object, or specificator.

In this passage, the author shows that contradiction is not merely about affirming and denying the same thing; it can also involve different accompanying factors. Understanding this expands your ability to detect true logical opposition.

Guided Path
1
Understand the standard definition of contradiction
Recall that two propositions are contradictory if they cannot both be true and cannot both be false. In non-modal propositions, this can occur even when the quality and quantity are the same, if a factor differs.
2
Examine the first example: cause (al-‘illa)
The passage says: 'the carpenter is a worker' meaning 'for the sultan', 'the carpenter is not a worker' meaning 'for other than him'. Here, the factor that changes is the cause—the entity for whom the work is done.
The cause is the reason or purpose of the action.
3
Examine the second example: object (al-maf‘ūl bihī)
The passage says: 'Zayd is a beater' meaning 'Amr', 'Zayd is not a beater' meaning 'Bakr'. The factor is the object of the action—who is being beaten.
The object is the recipient of the action.
4
Examine the third example: specificator (al-mumayyiz)
The passage says: 'I have twenty' meaning 'dirhams', 'I do not have twenty' meaning 'dinar'. The factor is the specificator that clarifies the type of the counted noun.
The specificator distinguishes between different kinds of the same quantity.
5
Generalize the concept
Contradiction can involve any contextual factor (cause, object, specificator, etc.), not just the truth value or the subject-predicate structure. The phrase 'and so on' (ilā ghayri dhālika) indicates there are other possible factors.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And the object: like 'Zayd is a beater' meaning 'Amr', 'Zayd is not a beater' meaning 'Bakr'. 4. And the specificator: like 'I have twenty' meaning 'dirhams', 'I do not have twenty' meaning 'dinar', and so on.
Source pages p. 653

Commentary
This passage is a definitional enumeration of types of contradiction in non-modal propositions. The author gives examples where the contradiction arises not from a difference in quality (affirmative/negative) but from a difference in the factor (al-‘illa), the object (al-maf‘ūl bihī), or the specificator (al-mumayyiz). The first example: 'the carpenter is a worker for the sultan' contradicts 'the carpenter is not a worker for anyone else'—here the cause (the sultan vs other) differs. The second: 'Zayd is a beater of Amr' contradicts 'Zayd is not a beater of Bakr'—the object differs. The third: 'I have twenty dirhams' contradicts 'I do not have twenty dinars'—the specificator (dirham vs dinar) differs. The phrase 'ilā ghayri dhālika' indicates that there are other factors as well. This is important because contradiction in logic is not only about quality and quantity but can involve other contextual factors.

The Contradictory of Necessary Propositions: General Possible
Author defines the contradictory of a necessary proposition as the general possible

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the contradictory of any necessary proposition and construct it using the general possible modality.

Core Idea
The contradictory of a necessary proposition negates its necessity, not its truth.

In non-modal logic, contradiction is simply a matter of differing in quality and quantity. But for modal propositions, the picture becomes complex because modalities interact. This lesson focuses on one clear rule from the classical tradition: the contradictory of a necessary proposition is the general possible. Al-Dassuqi's commentary unpacks the grammatical and logical nuances of this rule, emphasizing that the separating pronoun (ḍamīr faṣl) in the Arabic formulation ensures we do not confuse the predicate with an attribute. By understanding this rule, you lay the foundation for handling contradictions in modal logic, a crucial skill for debates in kalām and uṣūl al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the necessary proposition's modality
Recognize that necessary propositions assert that the predicate holds for all subjects as long as the subject exists. For example, 'Every human is necessarily an animal' means that for any human, the property of being an animal is necessary.
2
Understand the target of negation
The contradictory must negate the necessity, not the predicate itself. It should not assert that some humans are not animals, but that it is not necessary that every human is an animal. This is subtle but crucial.
3
Formulate the general possible proposition
Use the general possible modality (mumkina ʿāmma) to express 'not-necessary-that' for affirmatives. The contradictory of 'Every S is necessarily P' becomes 'Some S is not P by general possibility', meaning it is not necessary that all S are P.
For negatives, analogous rule applies: 'No S is necessarily P' would be contradicted by 'Some S is P by general possibility'.
4
Apply the separating pronoun rule
In the Arabic phrase 'al-naqīḍ li-l-ḍarūriyya huwa al-mumkina al-ʿāmma', the word 'huwa' is a separating pronoun that emphasizes that the predicate is exactly the general possible, not an adjective describing the necessary. This prevents misunderstanding the rule.
Al-Dassuqi explains that without this pronoun, one might read 'al-mumkina' as an adjective of 'al-ḍarūriyya', which would be logically wrong.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلَمْ أنَّ كيفيَّةَ التَّناقض في القضايا الغير الموجهة معلومة بمجرَّدِ الاختلافِ في الكيف والكمّ، وأما القضايا الموجهة؛ فلا يُعلَمُ حالها بمجرَّدِ الاختلافِ في الكيف والكمّ والجهة؛ إذ الجهات كثيرة، لا يُعرَفُ أنَّ هذهِ الجهة مثلاً مناقضة لأي جهةٍ، فلذا بين حال القضايا الموجّهة دون غيرها، فقال: (وَالنَّقِيضُ لِلضَّرُورِيَّةِ هِيَ الْمُمْكِنَةُ الْعَامَّةُ). قال الدسوقي: (قَوْلُهُ : وَالنَّقِيْضُ لِلضَّرُوْرِيَّةِ ... إلخ) النَّقيضُ : مبتدأ، خبره (الممكنة العامة)، جملة معرفةُ الطَّرفين، فتفيدُ الحصر، وأتى بضمير الفصل؛ إشارة إلى تأكيد الحصر المستفاد من تعريف الطَّرفين، وَلِدَفع أنَّ قولَه : (الممكنة) صفةٌ (للضَّروريَّة). وذلكَ الدَّفع هو الفائدة اللفظيَّةُ لهذا الضَّمير، ولهذا سُمِّيَ ضمير فصل؛ لفصله كونَ مَا بعدَهُ تابعاً لِمَا قبلَهُ؛ لا خبراً . (قَوْلُهُ: هُوَ الْمُمْكِنَةُ الْعَامَّةُ هذه العبارة تقتضي الحصر، وصحته باعتبار الفعليَّةَ تناقضُ الضَّروريّة من حيث اشتمالها على الإمكان؛ لَا مِن حيثُ ذاتها، وقوله : (والنَّقيضُ للضَّروريّة ... إلخ)؛ أي: الضَّروريّة المطلقة، وهي التي حكم فيها بضرورةِ النِّسبةِ مَا دامَتْ ذاتُ الموضوع موجودةً. والممكنة العامَّةُ : ما حكم فيها بعدم ضرورةِ خلافِ النِّسبةِ، ومثال ذلك في الموجبة قولنا : كلُّ إنسان حيوان بالضَّرورةِ، فَنقيضُهَا : بعضُ الإنسان ليس بحيوان بالإمكان العام؛ لأنَّ معناهُ سَلْبُ الضَّرورةِ عن الجانب المخالف، والجانب المخالف هنا هو الإيجاب.
And know that the manner of contradiction in non-modal propositions is known by mere difference in quality and quantity. As for modal propositions, their case is not known by mere difference in quality, quantity, and modality; because modalities are many, it is not known, for example, which modality contradicts which modality. Hence he clarified the case of modal propositions without others, saying: 'The contradictory of the necessary is the general possible.' Al-Dassuqi said: 'His statement: "The contradictory of the necessary..." — al-naqīḍ is the subject, its predicate is "the general possible", a sentence with definite both ends, thus indicating restriction. He introduced the separating pronoun to indicate emphasis of the restriction derived from the definiteness of both ends, and to preclude that his statement "the possible" is an adjective of "the necessary". That prevention is the verbal benefit of this pronoun, therefore it is called a separating pronoun because it separates what follows from being a qualifier of what precedes, not a predicate. His statement: "it is the general possible" — this expression necessitates restriction, and its correctness is considered effectively: it contradicts the necessary with respect to its inclusion of possibility, not with respect to its essence. And his statement: "the contradictory of the necessary" — that is, absolute necessary, which is that in which the necessity of the relation is judged as long as the subject exists. And the general possible is that in which it is judged that the opposite of the relation is not necessary. An example of that in the affirmative is our statement: Every human is an animal by necessity; its contradictory is: Some human is not an animal by general possibility, because its meaning is the negation of necessity concerning the opposite side, and the opposite side here is affirmation.'
Source pages p. 653

Commentary
This passage establishes the rule for contradiction in modal logic, specifically for necessary propositions. The author first states that for non-modal propositions, contradiction is known simply by difference in quality and quantity. For modal propositions, however, simply varying quality, quantity, and modality is insufficient because there are many modalities and it is unclear which contradict which. Therefore, the author gives the specific rule: the contradictory of a necessary proposition is the general possible proposition. Al-Dassuqi's commentary explains that this statement is a definite subject-predicate sentence with a separating pronoun (huwa) to emphasize that the predicate is indeed the general possible and not a description of the necessary. He clarifies that the phrase 'the contradictory of the necessary is the general possible' means that the contradictory negates the necessity, not the essence. For example, the necessary proposition 'Every human is necessarily an animal' is contradicted by 'Some human is possibly not an animal' (using general possibility: not-necessary that it is an animal). The contradictory therefore does not assert that some human is actually not an animal, but that it is not necessary that all humans are animals. This precision is important because the contradictory must be the exact opposite in modal sense: the necessary asserts necessity, so its contradictory must negate that necessity (i.e., possibility of non-existence).

Key Terms
ضرورية
ممكنة عامة
نقيض
ضمير فصل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The contradictory of a necessary proposition is always a general possible proposition, which negates the necessity without asserting the opposite necessity.

The Contradictory of a Necessary Proposition is the General Possible
Defining the contradictory of a necessary proposition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the contradictory of any necessary modal proposition and express it as a general possible proposition.

Core Idea
The contradictory of a necessary proposition is not a necessary negative, but a possible one.

In modal logic, the contradictory relationship between propositions depends on their mode. The author explains that a necessary proposition (affirmative or negative) is contradicted not by another necessary proposition, but by a proposition that denies the necessity. This is crucial for understanding how to construct valid arguments in kalām and uṣūl al-fiqh where modal statements are common.

Guided Path
1
Identify the modal proposition
Determine the subject, predicate, quantity, and modality of the given necessary proposition. For example, 'Every human is necessarily an animal' has the modality of absolute necessity.
Modality is indicated by terms like 'bi-al-ḍarūra' or 'ḍarūratan'.
2
State the contradictory according to the rule
The contradictory of a necessary proposition is a general possible proposition that denies the necessity from the opposite side. That is, it negates the necessity of the affirmation or negation, not the affirmation or negation itself.
General possible (al-mumkina al-‘āmma) means the opposite is possible, not impossible.
3
Apply to the example
For 'Every human is necessarily an animal', the contradictory is 'Some human is not an animal with general possibility' – meaning it is not necessary that every human is an animal; it is possible that some humans are not animals.
The contradictory has the opposite quality (negative) and the modality of general possibility.
4
Test the logical relationship
Verify that both propositions cannot be true together and cannot be false together. If the necessary proposition is true, its contradictory must be false, and vice versa.
This ensures the relationship is indeed contradiction (naqīḍ), not mere contrariety.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فلذا بين حال القضايا الموجهة دون غيرها، فقال: (والنقيض للضرورية) هو الممكنة العامة. هذه العبارة تقتضي الحصر، وصحته باعتبار الفعلية تناقض الضرورية من حيث اشتمالها على الإمكان؛ لا من حيث ذاتها. ومثال ذلك في الموجبة قولنا: كل إنسان حيوان بالضرورة، فنقيضها: بعض الإنسان ليس بحيوان بالإمكان العام؛ لأن معناه سلب الضرورة عن الجانب المخالف، والجانب المخالف هنا هو الإيجاب.
For this reason he explained the state of modal propositions exclusively, saying: 'And the contradictory of the necessary is the general possible.' This expression implies restriction, and its correctness is due to its contradicting the necessary in terms of containing possibility, not in terms of its essence. An example of that in the affirmative is our statement: Every human is an animal necessarily. Its contradictory is: Some human is not an animal with general possibility, because its meaning is the negation of necessity from the opposite side, and the opposite side here is affirmation.
Source pages p. 654

Commentary
This passage defines the contradictory (naqīḍ) of a necessary (ḍarūriyya) proposition. The commentator explains that the contradictory of a necessary proposition is the general possible (mumkina ‘āmma). This is because the necessary proposition affirms necessity of the predicate for the subject. Its contradictory must deny that necessity, but not by asserting impossibility; rather by asserting the possibility of the opposite. For example, 'Every human is necessarily an animal' is contradicted by 'Some human is not an animal with general possibility,' meaning it is not necessary that some human is an animal. The passage also notes that this contradictory relationship holds due to the aspect of possibility, not the essence of the necessary proposition.

Key Terms
النقيض (al-naqīḍ): The contradictory proposition; i.e., the proposition that directly opposes another in truth value such that both cannot be true and both cannot be false. [Note: No significant shift — stable across the tradition.]
الضرورية المطلقة (al-ḍarūriyya al-muṭlaqa): An absolute necessary proposition that judges the necessity of the relation (positive or negative) as long as the subject's essence exists. [Note: No significant shift — stable across the tradition.]
الممكنة العامة (al-mumkina al-‘āmma): A general possible proposition that judges the possibility of the opposite; i.e., it denies the necessity of the opposite side of the judgment. [Note: No significant shift — stable across the tradition.]
ضمير الفصل (ḍamīr al-faṣl): A separate pronoun (e.g., 'huwa') used between subject and predicate to emphasize restriction and prevent misreading the predicate as an adjective. [Note: No significant shift — stable across Arabic grammar tradition.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) Every human is an animal with general possibility.

Key Takeaway
The contradictory of a necessary proposition is always a general possible proposition that denies the necessity, not the impossibility.

Contradictory of Necessary and Perpetual Propositions
Author defines contradictory of necessary and perpetual propositions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the contradictory of any necessary or perpetual categorical proposition using the rules described in the passage.

Core Idea
The contradictory of a perpetual proposition is not another perpetual, but an absolute general.

In modal logic, understanding contradictions is crucial for constructing valid arguments. This passage from Al-Tadhheeb's commentary explains how to find the contradictory of necessary and perpetual propositions. The key insight is that the contradictory of a perpetual proposition is an absolute general proposition, not another perpetual. This rule is essential for converting between modal statement types in theological and juristic reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the modal proposition type
Determine whether the proposition is necessary (ḍarūriyya) or perpetual (dā’ima). Note its quantity (universal/particular) and quality (affirmative/negative).
Necessary propositions assert the relation is always true; perpetuals assert it holds as long as the subject exists.
2
For necessary propositions, negate necessity on the same side
The contradictory of a necessary affirmative is a possible negative particular; of a necessary negative is a possible affirmative particular. This follows from the definition: negating necessity yields possibility.
Example: 'Every man is necessarily a writer' contradicts 'Some man is not a writer possibly'.
3
For perpetual propositions, replace perpetual with absolute general
The contradictory of a perpetual proposition is an absolute general (muṭlaqa ʿāmma) proposition with opposite quality and quantity. Perpetual affirms at all times; absolute general denies at some time.
The absolute general asserts actuality without temporal restriction.
4
Verify with the provided examples
Check the examples: Perpetual affirmative 'Every man is always an animal' contradicts 'Some man is not an animal with absolute generality'. Perpetual negative 'Nothing of man is always [an animal]' contradicts 'Some man is a stone with absolute generality'.
Ensure the contradictory has opposite truth value in all situations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
التطقية الضرورية الموجبة - مناقض لسلب الضرورة عن جانب الإيجاب؛ وهو مفهوم السالبة الممكنة.
وكذا إثبات الضرورة في جانب السلب ـ وهو مفهوم الضرورية السالبة - مناقض لسلب الضرورة عن جانب السلب؛ وهو مفهوم الموجبة الممكنة.
(وَ) النَّقيضُ (لِلدَّائِمَةِ) هو (المُطْلَقَةُ العَامَّةُ)
مَفهومَ الضَّروريّةِ المطلقة الموجبة ؛ النِّسبةُ الضَّروريَّةُ ، لا إثباتُ الضَّرورة كما لا يخفى، وكذا يُقالُ فيما يأتي ما يناسبه في جانب الإيجاب؛ أي: المستفاد من القضية الموجبة . (قَوْلُهُ : مُنَاقِضُ) خبرُ «أَنَّ» .
(قَوْلُهُ : عَنْ جَانِبِ الْإِيْجَابِ) أي: لأنَّهُ الطَّرفُ المخالف في الممكنة السالبة. (قَوْلُهُ : وَالنَّقِيْضُ لِلدَّائِمَةِ ... إلخ) أي : المطلقة، والدَّائمة المطلقةُ : ما حكم فيها بدوامِ النِّسبةِ؛ ما دامت ذاتُ الموضوع موجودةً، والمطلقة العامَّةُ : ما حكم فيها بفعليَّةِ النِّسبةِ، ومثال ذلكَ في الموجبة : كُلُّ إنسان حيوان دائماً ، فَنقيضُهَا : بعضُ الإنسان ليس بحيوان بالإطلاق العام، وفى السَّالبة : لا شيء مِنَ الإنسان دائماً، فنقيضُها : بعضُ الإنسانِ حجرٌ بالإطلاقِ العام، وتوجيه التناقض في ذلك ما ذكرَهُ الشَّارح.
(قَوْلُهُ: وَهُوَ) راجع إلى الإثباتِ، وَقَوْلُهُ (مُناقض خبر أنَّ، ومثله نظيرُهُ الآتي. (قَوْلُهُ: وَكَذَا إِثْبَاتُ الضَّرُورَةِ فِي جَانِبِ السَّلْبِ) فَمَثَلُ قولِنَا : لَا شَيءٍ مِنَ الإنسان بكاتب بالضَّرورة؛ مُناقض لـ: بعضُ الإنسانِ كاتب بالإمكان العام، فَعُلِمَ مِنْ تفصيلِهِ أنَّ نقيضَ الضَّروريَّةِ المطلقة الموجبة الكُلِّيَّة؛ مُمكنةٌ عامة سالبة جزئية، ونقيض الضَّروريَّةِ السَّالبةِ الكُلِّيَّةِ ؛ الممكنة العامة الموجبة الجزئية، وهكذا البيان في الموضوع والشَّارح بيَّن هنا أنها إثبات الضرورة في جانب الإيجاب أو السلب، والجواب أن كلامه على تقدير مضاف؛ أي: موصوف هنا الإثبات ولك أن تقول : إنه تفسير باللازم . ا.هـ. الشَّرنوبي
The necessary affirmative proposition is the contradictory of the negation of necessity from the affirmative side; this is the concept of the possible negative. Similarly, the affirmation of necessity on the negative side — which is the concept of the necessary negative — is the contradictory of the negation of necessity from the negative side; this is the concept of the possible affirmative. (And) the contradictory of the perpetual is the absolute general. The concept of the necessary absolute affirmative is the necessary relation, not the affirmation of necessity, as is not hidden, and likewise it is said in what follows what is appropriate on the affirmative side; i.e., what is derived from the affirmative proposition. (His statement: 'contradictory' is the predicate of 'that'. His statement: 'from the affirmative side' means because it is the opposite side in the possible negative. His statement: 'and the contradictory of the perpetual... etc.' means the absolute, and the perpetual absolute is that in which the continuity of the relation is judged as long as the subject's essence exists, and the absolute general is that in which the actuality of the relation is judged. An example of that in the affirmative: Every man is always an animal, so its contradictory is: Some man is not an animal with the general absolute. In the negative: Nothing of man is always [an animal], so its contradictory is: Some man is a stone with the general absolute. The explanation of the contradiction in that is what the commentator mentioned. His statement: 'and it' refers to the affirmation, and his statement 'contradictory' is the predicate of 'that', and similar is its counterpart to follow. His statement: 'and likewise the affirmation of necessity on the negative side' so an example of our saying: Nothing of man is a writer necessarily is contradictory to: Some man is a writer with general possibility. So it is known from his detail that the contradictory of the necessary absolute universal affirmative is a possible general particular negative, and the contradictory of the necessary universal negative is the possible general particular affirmative. And thus the explanation in the subject and the commentator explained here that they are the affirmation of necessity on the affirmative or negative side, and the answer is that his statement is on the assumption of an added [word]; i.e., described here as affirmation, and you can say it is an interpretation by implication. End. Al-Sharnubi.
Source pages p. 656

Commentary
This passage defines the contradictory relations for modal propositions, specifically for necessary and perpetual propositions. The author first states that the contradictory of a necessary affirmative (e.g., 'Every man is necessarily a writer') is a possible negative (e.g., 'Some man is not a writer possibly'), because the negation of necessity on the affirmative side yields possibility. Similarly, the contradictory of a necessary negative is a possible affirmative. The text then asserts a key rule: the contradictory of a perpetual (dā'ima) proposition—one where the relation holds as long as the subject exists—is the absolute general (muṭlaqa ʿāmma), which judges the relation as actual without temporal condition. The commentator provides examples: the perpetual affirmative 'Every man is always an animal' contradicts 'Some man is not an animal' with absolute generality. The perpetual negative 'Nothing of man is always an animal' contradicts 'Some man is a stone' with absolute generality. The commentator further explains that the contradictory of the necessary absolute universal is a possible particular, and clarifies that 'necessity' in this context refers to the relation, not the assertion of necessity. This passage is essential for understanding how contradictions work across different modal propositions in logic.
