Real vs. Metaphorical Contradiction: Two Senses of Naqīḍ
Author clarifies that the contradictory of perpetual is not a real contradictory but a necessary concomitant.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the real contradictory and the necessary concomitant (lāzim musāwī) in modal propositions, and identify which sense of 'naqīḍ' is being used in a given context.

Core Idea
Not all oppositions are contradictions; the term 'naqīḍ' can refer to a real contradictory or a necessary concomitant.

In previous lessons, you learned about contradiction as a relation between two propositions that always have opposite truth values. However, in this passage, the commentator refines this: when we speak of the contradictory of a perpetual proposition, what appears to be the contradictory is actually a necessary concomitant. Understanding this nuance is crucial for correctly applying the rules of immediate inference in modal logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two propositions
The text contrasts the perpetual affirmative (e.g., 'Every man is always a writer') with the absolute general negative (e.g., 'Some man is not a writer' at some time). Note the difference in modality: perpetual vs. absolute.
Perpetual: holds at all times; absolute: holds at some time without commitment to all times.
2
Understand why they are not real contradictories
The real contradictory of the perpetual would be its negation: 'Not every man is always a writer' (i.e., the perpetual is false at some time). The absolute general negative does not directly negate the perpetual; it only asserts a negative at some time, which is a consequence of the non-perpetuity.
Real contradiction requires opposite truth values in all possible situations.
3
Recognize the necessary concomitant
The absolute general negative is a necessary concomitant (lāzim musāwī) of the real contradictory. Whenever the perpetual is false, the absolute general negative is true, and vice versa. Thus it is logically equivalent to the negation of the perpetual.
Necessary concomitant: truth conditions are identical; one implies the other and vice versa.
4
Observe the two uses of 'naqīḍ'
The commentator notes that the term 'naqīḍ' in this chapter is used in two senses: real contradiction (e.g., necessary vs. possible) and metaphorical (e.g., contradictory of the absolute general, which is actually the perpetual? No, the text says the contradictory of the absolute general is taken as the perpetual, but that is a metaphor). Abd al-Hakim verified that 'naqīḍ' can be used in a general sense covering both.
Context determines which sense is intended.

ولهذا قال شيخ الإسلام : ثمَّ الظَّاهِرُ أنَّهُ أراد بالمطلقة ما حكم فيها بفعلية النسبة على ما هو المتعارفُ عند القوم، انظر يس

أَنَّهُ
البواقي، وإنَّما كانَ كذلِكَ ؛ لأنَّ سلبَ ضَرورةِ الإيجاب إمكان عام سالبٌ، وسلب ضرورةِ السَّلْبِ إمكان عام موجبٌ . (قَوْلُهُ : لأَنَّ الْإِيْجَابَ فِي كُلِّ الْأَوْقَاتِ... إلخ) فَمِثلُ قولنا : كُلُّ إنسانٍ كاتب دائماً، يُنافي قولنا : ليس بعضُ الإنسانِ بِكاتبِ بِالإطلاقِ العام. (قَوْلُهُ : يُنَافِي السَّلْبَ فِي بَعْضِ الأَوْقَاتِ وإِنَّما عبَّرَ بالمنافاة للإشارة إلى ليسَ نَقيضاً حقيقةً؛ بل لأنَّه المساوي ؛ لأنَّ نقيض دوام السَّلْبِ عدم دوامِ السَّلبِ، والتبوتُ في البعض لازم له، ونقيضُ دوام الإيجاب رفعه، ويلزمُ السَّلْب في بعض الأوقات؛ سواء كان في جميع الأوقاتِ أَوْ لَا ، وهكذا يُقالُ في البقيَّةِ، فَلَفْظُ النَّقي المستعملُ في هذا الفصل قدْ يُرادُ بِهِ نفسُ النَّقيض كَمَا في قولِهِ : نقيضُ الضَّروريّةِ الممكنة، وقدْ يُرادُ بِهِ اللّازم المساوي كَمَا في قولهم : نقيضُ المطلقة العامَّةِ، فَلفْظُ النَّقيض مُستعمل في بعض المواضع في المعنى الحقيقي، وفي بعضها بالمعنى المجازي، أو في المعنى الأعم الصَّادِقِ على كُلِّ واحدٍ منهما على طريق عموم المجاز، أو في المعنى الأعمّ الصَّادِقِ على كُلِّ واحدٍ منهما على طريق عموم المجاز ؛ أي: مَا يُطلَقُ عليهِ لفظُ النَّقيض، كذا حقق عبد الحكيم، وقال شارح القسطاس : ما ذكروه في تناقض القضايا ليسَ نَقيضاً حقيقيًّا؛ بل مُساوياً

Chapter on Contradiction. Because the affirmative in all times — which is the concept of the perpetual affirmative — opposes the negative in some times; which is the concept of the absolute negative. (His statement: 'opposes the negative in some times... etc.') This requires that what is meant by the absolute general is what he previously expressed as the distributed. For this reason, Shaykh al-Islam said: Then it appears that he intended by the absolute that in which the actuality of the relation is judged, according to what is customary among the people, see Yas. The rest [of the propositions], and it is only so because the negation of the necessity of affirmation is a general possible negative, and the negation of the necessity of negation is a general possible affirmative. (His statement: 'because the affirmative in all times... etc.') So an example of our saying: Every man is always a writer opposes our saying: Not some man is a writer with general absolute. (His statement: 'opposes the negative in some times' and he only expressed it with opposition to indicate that it is not a real contradictory; rather because it is the equal, because the contradictory of the perpetuity of negation is the non-perpetuity of negation, and affirmation in some times is a concomitant of it, and the contradictory of the perpetuity of affirmation is its removal, and the negative in some times follows from it, whether in all times or not. And thus it is said for the rest. So the term 'contradictory' used in this chapter may intend the real contradictory as in his statement: the contradictory of the necessary is the possible, and it may intend the necessary concomitant as in their statement: the contradictory of the absolute general. So the term 'contradictory' is used in some places in the real sense, and in some places in the metaphorical sense, or in the more general sense true of each of them by way of generality of metaphor. That is, what the word 'contradictory' is applied to. Thus verified by Abd al-Hakim. And the commentator of al-Qistas said: What they mentioned about the contradiction of propositions is not a real contradictory, but rather an equivalent. (Footnote: His statement: 'see Yas' — the phrase of Yas is devoid of clarification; because he intended to express it with the distributed absolute that was not mentioned in the previous modalities, so he expressed it with the distributed absolute that was previously counted among the necessities, and this is an error; since necessity does not contradict perpetuity but rather possibility.)
Source pages p. 657

Commentary
This passage is a clarification on the nature of contradiction in modal logic, specifically addressing the relationship between perpetual and absolute general propositions. It begins by stating that an affirmative perpetual (e.g., 'Every man is always a writer') opposes a negative absolute general (e.g., 'Some man is not a writer' at some time). The commentator notes that the absolute general here means the 'distributed' (muntašira) as previously defined. Shaykh al-Islam opines that 'absolute' refers to actuality, not temporal modality. The core insight is that the opposition between a perpetual and an absolute general is not a real contradiction (tanāquḍ ḥaqīqī) but a necessary concomitant (lāzim musāwī). Real contradiction would be between perpetual and its negation (non-perpetuity), but the absolute general follows necessarily from that negation. The term 'naqīḍ' is thus used in two senses: real (like 'the contradictory of the necessary is the possible') and metaphorical (like 'the contradictory of the absolute general' which is actually a concomitant). The passage ends with a critique of Yas's confusion between necessity and perpetuity. This is a refinement because previous lessons might have assumed 'naqīḍ' always means real contradiction; here the author specifies its contextual usage.

Key Terms
نقيض (naqīḍ): In this context, it sometimes means the real contradictory (a proposition that must differ in truth value), sometimes the necessary concomitant (a proposition that is equivalent in truth conditions). [Note: In earlier lessons, 'naqīḍ' was used strictly for real contradiction; here the commentator expands its usage to include necessary concomitants.]
لازم مساو (lāzim musāwī): A proposition that is logically equivalent to another, so that their truth values always match; it is not a real contradictory but a necessary concomitant. [Note: This term is stable.]
دائمة (dā'ima): A perpetual proposition that holds for all times the subject exists. [Note: Same as in previous lesson.]
مطلقة عامة (muṭlaqa ʿāmma): An absolute general proposition that asserts actuality at some time, without perpetual commitment. [Note: Here it is equated with 'muntašira' (distributed), which is a variation.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Accident
D) Subcontrary

Key Takeaway
The term 'naqīḍ' is polysemous: it can refer to a real contradictory (opposite truth values) or a necessary concomitant (equivalent truth conditions), and context determines which is meant.

Distinguishing General and Expanded Absolute for Contradictories
Author discusses the correct negation of the perpetual negative and refines the definition of the absolute affirmative.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the general absolute (muṭlaqa ‘āmma) and the expanded absolute (muṭlaqa munṭashira) and identify the correct contradictory of a perpetual negative proposition.

Core Idea
The contradictory of 'always not' is the expanded, not the general, absolute.

This lesson examines the precise contradictory of a perpetual negative proposition. The text initially states that the contradictory is the absolute affirmative, but a key refinement shows that the absolute intended must be the expanded absolute, which affirms at some time, not the general absolute which lacks temporal condition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the perpetual negative and its contradictory claim
The perpetual negative says 'not at any time'; the claim is its contradictory is affirmation at some time.
2
Understand the objection
Al-Dasūqī objects that affirmation at some time is the expanded absolute, not the general absolute.
The expanded absolute explicitly includes a temporal quantifier.
3
Analyze the resolution
The answer says the general absolute indicates some time by implication. However, the refined view is that the correct contradictory is actually the expanded absolute.
The implication (luzūm) is not sufficient for direct contradiction.
4
Grasp the reason
The general absolute is more general and can be true of non-temporal subjects, so it does not directly contradict the temporal negation.
For example, 'Time exists' is true in general but not in a time.
5
Apply the distinction
Practice by identifying the contradictory of a given perpetual negative.
Use Islamic examples from kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكذا السلب في كلِّ الأوقات ـ وهو مفهوم الدائمة السالبة ـ ينافي الإيجاب في بعض الأوقاتِ، وهو مفهوم المطلقة الموجبة.
الدسوقي
لكن يرد عليهِ (۱) : أن الإيجاب والسلب في وقت ما مفهوم المطلقة المنتشرة، ويمكن الجواب (۲) : بأنَّه أرادَ دلالة المطلقة العامة على بعض الأوقاتِ بطريقِ اللُّزوم .
العطار لهُ، وَاستَحسَنَهُ السَّيِّدُ، ثمَّ إِنَّ إطلاق اسم النقيض على لازمه المساوي إنَّما يكونُ بعد رعاية اتّحادِ الموضوع والمحمول؛ حتَّى لا يكون قولنا : زيد ناطق؛ نَقيضاً لقولِنَا : زيدٌ ليس بإنسانٍ، وإنْ كانَ مُساوياً لِنَقِيضِهِ ; لأنَّ المساويات كثيرة، فَلَوْ لَمْ يعتبر رعاية اتحادِ الطَّرفينِ لَتَعشَرَ ضبطُ النَّقائض
(قَوْلُهُ : وَهُوَ مَفْهُوْمُ الْمُطْلَقَةِ الْمُوْجِبَةِ لِقائل أن يقول : الثبوت أو السَّلْبُ في وقتٍ مَا ليسَ مَفهوم المطلقة; لأنَّها المحكوم فيها بفعليَّةِ النِّسبةِ مِن غير قيدٍ آخر، وَهِي أعمُّ مِنَ الَّتي حكم فيها بفعليَّةِ النّسبة في وقتٍ ما ; أعني : المطلقة المنتشرة لجواز أنْ يكونَ الحكم بالفعل مِمَّا لا يتحقَّقُ في وقتٍ أصلاً، إِذْ ليسَ يلزمُ مِن صدق الحكم بالفعل في الجملة صدقه في شيءٍ من الأوقاتِ; لجواز أنْ يكونَ الموضوع نفس الوقتِ، فإنَّهُ لا يصدقُ الحكمُ عليهِ في وقتٍ، وإلا ; لكانَ لِلوقتِ وقت كَمَا يُقالُ : الزَّمانُ مَوجودٌ في الجملة أو غيرُ قار الذَّاتِ، إلى غيرِ القضايا التي موضوعاتها لا تقبلُ التَّقييد بالزَّمانِ، فَنقيضُ الدَّائمةِ المنتشرة لا المطلقة العامَّةُ، ونقيضُ المطلقة العامَّةِ غيرُ مَبيَّن، هذا ما حقَّقَهُ
(۱) (قَوْلُهُ : يرد عليه ... إلخ) أي : فكان الواجب أن يقول: ونقيض الدائمة المنتشرة دون المطلقة العامة، والفرق بينهما أن المطلقة العامة هي التي حكم فيها بفعلية النسبة مطلقاً، والمطلقة المنتشرة حكم فيها بفعلية النسبة في وقت ما والأولى أعمّ تنفرد فيما إذا كان الموضوع زماناً، وإلا ; لزم أن يكون للزمان زمان.
(۲) (قَوْلُهُ : ويمكن الجواب ... إلخ) الجواب الصحيح ما قاله شيخ الإسلام من أن المصنف بنى كلامه هنا على المشهور بين القوم، وإن كان التحقيق عنده ما قاله في شرح الأصل من أن نقيض الدائمة هو المطلقة المنتشرة دون المطلقة العامة، وأما جواب المحشَّي فلا يصح إذ المطلقة العامة أعم ولا يلزم من وجود الأعم وجود الأخص.
And likewise, the negation in all times — which is the concept of the perpetual negative — contradicts affirmation in some times, which is the concept of the absolute affirmative. Al-Dasūqī: But it is objected to him: that affirmation and negation in a time is the concept of the absolute expanded (muṭlaqa munṭashira). And the answer can be: that he intended the indication of the general absolute (muṭlaqa ‘āmma) onto some times by way of implication (luzūm). Al-‘Aṭṭār: And al-Sayyid approved this. Then, applying the name 'contradictory' to its equivalent necessary concomitant is only after observing the unity of subject and predicate; so that our statement 'Zayd is speaking' is not a contradictory of 'Zayd is not a human', even if it is equivalent to its contradictory, because equivalents are many. If the observance of the unity of the two sides is not considered, then the regulation of contradictories would be difficult. (His statement: and it is the concept of the absolute affirmative) — someone may say: affirmation or negation in some time is not the concept of the absolute (muṭlaqa), because it is that in which the actuality of the relation is judged without another condition, and it is more general than that in which actuality of the relation is judged in some time, i.e., the absolute expanded (muṭlaqa munṭashira), due to the possibility that the judgment of actuality is not realized in any time at all, since from the truth of the judgment of actuality in general it does not necessitate its truth in any one of the times, because the subject may be time itself, for the judgment on it is not true in a time, otherwise time would have a time, as is said: 'Time exists in general' or 'non-permanent essence', etc., among propositions whose subjects do not accept temporal restriction. So the contradictory of the perpetual (dā’ima) is the expanded (munṭashira), not the general absolute; and the contradictory of the general absolute is not clarified. This is what he verified. (1) (His statement: it is objected to him... etc.) i.e., the obligation was for him to say: and the contradictory of the perpetual expanded, not the general absolute; the difference between them is that the general absolute is that in which the actuality of the relation is judged absolutely, and the expanded absolute is that in which the actuality of the relation is judged in some time, and the first is more general, being unique when the subject is time, otherwise it would necessitate that time has a time. (2) (His statement: and the answer can be... etc.) The correct answer is what Shaykh al-Islām said: that the author built his statement here on what is well-known among the people, even though the verification according to him is what he said in the commentary on the original: that the contradictory of the perpetual is the expanded absolute, not the general absolute. As for the answer of the annotator, it is not valid because the general absolute is more general, and the existence of the more general does not necessitate the existence of the more specific.
Source pages p. 658

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement (taḥqīq) regarding the negation of the perpetual negative proposition, specifically the definition of its contradictory (naqīḍ). The main text states that negation in all times (i.e., the concept of perpetual negative) contradicts affirmation in some times (i.e., the concept of absolute affirmative). Al-Dasūqī raises an objection: affirmation or negation in a time is actually the concept of the absolute expanded (muṭlaqa munṭashira), not the general absolute (muṭlaqa ‘āmma). The answer given is that the author intended the general absolute to indicate some times by implication (luzūm). Al-‘Aṭṭār then notes a more precise condition: the term 'contradictory' applies to a necessary equivalent only if subject and predicate are identical; otherwise, many false equivalents would arise. The passage then delves deeper into a critical analysis: the absolute affirmative (muṭlaqa mūjiba) as defined (affirmation in some time) is not truly the concept of the absolute, because the absolute is judgment of actuality without any time condition. The absolute is more general than the expanded absolute, since a judgment of actuality may never be realized in any time (e.g., 'time exists' is true but not in a time). Therefore, the correct contradictory of the perpetual negative is the expanded absolute, not the general absolute. The footnotes clarify that the author adopted the common view in his summary, but in his longer commentary he held the expanded absolute to be the proper contradictory. This nuanced discussion guards against conflating the absolute (unconditioned) with the expanded (temporally restricted) proposition, which is crucial for accurate logical analysis.

المطلقة الموجبة

Conditions for Contradictories: Unity of Subject and Predicate
Author discusses the correct negation of the perpetual negative and refines the definition of the absolute affirmative.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the condition of unity of subject and predicate to determine genuine contradictories and avoid equating mere equivalences.

Core Idea
A proposition is not a contradictory just because it is equivalent; subject and predicate must match.

This lesson focuses on the crucial condition for applying the term 'contradictory' (naqīḍ): the unity of subject and predicate. Even if two propositions are logically equivalent, they are not contradictories unless they share the same subject and predicate. This prevents a proliferation of false contradictories.

Guided Path
1
Identify the rule
The term 'contradictory' can only be applied to a necessary equivalent after observing unity of subject and predicate.
2
Examine the example
'Zayd is speaking' vs 'Zayd is not a human' are equivalent (since speaking implies humanity) but not contradictories because predicates differ.
The example illustrates that equivalence is insufficient.
3
Understand the rationale
If we drop the unity condition, many equivalent pairs would be mislabeled as contradictories, making logical analysis messy.
The passage says 'the regulation of contradictories would be difficult'.
4
Apply the condition
Practise by determining whether a given pair are genuine contradictories.
Use Islamic examples from usul al-fiqh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكذا السلب في كلِّ الأوقات ـ وهو مفهوم الدائمة السالبة ـ ينافي الإيجاب في بعض الأوقاتِ، وهو مفهوم المطلقة الموجبة.
الدسوقي
لكن يرد عليهِ (۱) : أن الإيجاب والسلب في وقت ما مفهوم المطلقة المنتشرة، ويمكن الجواب (۲) : بأنَّه أرادَ دلالة المطلقة العامة على بعض الأوقاتِ بطريقِ اللُّزوم .
العطار لهُ، وَاستَحسَنَهُ السَّيِّدُ، ثمَّ إِنَّ إطلاق اسم النقيض على لازمه المساوي إنَّما يكونُ بعد رعاية اتّحادِ الموضوع والمحمول؛ حتَّى لا يكون قولنا : زيد ناطق؛ نَقيضاً لقولِنَا : زيدٌ ليس بإنسانٍ، وإنْ كانَ مُساوياً لِنَقِيضِهِ ; لأنَّ المساويات كثيرة، فَلَوْ لَمْ يعتبر رعاية اتحادِ الطَّرفينِ لَتَعشَرَ ضبطُ النَّقائض
(قَوْلُهُ : وَهُوَ مَفْهُوْمُ الْمُطْلَقَةِ الْمُوْجِبَةِ لِقائل أن يقول : الثبوت أو السَّلْبُ في وقتٍ مَا ليسَ مَفهوم المطلقة; لأنَّها المحكوم فيها بفعليَّةِ النِّسبةِ مِن غير قيدٍ آخر، وَهِي أعمُّ مِنَ الَّتي حكم فيها بفعليَّةِ النّسبة في وقتٍ ما ; أعني : المطلقة المنتشرة لجواز أنْ يكونَ الحكم بالفعل مِمَّا لا يتحقَّقُ في وقتٍ أصلاً، إِذْ ليسَ يلزمُ مِن صدق الحكم بالفعل في الجملة صدقه في شيءٍ من الأوقاتِ; لجواز أنْ يكونَ الموضوع نفس الوقتِ، فإنَّهُ لا يصدقُ الحكمُ عليهِ في وقتٍ، وإلا ; لكانَ لِلوقتِ وقت كَمَا يُقالُ : الزَّمانُ مَوجودٌ في الجملة أو غيرُ قار الذَّاتِ، إلى غيرِ القضايا التي موضوعاتها لا تقبلُ التَّقييد بالزَّمانِ، فَنقيضُ الدَّائمةِ المنتشرة لا المطلقة العامَّةُ، ونقيضُ المطلقة العامَّةِ غيرُ مَبيَّن، هذا ما حقَّقَهُ
(۱) (قَوْلُهُ : يرد عليه ... إلخ) أي : فكان الواجب أن يقول: ونقيض الدائمة المنتشرة دون المطلقة العامة، والفرق بينهما أن المطلقة العامة هي التي حكم فيها بفعلية النسبة مطلقاً، والمطلقة المنتشرة حكم فيها بفعلية النسبة في وقت ما والأولى أعمّ تنفرد فيما إذا كان الموضوع زماناً، وإلا ; لزم أن يكون للزمان زمان.
(۲) (قَوْلُهُ : ويمكن الجواب ... إلخ) الجواب الصحيح ما قاله شيخ الإسلام من أن المصنف بنى كلامه هنا على المشهور بين القوم، وإن كان التحقيق عنده ما قاله في شرح الأصل من أن نقيض الدائمة هو المطلقة المنتشرة دون المطلقة العامة، وأما جواب المحشَّي فلا يصح إذ المطلقة العامة أعم ولا يلزم من وجود الأعم وجود الأخص.
And likewise, the negation in all times — which is the concept of the perpetual negative — contradicts affirmation in some times, which is the concept of the absolute affirmative. Al-Dasūqī: But it is objected to him: that affirmation and negation in a time is the concept of the absolute expanded (muṭlaqa munṭashira). If the observance of the unity of the two sides is not considered, then the regulation of contradictories would be difficult. So the contradictory of the perpetual (dā’ima) is the expanded (munṭashira), not the general absolute; and the contradictory of the general absolute is not clarified. This is what he verified. (1) (His statement: it is objected to him... etc.) i.e., the obligation was for him to say: and the contradictory of the perpetual expanded, not the general absolute; the difference between them is that the general absolute is that in which the actuality of the relation is judged absolutely, and the expanded absolute is that in which the actuality of the relation is judged in some time, and the first is more general, being unique when the subject is time, otherwise it would necessitate that time has a time. (2) (His statement: and the answer can be... etc.) The correct answer is what Shaykh al-Islām said: that the author built his statement here on what is well-known among the people, even though the verification according to him is what he said in the commentary on the original: that the contradictory of the perpetual is the expanded absolute, not the general absolute. As for the answer of the annotator, it is not valid because the general absolute is more general, and the existence of the more general does not necessitate the existence of the more specific.

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement (taḥqīq) regarding the negation of the perpetual negative proposition, specifically the definition of its contradictory (naqīḍ). The main text states that negation in all times (i.e., the concept of perpetual negative) contradicts affirmation in some times (i.e., the concept of absolute affirmative). Al-Dasūqī raises an objection: affirmation or negation in a time is actually the concept of the absolute expanded (muṭlaqa munṭashira), not the general absolute (muṭlaqa ‘āmma). The answer given is that the author intended the general absolute to indicate some times by implication (luzūm). Al-‘Aṭṭār then notes a more precise condition: the term 'contradictory' applies to a necessary equivalent only if subject and predicate are identical; otherwise, many false equivalents would arise. The passage then delves deeper into a critical analysis: the absolute affirmative (muṭlaqa mūjiba) as defined (affirmation in some time) is not truly the concept of the absolute, because the absolute is judgment of actuality without any time condition. This nuanced discussion guards against conflating the absolute (unconditioned) with the expanded (temporally restricted) proposition, which is crucial for accurate logical analysis.

Identifying the Contradictory of the Conditioned Universal
Author states that the contradictory of the conditioned universal is the possible temporal proposition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the correct contradictory of a conditioned universal proposition and distinguish it from incorrect alternatives using modal and temporal precision.

Core Idea
The contradictory of a conditioned universal is a possible temporal: it negates necessity while the description holds.

In this lesson, we examine a subtle logical relationship: the contradictory (naqīḍ) of the conditioned universal (al-mashrūṭa al-‘āmma). The author clarifies that it is the possible temporal (al-ḥīniyya al-mumkina). This requires careful attention to temporal and modal exactness. We will break down the definition and apply it to examples from Islamic theology, especially in Kalam discussions of divine attributes and temporal events.

Guided Path
1
Understand the conditioned universal
A conditioned universal proposition has the form: 'Every A is B necessarily as long as A is described by C'. For example, 'Every writer is necessarily an animal as long as he is a writer'.
The condition 'as long as the description exists' is essential.
2
Recognize its contradictory requirement
The contradictory must deny the necessity while preserving the condition. It must be: 'Some A is not B possibly when A is described by C'. This is called the possible temporal (ḥīniyya mumkina).
The contradictory must have opposite truth value in all cases.
3
Contrast with common errors
Simply negating without the temporal condition (e.g., 'Some A is not B necessarily at all times') fails because it changes the condition. Also, using a universal quantifier or different modality breaks the contradictory relationship.
The condition 'as long as' must be retained in the contradictory.
4
Verify with the source example
Consider: 'Every writer is necessarily an animal as long as he is a writer'. Its contradictory is: 'Some writer is not an animal possibly when he is a writer'. Both cannot be true and both cannot be false, as shown in Sharḥ al-Maṭāli‘.
The example from the text tests the rule and confirms the correct contradictory.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ: فِي التَّنَاقُضِ
(وَ) النَّقيضُ لِلْمَشْرُوْطَةِ العَامَّةِ) هو (الحِينِيَّةُ المُمْكِنَةُ)
الدسوقي
(قَوْلُهُ : وَالنَّقِيضُ لِلْمَشْرُوطَةِ الْعَامَّةِ ... إلخ) المشروطة العامَّةُ : ما حكم فيها بضرورة النّسبة ما دامَ وصفُ الموضوع موجوداً، والممكنة الحينية عرفها الشارخ بقوله : التي حكم فيها ... إلخ .
العطار
الوقتي،
المصنِّفُ في شرح في شرح الأصل، وحينئذ ; فبينَ كلامَيْهِ تدافع ; اللَّهُمَّ إِلَّا أَنْ يُقالَ: بَنَى كلامه هنا على ما هو المشهورُ بين القوم ، وإِنْ كانَ التَّحقيقُ عِندَهُ مَا قالَهُ في شرحِ الأصل، وأمَّا مثلُ قولِنَا : اللهُ مَوجودٌ دائِماً أو بِالضَّرورة; فليسَ مِن قبيل مَا جُعِل الزَّمانُ فِيهِ مَوضوعاً; لأنَّ الموجودَ في الزَّمانِ مَقول بالاشتراكِ على معنيَيْنِ; أحدُهُما : أنْ يكونَ الزَّمانُ ظرفاً لهُ ومُنطبقاً عليهِ كَالماءِ في الكوز، وهذا عام في الممكنات ثانيهما : أنْ يكونَ مَنسوباً إليه أي : يكونَ مُصاحباً لهُ وَمَوجوداً معهُ كَالواجب; تَأَمَّلْ، قال العصامُ : وَلَكَ أن تقول : لا يصح أن تكون المطلقة المنتشرة أيضاً نقيضاً للدائمة; لأنَّ رفع دوامِ السَّلْبِ لَا يَقتضي الإيجاب في بعض أوقاتِ الذَّاتِ; لجواز أنْ يكونَ رفعُ الدَّوامِ الإطلاق العام الذي هو أعمُّ مِنَ الإطلاقِ فنقولُ : نقيضُ دوامِ السَّلْبِ رفعُهُ، ويلزمُهُ التُّبوتُ في الجملة أعمُّ مِن أنْ يكونَ بالثُبوتِ في جميعِ الأوقاتِ أو في البعض فقط، أو لا في وقتٍ ا.هـ. ووقع في الحاشية هنا سهو ; وَهُو قَولُهُ: المراد بالمطلقة العامَّةِ مَا عبّر عنها فيما تقدَّمَ بالمنتشرة .. هـ. لأنَّ المنتشرةَ مِن أقسامِ الضَّروريَّاتِ كَمَا تقدَّمَ لَا المطلقات، وأما المطلقه المنتشرةُ ; فَلَمْ تُذكَرْ سابقاً، وفرَّقَ بين المنتشرة المطلقة والمطلقة المنتشرة، وبعض الحواشي هنا قال كلاماً زعم أنَّهُ تَحقيق وَهُوَ بحذفِ حائِهِ حَقيق . (قَوْلُهُ : وَالنَّقِيضُ لِلْمَشْرُوْطَةِ العَامَّةِ هُوَ الْحِيْنِيَّةُ الْمُمْكِنَةُ) قَالَ فِي شرح المطالِعِ : هذا إنَّما يصح لو كان المشروطةُ هِيَ الضَّرورة ما دامَ الوصفُ، وأَمَّا لَو كَانَتْ بشرط الوصف ; فَلَا ; لاجتماعِهما على الكذب في مادَّةٍ ضرورة لا يكونُ لِوَصفِ الموضوع دخل فيها، فلا يصدقُ كُلُّ كاتب حيوان بالضرورة بشرط كونه كاتباً، وَلَا : ليس بعضُ الكاتب بحيوان بالإمكان حين هو كاتب ا.هـ.
Chapter: On Contradiction. And the contradictory of the conditioned universal (mashrūṭa ‘āmma) is the possible temporal (ḥīniyya mumkina). Al-Dasūqī: (His statement: and the contradictory of the conditioned universal... etc.) The conditioned universal is that in which the necessity of the relation is judged as long as the description of the subject exists. And the possible temporal was defined by the commentator as: that in which it is judged... etc. Al-‘Aṭṭār: The temporally temporal? The author in the commentary on the original, and then between his two statements there is contradiction; unless one says: he built his statement here on what is well-known among the people, even though the verification according to him is what he said in the commentary on the original. And as for our statement: 'God exists always or necessarily', it is not of the type where time is made the subject, because 'existent in time' is said by homonymy in two senses: one is that time is a vessel for it and applies to it, like water in a jug, and this is general in contingent things; the second is that it is attributed to it, i.e., it accompanies it and exists with it, like the necessary. Consider. Al-‘Iṣām said: And you may say: it is not valid that the expanded absolute (muṭlaqa munṭashira) is also a contradictory of the perpetual (dā’ima), because lifting the perpetuity of negation does not necessitate affirmation in some times of the essence, because lifting perpetuity may be the absolute generalization which is more general than the [temporal] restriction. So we say: the contradictory of the perpetuity of negation is its lifting, and it necessitates affirmation in general, more general than affirmation in all times or only some, or in no time. End. And there is an oversight in the commentary here: his statement that 'what is meant by the general absolute is what was previously expressed as the expanded (munṭashira)' — because the expanded is among the divisions of necessary propositions as previously, not the absolutes. As for the expanded absolute (muṭlaqa munṭashira), it was not mentioned before, and he distinguishes between the expanded of the absolute and the absolute expanded. Some glossators here said something claiming it is verification, and it deserves to be deleted. (His statement: and the contradictory of the conditioned universal is the possible temporal) He said in Sharḥ al-Maṭāli‘: this is only valid if the conditioned is 'necessity as long as the description', but if it is 'conditioned by the description', then no, because they both would be false in a matter where necessity does not involve the subject's description, e.g., 'Every writer is necessarily an animal' conditioned on being a writer is false, and 'Some writer is not an animal possibly when he is a writer' is also false. End.
Source pages p. 659

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and critical discussion of the contradictory (naqīḍ) of the conditioned universal (mashrūṭa ‘āmma). The main text of this chapter (faṣl) declares that the contradictory is the possible temporal (ḥīniyya mumkina). Al-Dasūqī expands: the conditioned universal is defined as a proposition that affirms necessity of the relation as long as the subject's description exists. The possible temporal is that which judges possibility while the description holds. Al-‘Aṭṭār notes an apparent contradiction between the author's statement here and his longer commentary; the resolution is that he follows the common view in this summary, while his own verification is elsewhere. The passage then addresses an example: 'God exists necessarily' — this is not a case where time is subject, because 'existent in time' is said in two senses: either time as container (for contingents) or as accompaniment (for necessary). Al-‘Iṣām adds that the expanded absolute cannot be the contradictory of the perpetual negative because lifting perpetuity only gives affirmation in general, not necessarily in some time. The glossators warn against confusing 'expanded of the absolute' and 'absolute expanded'. Finally, a quote from Sharḥ al-Maṭāli‘ clarifies that the contradictory rule holds only if the conditioned is 'necessity as long as description', not 'conditional on description'. In the latter case, both the conditioned and its supposed contradictory can be false, as shown with the example 'Every writer is necessarily an animal while being a writer' (false, because a writer is not necessarily an animal per se) and 'Some writer is not an animal possibly when he is a writer' (also false). Thus, the proper contradictory is established.

Key Terms
المشروطة العامة
الحينية الممكنة
النقيض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the contradictory of the conditioned universal proposition: 'Every writer is necessarily an animal as long as he is a writer'?
A) Some writer is not an animal possibly when he is a writer.
B) No writer is an animal at any time.
C) Some writer is not an animal necessarily.
D) Every writer is possibly not an animal.

Key Takeaway
The contradictory of a conditioned universal must negate the necessity while preserving the temporal condition, resulting in the possible temporal proposition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Write the contradictory in the possible temporal form and explain why each of the following is NOT the contradictory: (a) 'No moving thing is temporally originated at any time.', (b) 'Some moving thing is not temporally originated necessarily.'

The Contradictory of Temporal Absolute is Temporal Possible
Author defines contradiction between necessity and possibility modals and applies to temporal propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the contradictory of any temporal absolute proposition and check that it is a temporal possible proposition.

Core Idea
Necessity and possibility are contradictories: the opposite of 'must' is 'may not'.

In modal logic, a proposition with a necessity modality is contradicted by the corresponding possibility modality that negates the predicate. The passage applies this to temporal absolute propositions, which assert necessity at a specific time. Understanding this immediate inference is essential for evaluating arguments in Islamic theology and jurisprudence that rely on time-bound necessary truths.

Guided Path
1
Identify the modality of the proposition
Determine whether the proposition expresses necessity (ḍarūra) or possibility (imkān), and whether it is absolute (dhātiyya), descriptive (waṣfiyya), or temporal (waqtiyya). The passage focuses on temporal absolute (al-waqtiyya al-muṭlaqa) which asserts necessity at a specific time.
2
Recall the principle of modal contradiction
Necessity and possibility are contradictories. For any predicate P and subject S, 'S is necessarily P' is contradicted by 'S is possibly not P'. This holds for essential, descriptive, and temporal modalities.
3
Apply to affirmative temporal absolute
Take an affirmative temporal absolute like 'Every writer moves his fingers at the time of writing necessarily.' Its contradictory must deny the necessity at that specific time. So it becomes 'Some writer does not move his fingers at the time of writing possibly' (i.e., it is possible that some writer does not move fingers at writing time).
The contradictory is not a necessity proposition, but a possibility proposition.
4
Apply to negative temporal absolute
Take a negative temporal absolute like 'No writer is still in his fingers at the time of writing necessarily.' Its contradictory must deny the necessity of that negation at that time. So it becomes 'Some writer is still in his fingers at the time of writing possibly' (i.e., it is possible that some writer is still at writing time).
The contradictory is again a temporal possible, not a temporal absolute.
5
General rule: the contradictory of any temporal absolute is a temporal possible
Because necessity (waqtiyya muṭlaqa) and possibility (mumkina waqtiyya) are contradictories when time is fixed. No other modal type serves as the contradictory.
This is an immediate inference known as 'contradiction' (tanāquḍ).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ: فِي التَّنَاقُضِ. فكما أنَّ الضَّرورةَ الذَّاتِيَّة تنافي الإمكانَ الذَّاتي، كذلكَ الضَّرورةَ الوصفية تنافي الإمكان الوصفي. وَمِنْ هَهُنا يُعلَمُ أنَّ نقيض الوقتيَّةِ المطلقة هو الممكنة الوقتيَّة؛ لأنَّ الضَّرورة بحسـب الوقت المعيَّن، تناقض سلبها بحسب ذلك الوقت. ومثال ذلك في الموجبة: كلُّ كاتب متحرّك الأصابع وقت الكتابة بالضرورة، فَنقيضُها: بعضُ الكاتب ليس بمتحرِّكِ الأصابع وقت الكتابة بالإمكان العام، وفي السَّالبةِ: لا شيء مِنَ الكاتب بساكن الأصابع وقت الكتابة بالضرورة، فنقيضُها: بعضُ الكاتب ساكن الأصابع وقت الكتابة بالإمكان الوقتي. وحاصل الاعتذار عنه: أنَّهُ إِنَّما لم يتعرَّضُ لِذلِك؛ للعلمِ بهِ مِمَّا ذكرَهُ.
Section: On Contradiction. Just as essential necessity contradicts essential possibility, so descriptive necessity contradicts descriptive possibility. And from here it is known that the contradictory of the temporal absolute is the temporal possible; because necessity with respect to a specific time contradicts its negation with respect to that time. An example of this in the affirmative: Every writer moves his fingers at the time of writing necessarily, so its contradictory is: Some writer does not move his fingers at the time of writing with general possibility. And in the negative: No writer is still in his fingers at the time of writing necessarily, so its contradictory is: Some writer is still in his fingers at the time of writing with temporal possibility. And the gist of the apology [for the author] is: He only did not address that [earlier] because it is known from what he mentioned.
Source pages p. 661

Commentary
This passage defines the contradiction (tanāquḍ) between modal propositions involving necessity and possibility. It opens with an analogy: essential necessity (e.g., 'All humans are animals by essence') contradicts essential possibility ('Some human may not be an animal'), and similarly descriptive necessity (e.g., 'Every writer moves his fingers as long as he is a writer') contradicts descriptive possibility ('Some writer may not move his fingers at some time while being a writer'). The author then applies this to temporal absolute propositions, which assert necessity at a specific time. The contradictory of a temporal absolute is a temporal possible: a proposition that denies the necessity at that same time. For example, the affirmative temporal absolute 'Every writer moves his fingers at the time of writing necessarily' is contradicted by the temporal possible 'Some writer does not move his fingers at the time of writing with general possibility.' In the negative, 'No writer is still in his fingers at the time of writing necessarily' is contradicted by 'Some writer is still in his fingers at the time of writing with temporal possibility.' Finally, the commentator excuses the original author for not listing the contradictories of temporal absolute and pervasive absolute among the simple modals earlier, explaining that these contradictories can be derived by analogy from the principle stated, so there was no need to mention them separately.

الإمكان الذَّاتي
الضَّرورة الوصفية
الإمكان الوصفي
الوقتيَّة المطلقة
الممكنة الوقتيَّة
نقيض

B) Some prayer is not performed facing the qibla at the time of prayer possibly.
C) No prayer is performed facing the qibla at the time of prayer necessarily.
D) Some prayer is performed facing the qibla at the time of prayer possibly.

Key Takeaway
The contradictory of any temporal absolute proposition is a temporal possible proposition, because necessity and possibility are direct contradictories when time is fixed.

Negation of the Muntashira Mutlaqa: The Perpetual Possible
Defines the negation of the muntashira mutlaqa as the mumkina da'ima.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the negation (naqid) of a muntashira mutlaqa proposition and construct its correct mumkina da'ima counterpart for both affirmative and negative forms.

Core Idea
The negation of 'necessarily at some time' is 'possibly not at all times'.

In this lesson, we examine how the negation of a temporally-restricted necessity proposition works. The muntashira mutlaqa asserts necessity at some unspecified time; its contradictory must deny necessity at all times, yielding the mumkina da'ima. This is a key immediate inference in Islamic modal logic, used in kalam arguments about temporal origination.

Guided Path
1
Understand the muntashira mutlaqa
Identify this proposition as one that judges necessity of a predicate for the subject at some (unspecified) time. For example, 'Every human is necessarily breathing at some time.'
2
Recognize the temporal scope
Notice that the necessity is tied to a specific time, but that time is not fixed. The negation must cover all times to contradict the original.
3
Formulate the negation as mumkina da'ima
The contradictory asserts that the opposite relation is not necessary at any time. This yields a proposition of perpetual possibility: 'Some human is not breathing with general possibility always.'
The mumkina da'ima can be general or perpetual possibility.
4
Apply to both affirmative and negative forms
For an affirmative muntashira (e.g., 'Every human is necessarily breathing at some time'), the negation is a particular negative mumkina da'ima. For a negative muntashira (e.g., 'No human is necessarily breathing at some time'), the negation is a particular affirmative mumkina da'ima.
The temporal and modal quantifiers invert according to the rules of contradiction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكذا نقيضُ المنتشرة المطلقة، هو الممكنة الدائمةُ ؛ لأنَّ الضَّرورة في وقت ما ؛ تنافي سلبها في جميع الأوقات.
(قَوْلُهُ: وَكَذَا نَقِيضُ الْمُنْتَشِرَةِ ... إلخ) المنتشرة المطلقة : هي التي حكم فيها بضرورةِ النَّسبة في وقتٍ مَا والممكنةُ الدَّائمةُ : ما حكم فيها بسلب الضرورة عن الجانب المخالفِ في جميعِ الأوقات . ومثال ذلك في الموجبة : كلُّ إنسانٍ مُتنفّسُ بالضَّرورة وقتاً ما، فنقيضُهَا : بعضُ الإنسان ليسَ بِمتنفّس بالإمكان العام دائماً، وفي السَّالبةِ : لَا شيءَ مِنَ الإِنسانِ يمتنفّس وقتاً ما بالضَّرورةِ، فَنقيضُها : بعضُ الإنسانِ متنفّس بالإمكان العام دائماً ، وتوجيه التناقضِ في ذلكَ مَا ذكرَهُ الشَّارح .
(قَوْلُهُ: هُوَ الْمُمْكِنَةُ الدَّائِمَةُ) الَّتي حُكِمَ فيها بسلبِ الضَّرورة دائماً عن الجانب المخالفِ، فَنقيضُ قولِنَا بالضَّرورةِ: كُلُّ إنسانٍ مُتنفّس في وقتٍ مَا ؛ قولنا : بعضُ الإنسانِ ليسَ بِمتنفّس دائماً بالإمكان أو بالإمكان الدائمي، ونسبتها إلى المنتشرة المطلقة كنسبة الممكنة الوقتيّة إلى الوقتيّة المطلقة .
(۱) (قَوْلُهُ : ما ذكره (الشَّارح أي بقوله : لأنَّ الضَّرورة بحسب الوقت المعين تناقض سلبها إلخ، أي: سلب ضرورة الإيجاب في السالبة وسلب ضرورة السلب في الموجبة، ويوضحه مثالاً المحشي، وكذا يقال : نقيض المنتشرة . ا . هـ. الشَّرنوبي .
And likewise the negation of the muntashira mutlaqa is the mumkina da'ima; because necessity at a certain time contradicts its negation at all times. (His statement: 'And likewise the negation of the muntashira... etc.') The muntashira mutlaqa is that in which the necessity of the relation is judged at some time, and the mumkina da'ima is that in which the negation of necessity is judged from the opposite side at all times. An example of that in the affirmative: 'Every human is necessarily breathing at some time', so its negation is: 'Some human is not breathing with general possibility always'; and in the negative: 'No human is breathing at some time with necessity', so its negation is: 'Some human is breathing with general possibility always'. And the direction of the contradiction in that is what the commentator mentioned. (His statement: 'It is the mumkina da'ima' which is judged with the negation of necessity always from the opposite side, so the negation of our statement with necessity: 'Every human is breathing at some time' is our statement: 'Some human is not breathing always with possibility or with perpetual possibility', and its relation to the muntashira mutlaqa is like the relation of the possibility-temporal to the temporal-mutlaqa. (1) (His statement: 'What the commentator mentioned' meaning his statement: 'Because necessity according to a specific time contradicts its negation etc.', i.e., the negation of the necessity of affirmation in the negative and the negation of the necessity of negation in the affirmative, and it is clarified by an example of the annotator, and likewise it is said: the negation of the muntashira. End. Al-Sharnubi.
Source pages p. 663

Commentary
This passage defines the negation (naqid) of a specific type of quantified temporal proposition, the muntashira mutlaqa (the absolute scattered). The muntashira mutlaqa is a proposition that judges necessity of a relation at some time (e.g., 'Every human is necessarily breathing at some time'). Its contradictory is the mumkina da'ima (the perpetual possible), which judges that the opposite relation is not necessary at any time—i.e., it is possibly not the case at all times. The commentator al-Dasuqi elaborates: the muntashira mutlaqa has a built-in temporal restriction (necessity at some time), so its negation must be universal in time (possibility of non-occurrence at all times). He gives examples for both affirmative and negative forms. Then al-'Attar adds that the mumkina da'ima can be either general possibility (imkan 'amm) or perpetual possibility (imkan da'imi), and that the relation between the muntashira mutlaqa and its negation is analogous to the relation between the possibility-temporal (mumkina waqtiyya) and the temporal-mutlaqa (waqtiyya mutlaqa). The marginal note by al-Sharnubi clarifies that the contradiction is based on the specific time: the necessity at that time negates the absolute negation of that necessity. This is a classic immediate inference exercise in modal logic, where the student must understand how temporal quantifiers interact with necessity and possibility.

Key Terms
المنتشرة المطلقة
الممكنة الدائمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Some human is not possibly breathing at some time.
B) Some human is not breathing with general possibility always.
C) No human is necessarily breathing at some time.
D) Some human is necessarily not breathing at all times.

Key Takeaway
The muntashira mutlaqa's negation is always the mumkina da'ima, because necessity at a time contradicts the absence of that necessity at all times.

Negation of the 'Urfiyya 'Amma and Summary of Simple Propositions
Defines the negation of the 'urfiyya 'amma as the hiniyya mutlaqa and lists all simple propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the negation of an 'urfiyya 'amma proposition as a hiniyya mutlaqa and enumerate the twelve simple propositions.

Core Idea
The contradictory of 'always as long as' is 'sometimes not' in actuality.

In the previous lesson, you learned about the negations of the perpetual and absolute general propositions. Now we examine the negation of the 'urfiyya 'amma, which judges the permanence of a relation as long as a description of the subject holds. Its contradictory is the 'hiniyya mutlaqa', which judges the actuality of the relation at some time during that description. Understanding this relation is essential for mastering the system of simple propositions in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the 'urfiyya 'amma
The 'urfiyya 'amma judges that the predicate holds always as long as the subject's description exists. For example: 'Every writer moves his fingers as long as he is a writer.' Note the condition 'as long as' (ma dama).
This is a type of conditional proposition.
2
Understand its negation
The negation of the 'urfiyya 'amma is the hiniyya mutlaqa, which judges the actuality of the relation at some time during the description. It affirms or denies the predicate at some instant when the description holds.
The contradictory is not a simple 'sometimes not' in possibility, but actual at some time.
3
Apply to examples
From the text: The affirmative 'Every writer is moving his fingers always as long as he is a writer' is negated by 'Some writer is not moving his fingers with absolute generality when he is a writer.' The negative 'No writer is still of the fingers always as long as he is a writer' is negated by 'Some writer is still of the fingers with absolute generality when he is a writer.' Work through these to see the pattern.
The negation of a universal affirmative is a particular negative, and of a universal negative is a particular affirmative.
4
Grasp the analogy
The relation between the perpetual (da'ima) and absolute general (mutlaqa 'amma) is analogous to that between the 'urfiyya 'amma and the absolute temporal (hiniyya mutlaqa). Just as perpetual affirmation in all times contradicts negation in some times, so perpetual affirmation in all times of description contradicts negation in some times of description.
The text illustrates this with the pleurisy example: 'Everyone with pleurisy coughs as long as he has pleurisy' is negated by 'Not everyone with pleurisy coughs at some time while pleuritic.'
5
Count the simple propositions
The passage states that the contradictions of the four later types (including the temporal ones) when added to the earlier simple propositions from the matn yield twelve simple propositions. The total of simple and compound propositions together is nineteen.
Memorize the total numbers: 12 simple, 7 compound (implied), total 19.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(وَ) النَّقيضُ لِلْعُرْفِيَّةِ العَامَّةِ) هو (الجِينِيَّةُ المُطْلَقَةُ) الَّتي حُكِمَ فيها بفعليَّة النّسبةِ في بعض أوقاتِ وصفِ الموضوع.
(قَوْلُهُ : وَالنَّقيضُ لِلْعُرْفِيَّةِ الْعَامَّةِ ... إلخ) العرفيَّةُ العامَّةُ : هي التي حكم فيها بدوام النّسبةِ ما دامَ وصفُ الموضوع موجوداً، والمطلقة الحينيَّةُ عَرَّفَهَا الشَّارِحُ.
ومثال ذلك في الموجبة : كلُّ كاتب متحرك الأصابع دائماً ما دام كاتباً، فنقيضُها : بعضُ الكاتب ليس متحرك الأصابع بالإطلاق العام حين هو كاتب، وفي السالبة : لا شيء من الكاتب بِساكن الأصابع دائماً ما دام كاتباً، فنقيضُها : بعض الكاتب ساكن الأصابع بالإطلاقِ العام حين هو كاتب؛ أي: في وقتٍ من أوقاتِ وصف الموضوع، وتوجيه تناقض ذلكَ مَا ذكرَ الشَّارح .

(قَوْلُهُ : هَذِهِ) أي : ما تقدَّمَ مِن قولِهِ : والنَّقيضُ لِلضَّروريَّةِ ... إلى هنا، تناقضُ البسائط، وتلخص من هذا : أنَّ تناقض الأربعةِ الأخيرة لم تتقدَّم، فإذا أضفتها إلى ما تقدَّمَ في المصنفِ من البسائط ؛ كانت اثني عشر قضيَّةً بسيطةً، وحينئذ؛ فتكونُ جملةُ القضايا البسيطة والمركّبة تسعة عشر قضيَّة .
(قَوْلُهُ : كَنِسْبَةِ الْمُطْلَقَةِ الْعَامَّةِ إِلَى الدَّائِمَةِ يعني : كما أنَّ النِّسبة بينهما بالعموم والخصوص المطلق؛ كذلكَ النِّسبةُ بين الحينيَّةِ المطلقة والعرفية العامة بهما . (قَوْلُهُ : فَكَمَا أَنَّ الدَّوَامَ الذَّاتِي ... إِلَخ) يعني : أنَّ الحينيَّة المطلقة مِنَ العرفية العامة بمنزلة المطلقة العامَّةِ منَ الدَّائمةِ ; لأنَّهُ كَمَا أنَّ الإيجاب في جميع أوقاتِ الذَّاتِ يناقضُ السَّلْبَ فى بعضها ، والسَّلْبَ في جميعها يناقض الإيجاب في بعضها ; فكذا الإيجاب في أوقاتِ الوصْفِ يناقضُ السَّلْبَ في بعضها ، والسَّلْبُ في جميعِهَا يُناقضُ الإيجاب في بعضِهَا ، فَنقيضُ قَولِنَا : بالدَّوامِ كُلَّ مَن بِهِ ذات الجنب يسعلُ ما دامَ بذاتِ الجنب ; قولنا : بالإطلاقِ ليسَ كُلُّ مَن بِهِ ذاتَ الجنب يسعل في بعض أوقاتٍ كونَهُ مَجنوباً .
(قَوْلُهُ : هَذِهِ نَقَائِضُ الْبَسَائِطِ الظَّاهِرُ أنَّ الإشارة إلى كُلِّ مَا ذَكَرَ في المتنِ والشَّارحِ معاً ، وإلا ; فهو لم يذكر في المتن كُلَّ البسائط .
(And) the negation of the 'urfiyya 'amma is the hiniyya mutlaqa, which judges the actuality of the relation in some times of the subject's description. (His statement: 'And the negation of the 'urfiyya 'amma... etc.') Al-'urfiyya al-'amma: it is that in which the perpetuity of the relation is judged as long as the subject's description exists, and the absolute temporal (al-mutlaqa al-hiniyya) has been defined by the commentator. And an example of that in the affirmative: 'Every writer is moving his fingers always as long as he is a writer', so its negation is: 'Some writer is not moving his fingers with absolute generality when he is a writer'; and in the negative: 'No writer is still of the fingers always as long as he is a writer', so its negation is: 'Some writer is still of the fingers with absolute generality when he is a writer', i.e., at some time of the subject's description, and the direction of its contradiction is what the commentator mentioned. (His statement: 'And its relation') i.e., the absolute temporal, and his statement: 'like the relation of' i.e., in mutual exclusion. (His statement: 'For just as... etc.') It is a clarification of what preceded from his statement: 'And its relation... etc.' so it is an explanation of it. (His statement: 'These') i.e., what has preceded from his statement: 'And the negation of the necessary...' up to here, the contradictions of the simple propositions. And it is summarized from this: that the contradiction of the last four has not preceded, so if you add them to what preceded in the matn from the simple propositions, they become twelve simple propositions. And then, the total of simple and compound propositions becomes nineteen propositions. (His statement: 'Like the relation of the absolute general to the perpetual') means: just as the relation between them is by absolute generality and specificity, likewise the relation between the absolute temporal and the 'urfiyya 'amma is by both. (His statement: 'For just as the essential perpetuity... etc.') means: that the absolute temporal is to the 'urfiyya 'amma as the absolute general is to the perpetual; because just as affirmation in all times of the essence contradicts negation in some of them, and negation in all contradicts affirmation in some, likewise affirmation in times of the description contradicts negation in some of them, and negation in all contradicts affirmation in some. So the negation of our statement with perpetuity: 'Everyone with pleurisy coughs as long as he has pleurisy' is our statement with absoluteness: 'Not everyone with pleurisy coughs at some times of his being pleuritic.' (His statement: 'These are the contradictions of the simple propositions') Apparently the reference is to everything mentioned in the matn and the commentator together; otherwise, he did not mention in the matn all the simple propositions.
Source pages p. 663

Commentary
This passage first defines the negation of the 'urfiyya 'amma (the common conditional) as the hiniyya mutlaqa (the absolute temporal). The 'urfiyya 'amma judges that the predicate holds as long as the subject's description exists (e.g., 'Every writer moves his fingers as long as he is a writer'). Its contradictory is the proposition that judges the actuality of the relation at some time during the description (e.g., 'Some writer does not move his fingers at some time when he is a writer'). Al-Dasuqi gives examples in both affirmative and negative forms, mirroring the structure of the previous lesson. Al-'Attar then explains the relationship between the two types by analogy: the relation between the 'hiniyya mutlaqa' and the 'urfiyya 'amma' is like that between the 'mutlaqa 'amma' (absolute general) and the 'da'ima' (perpetual). He clarifies that just as perpetual judgment in all times of the essence contradicts partial negation, so perpetual judgment in all times of the description contradicts partial negation in some times of the description. He illustrates with a medical example: 'Everyone with pleurisy coughs as long as he has pleurisy'—its negation is 'Not everyone with pleurisy coughs at some time during his pleurisy.' The passage concludes with a summary: the contradictions of the four later types (which include these temporal ones) when added to the earlier simple propositions from the matn yield a total of twelve simple propositions. The grand total of simple and compound propositions together is nineteen. This enumeration is crucial for understanding the complete system of propositions in Islamic logic.

Key Terms
العرفية العامة
الحينية المطلقة
نقائض البسائط

B) Some writer is not moving his fingers with absolute generality when he is a writer.
C) No writer is moving his fingers always as long as he is a writer.

Key Takeaway
The negation of the 'urfiyya 'amma is the hiniyya mutlaqa, which judges the actuality of the relation at some time during the subject's description.

Constructing Contradictories of Simple Modal Propositions
Author gives examples and summarizes contradictions of four simple modal propositions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct the contradictory of any universal affirmative or negative simple modal proposition by correctly switching the quantifier and the time/condition modality.

Core Idea
The contradictory of a universal modal proposition is a particular proposition with the opposite time modality.

In this passage, the author explains how to find the contradictory of simple modal propositions, using the analogy between the absolute general/eternal pair and the absolute conditional/general conventional pair. Understanding this is essential for constructing valid immediate inferences in modal logic, which has direct application in Islamic theological and legal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify modality and quantifier
Determine if the proposition is universal affirmative (a) or universal negative (e), and identify its modality: eternal by essence (dā'ima), eternal by description (ʿurfiyya ʿāmma), absolute general (muṭlaqa ʿāmma), or absolute conditional (ḥīniyya muṭlaqa).
The modality is often expressed with phrases like 'always', 'at all times', 'as long as', or 'at some time'.
2
Flip the quantifier
The contradictory of a universal proposition is always a particular proposition: a becomes i (particular affirmative), e becomes o (particular negative).
3
Swap the modality to its opposite
If the original is eternal (either by essence or description), the contradictory must use the corresponding absolute modality. If the original is absolute, the contradictory uses the corresponding eternal modality. Specifically, dā'ima ↔ muṭlaqa ʿāmma, and ʿurfiyya ʿāmma ↔ ḥīniyya muṭlaqa.
This ensures that the contradictory denies the original's claim about all times or the condition.
4
Formulate the contradictory proposition
Combine the new quantifier and new modality into a proposition that has the same subject and predicate as the original. For example, 'Every S is P eternally' becomes 'Some S is not P with absolute generality' (i.e., at some time).
Be precise with the wording to reflect the time/condition quantifier.
5
Verify the logical relationship
Ensure that the original and its contradictory cannot both be true and cannot both be false. This is the defining test of contradiction.
Use a concrete example to test: if the original is true, the contradictory must be false, and vice versa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ونسبتها إلى العرفية العامة كنسبة المطلقة العامَّةِ إلى الدائمة، فكما أنَّ الدَّوامَ الذَّاتي ينافي الإطلاق الذاتي، كذلك الدَّوامُ الوصفي، يناقضُ الإطلاق الوصفي، هذه نقائض البسائط . الدسوقي ومثال ذلكَ في الموجبة : كلُّ كاتب متحرك الأصابع دائماً ما دام كاتباً، فنقيضُها : بعضُ الكاتب ليس متحرك الأصابع بالإطلاق العام حين هو كاتب، وفي السالبة : لا شيء من الكاتب بِساكن الأصابع دائماً ما دام كاتباً، فنقيضُها : بعض الكاتب ساكن الأصابع بالإطلاقِ العام حين هو كاتب؛ أي: في وقتٍ من أوقاتِ وصف الموضوع، وتوجيه تناقض ذلكَ مَا ذكرَ الشَّارح . (قَوْلُهُ : فَكَمَا أَنَّ الدَّوَامَ الذَّاتِي ... إِلَخ) يعني : أنَّ الحينيَّة المطلقة مِنَ العرفية العامة بمنزلة المطلقة العامَّةِ منَ الدَّائمةِ؛ لأنَّهُ كَمَا أنَّ الإيجاب في جميع أوقاتِ الذَّاتِ يناقضُ السَّلْبَ فى بعضها ، والسَّلْبَ في جميعها يناقض الإيجاب في بعضها ; فكذا الإيجاب في أوقاتِ الوصْفِ يناقضُ السَّلْبَ في بعضها ، والسَّلْبُ في جميعِهَا يُناقضُ الإيجاب في بعضِهَا ، فَنقيضُ قَولِنَا : بالدَّوامِ كُلَّ مَن بِهِ ذات الجنب يسعلُ ما دامَ بذاتِ الجنب ; جميعِ قولنا : بالإطلاقِ ليسَ كُلُّ مَن بِهِ ذاتَ الجنب يسعل في بعض أوقاتٍ كونَهُ مَجنوباً . (قَوْلُهُ : هَذِهِ نَقَائُضُ الْبَسَائِطِ الظَّاهِرُ أنَّ الإشارة إلى كُلِّ مَا ذَكَرَ في المتنِ والشَّارحِ معاً ، وإلا ; فهو لم يذكر في المتن كُلَّ البسائط .
And its relationship to the general conventional is like the relationship of the absolute general to the eternal. For just as eternal by essence contradicts absolute by essence, so too eternal by description contradicts absolute by description. These are the contradictions of the simple propositions. Al-Dasūqī: And an example of that in the affirmative: 'Every writer is moving his fingers eternally as long as he is a writer' – its contradictory is 'Some writer is not moving his fingers with absolute generality when he is a writer'. And in the negative: 'No writer is still-fingered eternally as long as he is a writer' – its contradictory is 'Some writer is still-fingered with absolute generality when he is a writer', i.e., at some time of the subject's description. And the explanation of that contradiction is what the commentator mentioned. (His statement: 'and its relationship') – he means the absolute conditionality. And his statement: 'like the relationship' – i.e., in mutual exclusion. (His statement: 'so just as, etc.') – it is an expansion on what preceded from his statement 'and its relationship etc.', so it is an explanation of it. (His statement: 'these') – i.e., what came before from his statement 'and the contradictory of the necessary... up to here, they are the contradictions of the simple. And it is summarized from this: that the contradictions of the last four have not preceded. So if you add them to what preceded in the text of the simple, they become twelve simple propositions. Then the total of simple and compound propositions becomes nineteen propositions. Al-ʿAṭṭār: (His statement: 'like the relationship of the absolute general to the eternal') – he means: just as the relationship between them is by absolute generality and particularity, so too the relationship between the absolute conditional and the general conventional is by them. (His statement: 'so just as the eternal by essence, etc.') – he means: that the absolute conditional is from the general conventional in the position of the absolute general from the eternal; because just as affirmation in all times of the essence contradicts negation in some of them, and negation in all of them contradicts affirmation in some of them; so too affirmation in times of the description contradicts negation in some of them, and negation in all of them contradicts affirmation in some of them. So the contradictory of our statement with the eternal: 'Everyone with pleurisy coughs as long as he has pleurisy' is our statement with absolute generality: 'Not everyone with pleurisy coughs at some times of his being pleuritic'. (His statement: 'these are the contradictions of the simple') – it is apparent that the reference is to all that was mentioned in the text and the commentator together; otherwise he did not mention in the text all the simple [propositions].
Source pages p. 664

Commentary
This passage explains the contradictions of simple modal propositions and provides examples. The author first establishes the relationship between the 'absolute conditional' (al-ḥīniyya al-muṭlaqa) and the 'general conventional' (al-ʿurfiyya al-ʿāmma) as analogous to the relationship between the 'absolute general' (al-muṭlaqa al-ʿāmma) and the 'eternal' (al-dāʾima) in terms of mutual exclusion. Then al-Dasūqī gives concrete examples: for the affirmative eternal proposition 'Every writer is eternally moving his fingers as long as he is a writer', its contradictory is 'Some writer is not moving his fingers with absolute generality at the time of being a writer'. Note that the contradictory is not simply 'Some writer is not moving his fingers' but must include the time condition. Similarly, the contradictory of the negative eternal 'No writer is eternally still-fingered as long as he is a writer' is 'Some writer is still-fingered with absolute generality when he is a writer'. The commentator explains that the contradictory uses absolute generality (i.e., at some time) rather than eternal duration. This is because the contradictory must be true at some time, not all times. The explanation that follows clarifies that 'these' contradictions refer to the set of simple modal contradictions that were previously partially enumerated. The text then summarises that the contradictions of the last four types (which are the four new ones) have not been previously covered, and adding them to the earlier simple propositions yields twelve simple propositions total, and when combined with compound propositions the total number of all propositions (simple and compound) becomes nineteen. Al-ʿAṭṭār then expands on the initial analogy: the absolute conditional relates to the general conventional as the absolute general relates to the eternal, in that the latter is more specific. He illustrates with a medical example: the contradictory of 'Everyone with pleurisy coughs as long as he has pleurisy' (eternal by description) is 'Not everyone with pleurisy coughs at some times of his being pleuritic' (absolute general). The passage concludes with a note that the phrase 'these are the contradictions of the simple' refers to all the contradictions mentioned in both the text and the commentary, as the text itself did not list all simple contradictions.

Contradiction of Compound Modal Propositions
Defines contradiction of compound modals and lists modal oppositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct the contradictory of any compound modal proposition as a disjunction between the contradictories of its parts.

Core Idea
The contradictory of a compound modal is a disjunction, not a simple proposition.

This passage opens a chapter on how to contradict compound modal propositions. Unlike simple modals, compound modals require a different treatment because they have two parts. The key is that the contradictory is a disjunctive concept (mafhūm muraddad) between the two contradictories of the parts. The passage also shows a detailed mapping of modal opposites by al-Dasūqī.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two parts of the compound
Locate the two simple propositions that make up the compound modal. For example, in 'Every A is either necessarily B or possibly C', the parts are 'Every A is necessarily B' and 'Every A is possibly C'.
The compound may be conditional or disjunctive.
2
Find the contradictory of each part
Using the table of modal oppositions from al-Dasūqī (e.g., necessity by essence is contradicted by possibility general by essence), determine the contradictory of each part.
The passage provides the correspondences for necessity and eternity.
3
Form a disjunction between the two contradictories
Combine the two simple contradictories with a disjunctive particle (e.g., 'or') to form the overall contradictory of the compound.
This disjunction is the contradictory, not a single proposition.
4
Verify the contradiction
Check that the disjunction and the original compound cannot both be true and cannot both be false, establishing a true logical contradiction.
This follows from the definition of contradictory as mutually exclusive and exhaustive.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ: فِي النَّنَاقُضِ [ كيفيَّةُ التَّناقض في الموجهات المركبة] ٦٦٥ وأمَّا النَّقيضُ لِلْمُرَكَّب ) فهو (المنْهُوْمُ الْمُرَدَّدُ بَيْنَ نَقِيضَيِ الجُزْأَيْنِ)، الدسوقي وتحصل من هذا : أنَّ الضَّرورة بحسبِ الذَّاتِ يُقابلها الإمكانُ العالم بحسب الذات، وأنَّ الضَّرورة بحسب الوصف ؛ يُقابلها الإمكان المقيد بحين الوصف، وأنّ الضَّرورةً بحسب الوقتِ المعيَّن; يُقابلها الإمكان بحسب ذلك الوقت، وأن الضرورة بحسب وقت ما ; يقابلها الإمكانُ المقيَّدُ بحسب الزَّمان، وأنَّ الدَّوامَ بحسب الذَّاتِ; يُقابله الإطلاقُ بحسب الذَّاتِ، وأنَّ الدَّوامَ بحسبِ الوصفِ; يُقابلهُ الإطلاق المفيد بحين ذلك الوصف . (قَوْلُهُ : وَأَمَّا النَّقِيْضُ ... إلخ) أي داع إلى تقدير أمَّا في كلام المصنف المحوِجِ لإضمار الفاء معَ المبتدأ ، مع عدم ملائمته للسّياق؟ والأظهرُ والأخصرُ أنْ يقول : والنَّقيضُ لِلمركَّب المفهوم . . . إلخ . (قَوْلُهُ : بَيْنَ) ظَرْفٌ لِلمردَّدِ ; أي : شيءٌ رُدّدَ بين أمرين، وهما نقيضا مُزأَيْ المركَّبة . العطار (قَوْلُهُ: وَأَمَّا النَّقِيْضُ لِلْمُرَكَّبِ) أيُّ داعٍ إلى تقدير (أما) في كلام المصنفِ المحوج لإضمار الفاء مع المبتدأ مع عدم ملائمتِهِ لِلسّياق؟ كَذَا في الحاشية، وأجابَ البعضُ بَأنَّ الشَّارِحَ لم يرد بِمَا قَرَّرَ أَنَّ ذلكَ مُقدَّرٌ في كلام المصنفِ، وإِنَّما هو تصرُّفٌ منهُ دعاه إليهِ عْرضُ الرَّبْطِ بينَ قَولِهِ : هذه نقائص البسائط)، وبين كلام المصنفِ . هـ وأقولُ : لَمَّا كانَ أخْذُ نقيض المركَّباتِ أدقَّ مِنَ البسائط ; فيحتاج إلى مزيدِ عناية; أورد أما المفيدة للتأكيد والاهتمام بالحكم الذي بعدها، واعْلَمْ أَنَّ الحملية قدْ تكون شبيهة بالمنفصلة، والمنفصلة قد تكون شبيهة بالحمليَّةِ، فَإِنَّهُ إِذا حُمِلَ على ضوع واحدٍ أمران متقابلان; فإن قدم الموضوع على حرفِ العنادِ كَقولنا : العدد إما زوج وإمَّا فَردٌ ; فالقضيَّةُ حَمليَّةٌ شَبيهة بالمنفصلة، وإنْ أُخْرَ عَنهَا كَقولِنَا : إِمَّا أَنْ يكونَ العدد زوجاً أو فرداً، فهي منفصلة شبيهة بالحملية، وهناكَ حَمليَّةٌ صرفةٌ ومُنفصلةٌ صرفةٌ، وَهُمَا ظاهرانِ ، ثمَّ الحملية والمنفصلة المتشابهان إذا كانتا كُلِّيَّتَيْنِ; لمْ
Chapter: On Contradiction [How to Contradict Compound Modal Propositions]. And as for the contradictory of the compound, it is the understood concept that is disjunctive between the two contradictories of the two parts. Al-Dasūqī: And it is obtained from this: that necessity in terms of essence is countered by possibility general in terms of essence; and necessity in terms of description is countered by possibility restricted to the time of description; and necessity in terms of a specific time is countered by possibility in terms of that time; and necessity in terms of some time is countered by possibility restricted to time; and eternity in terms of essence is countered by absolute in terms of essence; and eternity in terms of description is countered by absolute that is useful at the time of that description. (His statement: 'and as for the contradictory, etc.') – what caused the estimation of 'ammā' in the author's speech, necessitating the containing of 'fa' with the subject, while it is not suitable to the context? The most apparent and concise would be for him to say: 'and the contradictory of the compound is the understood concept... etc.' (His statement: 'between') – it is a circumstance for 'disjunctive'; i.e., a thing that is disjunctive between two things, and they are the two contradictories of the two parts of the compound. Al-ʿAṭṭār: (His statement: 'and as for the contradictory of the compound') – what caused the estimation of 'ammā' in the author's speech... etc. This is in the gloss. And some answered that the commentator did not intend that it is estimated in the author's speech; rather it is his own adaptation motivated by the need for connection between his statement 'these are the contradictions of the simple' and the author's speech. And I say: since taking the contradictory of compounds is more subtle than the simple, it requires extra attention; so he brought 'ammā' which conveys emphasis and care for the judgment after it. And know that the categorical may resemble the disjunctive, and the disjunctive may resemble the categorical. For if two opposite things are predicated on one subject, and the subject is advanced before the particle of contradiction like our statement: 'The number is either even or odd', then the proposition is categorical resembling the disjunctive. And if it is brought in a different way like our statement: 'Either the number is even or odd', then it is disjunctive resembling the categorical. And there is pure categorical and pure disjunctive, and they are apparent. Then the similar categorical and disjunctive, if they are universal; did not...
Source pages p. 665

Commentary
This passage begins a new chapter on the contradiction of compound modal propositions. The definition is given: the contradictory of a compound (murakkab) proposition is a concept (mafhūm) that is disjunctive (muraddad) between the two contradictories of its two parts. In other words, the contradictory of a compound modal proposition is not itself a simple proposition but a disjunction of two simple contradictories. For example, the contradictory of 'Every A is B necessarily' would be a disjunction between 'Some A is not B possibly' and something else, depending on the parts. Then al-Dasūqī summarises a set of correspondences: necessity by essence is countered by possibility general by essence; necessity by description is countered by possibility restricted to the time of description; etc. This gives a map of how the modalities oppose each other. The text then raises a criticism about the use of the Arabic particle 'ammā' (as for) at the beginning of the definition. The commentator (presumably al-Dasūqī) questions why the author used 'ammā' because it seems to require an implied 'fa' (thus) and a subject that is not present. He suggests it would be clearer to simply say 'And the contradictory of the compound...' without 'ammā'. Al-ʿAṭṭār reports that some responded that the commentator (the author of the original sharh) did not intend that 'ammā' was estimated in the original text; rather, it is his own addition to connect with the previous statement about contradictions of simple propositions. Al-ʿAṭṭār himself adds that since handling compounds is more subtle, the 'ammā' is used for emphasis and care. Then he discusses the distinction between categorical (ḥamliyya) and disjunctive (munfaṣila) propositions, noting that they can resemble each other. He gives an example: 'The number is either even or odd' – this is a categorical proposition because it has a single subject ('the number') and two predicates, but it functions like a disjunction. Conversely, 'Either the number is even or odd' is a full disjunctive. This distinction is important for understanding how to treat compound modals because the compound modal itself may be a disjunctive or categorical in form. The passage ends abruptly in the middle of explaining that when the resembling categorical and disjunctive are universal, they ... (cut off). This indicates that the next part would discuss how to find contradictions for such resembling propositions.
